Universal Simple, Modified, and Privative Propositions
Definition and table of universal categorical proposition types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple, modified, and privative universal categorical propositions, and state their truth conditions based on the table provided.

Core Idea
The truth of a universal proposition depends on the presence or absence of the attribute, not just on wording.

In this passage, the author introduces a taxonomy of universal categorical propositions beyond the simple affirmative and negative. He defines modified (maʿdūla) and privative (ʿadamiyya) propositions, each with its own truth conditions. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for rigorous logical analysis in Islamic sciences, where definitions and arguments rely on precise predication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five proposition types from the table
List each type: simple affirmative, simple negative, modified negative, modified affirmative, and privative affirmative. Note the Arabic labels.
The table is given in the text after 'ليكن لوحها هكذا'
2
Examine the truth conditions for each
For each proposition, determine when it is true and when false. For example, the simple affirmative is true only if all individuals have the attribute.
The text provides explicit conditions for each type.
3
Compare simple and modified propositions
Note the difference: modified propositions use a negated predicate (e.g., 'not-just'), while simple propositions use the positive predicate directly.
Simple affirmative: 'Every human is just'; modified affirmative: 'Every human is not-just'.
4
Distinguish privative propositions
Privative affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is unjust') asserts the absence of justice, not the presence of injustice. It is true only if all are unjust.
This differs from the modified affirmative, which is true under wider conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في القضايا الحملية
مختلطين، وكذا الموجبة المعدولة أعم صدقاً من الموجبة العدمية؛ لضرورة أما ما كان أعمّ صدقاً من شيء فمقابله أخص صدقاً من مقابل ذلك الشيء، والموجبة العدمية مقابلة للسالبة العدمية والموجبة المعدولة مقابلة السالبة المعدولة، فوجب أن تكون الموجبة العدمية أخص من الموجبة المعدولة، وعلى هذا فنسبة السالبة المعدولة إلى ما سامتها قطراً من الموجبة العدمية فعلى ما مضى من منع الجمع في الصدق دون الكذب، وكذا في المنع من الجمع بين الموجبة المعدولة وما سامتها قطراً من السالبة العدمية في الكذب دون الصدق. وأما المتضادة فحكمها طولاً في العموم والخصوص، وقطراً من الإيجابيات والسالبة المعدولة مع الموجبة العدمية، فعلى ما مضى في المخصوصات دون حكم التقابل عرضاً وباقي الأقطار، وليكن لوحها هكذا:
كل إنسان هو عدل: كلية موجبة بسيطة تصدق إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين وتكذب في الباقي.
لا واحد من الإنسان عدلاً: كلية سالبة بسيطة تكذب إذا كان الكل أو البعض عادلين وتصدق في الباقي.
لا واحد من الإنسان هو لا عادل: كلية سالبة معدولة تكذب إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين وتصدق في الباقي.
كل إنسان هو لا عادل: كلية موجبة معدولة تكذب إذا كانوا كلهم أو بعضهم معدومين أو عادلين وتصدق في الباقي.
كل إنسان هو جائر: كلية موجبة عدمية تصدق إذا كانوا كلهم جائرين فقط وتكذب في الباقي.
فالسالبة المعدولة أعمّ صدقاً من الموجبة البسيطة؛ لصدقها في ما تصدق فيه وزيادة والسالبة العدمية أعم صدقاً من السالبة المعدولة؛ لصدقها في ما تصدق فيه وزيادة، وكذا السالبة البسيطة أعمّ صدقاً من الموجبة المعدولة لصدقها في ما تصدق فيه وزيادة، وكذا حكم الموجبة المعدولة بالنسبة إلى الموجبة العدمية فكانت أحكامها طولاً موافقة لما مضى في المخصوصات.
وأما حكمها عرضاً فامتناع اجتماع كل طبقة مع ما سامتها في الصدق دون الكذب، إذ الصدق لا يتم إلا بصدق كل واحد منها، وفيه صدق إيجاب الشيء الواحد وسلبه معاً وهو محال، والكذب فقد لا يمتنع أن يشتركا فيه، ويكون كذب كل قضية في بعض لم تكن الأخرى فيه كاذبة كما في قولنا : (كل حيوان إنسان) (لا واحد من الحيوان إنسان).
وأما حكم الموجبات قطراً فعلى ما مضى، إذ يستحيل الجمع بينها في الصدق، وإن لم يمتنع في الكذب.
وأما حكم السوالب قطراً وحكم الموجبة المعدولة قطراً بالنسبة إلى السالبة العدمية فبخلاف ما مضى، إذ لا يمتنع الجمع بينها في الكذب واعتبره بنفسك تجده على ما مهدناه.
The first art: On categorical propositions. Mixed, and likewise the modified affirmative is broader in truth than the privative affirmative; necessarily, for whatever is broader in truth than something, its opposite is narrower in truth than the opposite of that thing. The privative affirmative is opposite to the privative negative, and the modified affirmative is opposite to the modified negative. Therefore, the privative affirmative must be narrower than the modified affirmative. According to this, the relation of the modified negative to what is diagonal to it from the privative affirmative follows what has passed of preventing conjunction in truth but not in falsehood, and likewise in preventing conjunction between the modified affirmative and what is diagonal to it from the privative negative in falsehood but not in truth. As for the contraries, their rule vertically in generality and particularity, and diagonally from affirmatives and the modified negative with the privative affirmative, follows what has passed in the specifics, without the rule of opposition horizontally and the remaining diagonals. Let its table be like this: Every human is just: universal simple affirmative, true if all are just, false otherwise. No human is just: universal simple negative, false if all or some are just, true otherwise. No human is not-just: universal modified negative, false if all are just, true otherwise. Every human is not-just: universal modified affirmative, false if all or some are nonexistent or just, true otherwise. Every human is unjust: universal privative affirmative, true if all are unjust only, false otherwise. So the modified negative is broader in truth than the simple affirmative, because it is true wherever it is true and more. The privative negative is broader in truth than the modified negative, because it is true wherever it is true and more. Likewise, the simple negative is broader in truth than the modified affirmative, because it is true wherever it is true and more. Likewise, the rule of the modified affirmative relative to the privative affirmative: so their rules vertically agree with what has passed in the specifics. As for their rule horizontally, it is impossibility of each layer together with its counterpart in truth but not falsehood, because truth is only achieved by the truth of each one of them, and in it is the affirmation and negation of one thing together, which is impossible. As for falsehood, it is not impossible that they share in it, and the falsehood of each proposition in some cases where the other is not false, as in our saying: 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human.' As for the rule of the affirmatives diagonally, it follows what has passed, for conjunction between them in truth is impossible, though not impossible in falsehood. As for the rule of negatives diagonally and the rule of the modified affirmative diagonally relative to the privative negative, it is contrary to what has passed, for conjunction between them in falsehood is not impossible. Consider it yourself, you will find it as we have laid out.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional exposition of the truth conditions for universal categorical propositions, distinguishing between simple (basīṭa), modified (maʿdūla), and privative (ʿadamiyya) forms. The author begins by asserting that the modified affirmative is broader in truth than the privative affirmative, using a principle of opposites: if A is broader than B, then the opposite of A is narrower than the opposite of B. Thus, since the privative affirmative opposes the privative negative, and the modified affirmative opposes the modified negative, the privative affirmative is narrower. The author then discusses the relationships between the diagonal pairs (e.g., modified negative vs. privative affirmative) in terms of the possibility of simultaneous truth or falsehood. The 'contraries' (mutadāddah) are examined vertically (in terms of generality and specificity) and diagonally, following patterns previously established for particular propositions. A table is presented listing five universal propositions: simple affirmative, simple negative, modified negative, modified affirmative, and privative affirmative, each with its truth conditions. After the table, the author explains the relative breadth of truth among these types: the modified negative is broader than the simple affirmative; the privative negative (not listed but implied) is broader than the modified negative; the simple negative is broader than the modified affirmative; and the modified affirmative is broader than the privative affirmative. These vertical relations agree with prior rules for particulars. Horizontally, propositions of the same 'layer' (e.g., two affirmative or two negative) cannot both be true because that would entail affirming and negating the same thing, but they can both be false, as illustrated by the example 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human.' Diagonally, affirmatives cannot be simultaneously true but can be simultaneously false; negatives and the modified affirmative vs. privative negative, however, can be simultaneously false. The author concludes by inviting the reader to verify these rules.

Key Terms
قضية حملية
موجبة بسيطة
سالبة بسيطة
موجبة معدولة
سالبة معدولة
موجبة عدمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the table, which proposition is true only when all individuals are unjust?
A) Universal simple affirmative
B) Universal modified affirmative
C) Universal privative affirmative
D) Universal modified negative

Key Takeaway
Every universal categorical proposition has a distinct truth condition depending on its type: simple, modified, or privative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write a universal modified negative proposition about a subject from Islamic theology (e.g., 'No angel is disobedient') and state its truth condition using the author's schema.
?
Identify the proposition type of the following Quranic statement: 'وما خلقنا السماوات والأرض وما بينهما لاعبين' (We did not create the heavens and earth and what is between them in play). Is it simple, modified, or privative? Explain your reasoning.

Vertical Relations: Broader and Narrower in Truth
Explanation of vertical breadth relations among proposition types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to order the five universal proposition types by breadth of truth and explain the principle of opposites used to derive the hierarchy.

Core Idea
Breadth of truth follows a strict hierarchy: privative negative > modified negative > simple affirmative; simple negative > modified affirmative > privative affirmative.

After presenting the table, the author explains the vertical (طولاً) relationships between the proposition types in terms of 'broader in truth' (أعم صدقاً). He uses a principle of opposites: if A is broader than B, then the opposite of A is narrower than the opposite of B. Let's trace these relations to understand the ordering.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle of opposites
The author asserts: 'whatever is broader in truth than something, its opposite is narrower in truth than the opposite of that thing.' Apply this to the pairs: modified affirmative and privative affirmative.
This principle is stated early in the passage.
2
Trace the vertical ordering from the text
The author gives four explicit relations: (1) modified negative > simple affirmative, (2) privative negative > modified negative, (3) simple negative > modified affirmative, (4) modified affirmative > privative affirmative. List these from broadest to narrowest.
The text says: 'فالسالبة المعدولة أعمّ صدقاً من الموجبة البسيطة' etc.
3
Combine the two chains
Observe that the hierarchical relations form two chains: one from privative negative down to simple affirmative, and another from simple negative down to privative affirmative. Note that these chains cross: the simple affirmative is narrower than the modified negative, but not directly comparable to the simple negative?
The author treats vertical relations separately from horizontal (عرضاً) and diagonal (قطراً) relations.
4
Verify with the principle of opposites
Using the principle, derive one of the relations yourself. For example, given that modified affirmative is broader than privative affirmative, their opposites: modified negative should be narrower than privative negative. But the text says privative negative is broader; check consistency.
The author's derived relations are internally consistent; confirm with an example.

Source Text

الفن الأول: في القضايا الحملية
مختلطين، وكذا الموجبة المعدولة أعم صدقاً من الموجبة العدمية؛ لضرورة أما ما كان أعمّ صدقاً من شيء فمقابله أخص صدقاً من مقابل ذلك الشيء، والموجبة العدمية مقابلة للسالبة العدمية والموجبة المعدولة مقابلة السالبة المعدولة، فوجب أن تكون الموجبة العدمية أخص من الموجبة المعدولة، وعلى هذا فنسبة السالبة المعدولة إلى ما سامتها قطراً من الموجبة العدمية فعلى ما مضى من منع الجمع في الصدق دون الكذب، وكذا في المنع من الجمع بين الموجبة المعدولة وما سامتها قطراً من السالبة العدمية في الكذب دون الصدق. وأما المتضادة فحكمها طولاً في العموم والخصوص، وقطراً من الإيجابيات والسالبة المعدولة مع الموجبة العدمية، فعلى ما مضى في المخصوصات دون حكم التقابل عرضاً وباقي الأقطار، وليكن لوحها هكذا:
كل إنسان هو عدل: كلية موجبة بسيطة تصدق إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين وتكذب في الباقي.
لا واحد من الإنسان عدلاً: كلية سالبة بسيطة تكذب إذا كان الكل أو البعض عادلين وتصدق في الباقي.
لا واحد من الإنسان هو لا عادل: كلية سالبة معدولة تكذب إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين وتصدق في الباقي.
كل إنسان هو لا عادل: كلية موجبة معدولة تكذب إذا كانوا كلهم أو بعضهم معدومين أو عادلين وتصدق في الباقي.
كل إنسان هو جائر: كلية موجبة عدمية تصدق إذا كانوا كلهم جائرين فقط وتكذب في الباقي.
فالسالبة المعدولة أعمّ صدقاً من الموجبة البسيطة؛ لصدقها في ما تصدق فيه وزيادة والسالبة العدمية أعم صدقاً من السالبة المعدولة؛ لصدقها في ما تصدق فيه وزيادة، وكذا السالبة البسيطة أعمّ صدقاً من الموجبة المعدولة لصدقها في ما تصدق فيه وزيادة، وكذا حكم الموجبة المعدولة بالنسبة إلى الموجبة العدمية فكانت أحكامها طولاً موافقة لما مضى في المخصوصات.
وأما حكمها عرضاً فامتناع اجتماع كل طبقة مع ما سامتها في الصدق دون الكذب، إذ الصدق لا يتم إلا بصدق كل واحد منها، وفيه صدق إيجاب الشيء الواحد وسلبه معاً وهو محال، والكذب فقد لا يمتنع أن يشتركا فيه، ويكون كذب كل قضية في بعض لم تكن الأخرى فيه كاذبة كما في قولنا : (كل حيوان إنسان) (لا واحد من الحيوان إنسان).
وأما حكم الموجبات قطراً فعلى ما مضى، إذ يستحيل الجمع بينها في الصدق، وإن لم يمتنع في الكذب.
وأما حكم السوالب قطراً وحكم الموجبة المعدولة قطراً بالنسبة إلى السالبة العدمية فبخلاف ما مضى، إذ لا يمتنع الجمع بينها في الكذب واعتبره بنفسك تجده على ما مهدناه.
The first art: On categorical propositions. Mixed, and likewise the modified affirmative is broader in truth than the privative affirmative; necessarily, for whatever is broader in truth than something, its opposite is narrower in truth than the opposite of that thing. The privative affirmative is opposite to the privative negative, and the modified affirmative is opposite to the modified negative. Therefore, the privative affirmative must be narrower than the modified affirmative. According to this, the relation of the modified negative to what is diagonal to it from the privative affirmative follows what has passed of preventing conjunction in truth but not in falsehood, and likewise in preventing conjunction between the modified affirmative and what is diagonal to it from the privative negative in falsehood but not in truth. As for the contraries, their rule vertically in generality and particularity, and diagonally from affirmatives and the modified negative with the privative affirmative, follows what has passed in the specifics, without the rule of opposition horizontally and the remaining diagonals. Let its table be like this: Every human is just: universal simple affirmative, true if all are just, false otherwise. No human is just: universal simple negative, false if all or some are just, true otherwise. No human is not-just: universal modified negative, false if all are just, true otherwise. Every human is not-just: universal modified affirmative, false if all or some are nonexistent or just, true otherwise. Every human is unjust: universal privative affirmative, true if all are unjust only, false otherwise. So the modified negative is broader in truth than the simple affirmative, because it is true wherever it is true and more. The privative negative is broader in truth than the modified negative, because it is true wherever it is true and more. Likewise, the simple negative is broader in truth than the modified affirmative, because it is true wherever it is true and more. Likewise, the rule of the modified affirmative relative to the privative affirmative: so their rules vertically agree with what has passed in the specifics. As for their rule horizontally, it is impossibility of each layer together with its counterpart in truth but not falsehood, because truth is only achieved by the truth of each one of them, and in it is the affirmation and negation of one thing together, which is impossible. As for falsehood, it is not impossible that they share in it, and the falsehood of each proposition in some cases where the other is not false, as in our saying: 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human.' As for the rule of the affirmatives diagonally, it follows what has passed, for conjunction between them in truth is impossible, though not impossible in falsehood. As for the rule of negatives diagonally and the rule of the modified affirmative diagonally relative to the privative negative, it is contrary to what has passed, for conjunction between them in falsehood is not impossible. Consider it yourself, you will find it as we have laid out.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional exposition of the truth conditions for universal categorical propositions, distinguishing between simple (basīṭa), modified (maʿdūla), and privative (ʿadamiyya) forms. The author begins by asserting that the modified affirmative is broader in truth than the privative affirmative, using a principle of opposites: if A is broader than B, then the opposite of A is narrower than the opposite of B. Thus, since the privative affirmative opposes the privative negative, and the modified affirmative opposes the modified negative, the privative affirmative is narrower. The author then discusses the relationships between the diagonal pairs (e.g., modified negative vs. privative affirmative) in terms of the possibility of simultaneous truth or falsehood. The 'contraries' (mutadāddah) are examined vertically (in terms of generality and specificity) and diagonally, following patterns previously established for particular propositions. A table is presented listing five universal propositions: simple affirmative, simple negative, modified negative, modified affirmative, and privative affirmative, each with its truth conditions. After the table, the author explains the relative breadth of truth among these types: the modified negative is broader than the simple affirmative; the privative negative (not listed but implied) is broader than the modified negative; the simple negative is broader than the modified affirmative; and the modified affirmative is broader than the privative affirmative. These vertical relations agree with prior rules for particulars. Horizontally, propositions of the same 'layer' (e.g., two affirmative or two negative) cannot both be true because that would entail affirming and negating the same thing, but they can both be false, as illustrated by the example 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human.' Diagonally, affirmatives cannot be simultaneously true but can be simultaneously false; negatives and the modified affirmative vs. privative negative, however, can be simultaneously false. The author concludes by inviting the reader to verify these rules.

Horizontal and Diagonal Relations: Simultaneous Truth and Falsehood
Explanation of horizontal and diagonal truth relations among proposition types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which universal proposition types can be simultaneously true or false, using the horizontal (عرضاً) and diagonal (قطراً) rules.

Core Idea
Propositions of the same type (e.g., two affirmatives or two negatives) cannot both be true, but can both be false; diagonally, affirmatives cannot both be true but negatives can both be false.

The author continues by examining the relationships between propositions in the same row (horizontal) and across the table (diagonal). He explains that no two propositions of the same layer (e.g., two affirmatives) can be simultaneously true because truth would require affirming and negating the same attribute. However, they can both be false. Diagonally, the rules differ based on the types involved.

Guided Path
1
Understand the horizontal rule (عرضاً)
The author says it is impossible for each layer (i.e., propositions of the same type: two affirmatives or two negatives) to be true together, but they can be false together. Find the example given: 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human'.
These two cannot both be true, but both are false in reality.
2
Identify the diagonal rule for affirmatives
The author states that for diagonally related affirmatives (e.g., simple affirmative and modified affirmative?), conjunction in truth is impossible, but falsehood may concur. Verify with the table.
The text says: 'وأما حكم الموجبات قطراً فعلى ما مضى إذ يستحيل الجمع بينها في الصدق وإن لم يمتنع في الكذب'.
3
Identify the diagonal rule for negatives and mixed pairs
For diagonally related negatives and for the modified affirmative vs. privative negative, the rule is opposite: conjunction in falsehood is possible. The author says 'بخلاف ما مضى إذ لا يمتنع الجمع بينها في الكذب'.
This asymmetry is important for logical analysis.
4
Apply the rules to an Islamic example
Take two universal propositions from Fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and 'No intoxicant is forbidden'. Can they both be true? Can they both be false? Use the horizontal rule.
They are simple affirmative and simple negative, which are horizontal contradictories? Actually they are contradictories, so they cannot both be true nor both false. But the author's horizontal rule says two affirmatives cannot both be true, but can both be false. Here it's an affirmative and a negative, which is a different case. Perhaps the horizontal rule applies to propositions of the same sign? Clarify.

الفن الأول: في القضايا الحملية
مختلطين، وكذا الموجبة المعدولة أعم صدقاً من الموجبة العدمية؛ لضرورة أما ما كان أعمّ صدقاً من شيء فمقابله أخص صدقاً من مقابل ذلك الشيء، والموجبة العدمية مقابلة للسالبة العدمية والموجبة المعدولة مقابلة السالبة المعدولة، فوجب أن تكون الموجبة العدمية أخص من الموجبة المعدولة، وعلى هذا فنسبة السالبة المعدولة إلى ما سامتها قطراً من الموجبة العدمية فعلى ما مضى من منع الجمع في الصدق دون الكذب، وكذا في المنع من الجمع بين الموجبة المعدولة وما سامتها قطراً من السالبة العدمية في الكذب دون الصدق. وأما المتضادة فحكمها طولاً في العموم والخصوص، وقطراً من الإيجابيات والسالبة المعدولة مع الموجبة العدمية، فعلى ما مضى في المخصوصات دون حكم التقابل عرضاً وباقي الأقطار، وليكن لوحها هكذا:
كل إنسان هو عدل: كلية موجبة بسيطة تصدق إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين وتكذب في الباقي.
لا واحد من الإنسان عدلاً: كلية سالبة بسيطة تكذب إذا كان الكل أو البعض عادلين وتصدق في الباقي.
لا واحد من الإنسان هو لا عادل: كلية سالبة معدولة تكذب إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين وتصدق في الباقي.
كل إنسان هو لا عادل: كلية موجبة معدولة تكذب إذا كانوا كلهم أو بعضهم معدومين أو عادلين وتصدق في الباقي.
كل إنسان هو جائر: كلية موجبة عدمية تصدق إذا كانوا كلهم جائرين فقط وتكذب في الباقي.
فالسالبة المعدولة أعمّ صدقاً من الموجبة البسيطة؛ لصدقها في ما تصدق فيه وزيادة والسالبة العدمية أعم صدقاً من السالبة المعدولة؛ لصدقها في ما تصدق فيه وزيادة، وكذا السالبة البسيطة أعمّ صدقاً من الموجبة المعدولة لصدقها في ما تصدق فيه وزيادة، وكذا حكم الموجبة المعدولة بالنسبة إلى الموجبة العدمية فكانت أحكامها طولاً موافقة لما مضى في المخصوصات.
وأما حكمها عرضاً فامتناع اجتماع كل طبقة مع ما سامتها في الصدق دون الكذب، إذ الصدق لا يتم إلا بصدق كل واحد منها، وفيه صدق إيجاب الشيء الواحد وسلبه معاً وهو محال، والكذب فقد لا يمتنع أن يشتركا فيه، ويكون كذب كل قضية في بعض لم تكن الأخرى فيه كاذبة كما في قولنا : (كل حيوان إنسان) (لا واحد من الحيوان إنسان).
وأما حكم الموجبات قطراً فعلى ما مضى، إذ يستحيل الجمع بينها في الصدق، وإن لم يمتنع في الكذب.
وأما حكم السوالب قطراً وحكم الموجبة المعدولة قطراً بالنسبة إلى السالبة العدمية فبخلاف ما مضى، إذ لا يمتنع الجمع بينها في الكذب واعتبره بنفسك تجده على ما مهدناه.
The first art: On categorical propositions. Mixed, and likewise the modified affirmative is broader in truth than the privative affirmative; necessarily, for whatever is broader in truth than something, its opposite is narrower in truth than the opposite of that thing. The privative affirmative is opposite to the privative negative, and the modified affirmative is opposite to the modified negative. Therefore, the privative affirmative must be narrower than the modified affirmative. According to this, the relation of the modified negative to what is diagonal to it from the privative affirmative follows what has passed of preventing conjunction in truth but not in falsehood, and likewise in preventing conjunction between the modified affirmative and what is diagonal to it from the privative negative in falsehood but not in truth. As for the contraries, their rule vertically in generality and particularity, and diagonally from affirmatives and the modified negative with the privative affirmative, follows what has passed in the specifics, without the rule of opposition horizontally and the remaining diagonals. Let its table be like this: Every human is just: universal simple affirmative, true if all are just, false otherwise. No human is just: universal simple negative, false if all or some are just, true otherwise. No human is not-just: universal modified negative, false if all are just, true otherwise. Every human is not-just: universal modified affirmative, false if all or some are nonexistent or just, true otherwise. Every human is unjust: universal privative affirmative, true if all are unjust only, false otherwise. So the modified negative is broader in truth than the simple affirmative, because it is true wherever it is true and more. The privative negative is broader in truth than the modified negative, because it is true wherever it is true and more. Likewise, the simple negative is broader in truth than the modified affirmative, because it is true wherever it is true and more. Likewise, the rule of the modified affirmative relative to the privative affirmative: so their rules vertically agree with what has passed in the specifics. As for their rule horizontally, it is impossibility of each layer together with its counterpart in truth but not falsehood, because truth is only achieved by the truth of each one of them, and in it is the affirmation and negation of one thing together, which is impossible. As for falsehood, it is not impossible that they share in it, and the falsehood of each proposition in some cases where the other is not false, as in our saying: 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human.' As for the rule of the affirmatives diagonally, it follows what has passed, for conjunction between them in truth is impossible, though not impossible in falsehood. As for the rule of negatives diagonally and the rule of the modified affirmative diagonally relative to the privative negative, it is contrary to what has passed, for conjunction between them in falsehood is not impossible. Consider it yourself, you will find it as we have laid out.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definitional exposition of the truth conditions for universal categorical propositions, distinguishing between simple (basīṭa), modified (maʿdūla), and privative (ʿadamiyya) forms. The author begins by asserting that the modified affirmative is broader in truth than the privative affirmative, using a principle of opposites: if A is broader than B, then the opposite of A is narrower than the opposite of B. Thus, since the privative affirmative opposes the privative negative, and the modified affirmative opposes the modified negative, the privative affirmative is narrower. The author then discusses the relationships between the diagonal pairs (e.g., modified negative vs. privative affirmative) in terms of the possibility of simultaneous truth or falsehood. The 'contraries' (mutadāddah) are examined vertically (in terms of generality and specificity) and diagonally, following patterns previously established for particular propositions. A table is presented listing five universal propositions: simple affirmative, simple negative, modified negative, modified affirmative, and privative affirmative, each with its truth conditions. After the table, the author explains the relative breadth of truth among these types: the modified negative is broader than the simple affirmative; the privative negative (not listed but implied) is broader than the modified negative; the simple negative is broader than the modified affirmative; and the modified affirmative is broader than the privative affirmative. These vertical relations agree with prior rules for particulars. Horizontally, propositions of the same 'layer' (e.g., two affirmative or two negative) cannot both be true because that would entail affirming and negating the same thing, but they can both be false, as illustrated by the example 'Every animal is a human' and 'No animal is a human.' Diagonally, affirmatives cannot be simultaneously true but can be simultaneously false; negatives and the modified affirmative vs. privative negative, however, can be simultaneously false. The author concludes by inviting the reader to verify these rules.

Truth Conditions of Particular Categorical Propositions
Author defines particular simple, modified, and privative propositions with truth tables.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of any particular categorical proposition (simple or modified) by applying the truth conditions from the classical text.

Core Idea
Particular propositions' truth depends on whether the predicate applies to at least one subject, with special rules for negatives and modified terms.

In this lesson, we explore the truth conditions for particular categorical propositions as defined by the author. You will learn to distinguish between simple and modified affirmatives and negatives, and see how each is evaluated against the contrary cases. This builds essential skills for analyzing logical claims in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where 'some' or 'not all' statements are common.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is affirmative or negative, simple or modified. For example, 'Not every human is just' is a particular simple negative. Look for keywords like 'بعض' (some) for affirmatives and 'ليس كل' (not all) for negatives, and note if the predicate is modified (e.g., 'غير عادل').
Modified propositions use the negated form of the predicate.
2
Extract the subject and predicate
Clearly identify the subject term (e.g., 'human') and the predicate term (e.g., 'just'). For modified predicates, the predicate is the negated term (e.g., 'not-just').
This step is crucial for applying the truth condition correctly.
3
Apply the truth condition from the table
For each type, the text gives precise conditions. For example, the particular simple affirmative is true if all or some of the subject are just, false otherwise. The particular simple negative is false if all are just, true otherwise. The particular modified negative is true if all or some are just or nonexistent, false otherwise. The particular modified affirmative is false if all are just or nonexistent, true otherwise.
Memorize the patterns: simple affirmative true when at least some; simple negative false only when all; modified negative true when at least some are just or nonexistent; modified affirmative false when all are just or nonexistent.
4
Test with a concrete scenario
Consider a scenario: in a group of 10 Muslims, 5 are just, 3 are unjust, and 2 are nonexistent (i.e., not part of the domain). Determine the truth of each particular proposition about Muslims and justice. For example, 'Some Muslims are just' is true, 'Not every Muslim is just' is true, 'Not every Muslim is not-just' is true (since some are just and some are nonexistent), 'Some Muslims are not-just' is true (since some are unjust).
Practice with different distributions to internalize the conditions.

Truth Conditions of Particular Propositions under Contradictory Terms
Author lists truth conditions for six particular propositions under contradictory terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the truth conditions for each of the six particular proposition forms involving contradictory terms like 'just' and 'unjust'.

Core Idea
Particular propositions with contradictory predicates have precise truth conditions based on distribution of just, unjust, and intermediate states.

This passage examines six particular propositions involving justice ('adl) and injustice (jauwr). The author specifies exactly when each is true or false. This precision is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic ethics and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the six proposition types
Identify the simple affirmative/negative, privative (ma'dula) affirmative/negative, and negative ('adamiyya) affirmative/negative forms. Note the predicate differences: 'just', 'not-just', 'unjust'.
The privative uses 'not-just' (ghayr 'adil); the negative uses 'unjust' (jair).
2
Identify the three possible states of individuals
Under contradictory terms, individuals can be either: (a) just, (b) unjust, or (c) lacking (ma'dum, i.e., neither just nor unjust).
The 'lacking' state creates the middle ground.
3
Determine truth conditions for each proposition
For each proposition, check when it is true based on the distribution of the three states among individuals. Use the author's table as reference.
For example, the simple affirmative is true if all are just or some are just (rest any).
4
Compare truth ranges between forms
Notice which propositions have broader truth ranges. The privative negative is broader than the simple affirmative but narrower than the negative negative.
This hierarchy is the basis of lengthwise opposition.
5
Verify with the provided table
Cross-check each proposition's truth condition against the passage's explicit statements.

أما أنها موافقة للمخصوصات في المضالعة طولاً، فلان الموجبة البسيطة لا تصدق إلا إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين أو بعضهم والسالبة المعدولة تصدق مع كون كلهم معدومين أو بعضهم، والسالبة العدمية تصدق في الجميع إلا إذا كانوا كلهم جائرين فكانت السالبة المعدولة أعمّ صدقاً من الموجبة البسيطة وأخص من السالبة العدمية، وكذا الموجبة المعدولة أعم صدقاً من الموجبة العدمية وأخص من السالبة البسيطة، إذ هي تصدق عند كون الكل جائرين أو بعضهم، وهو ما تصدق فيه الموجبة العدمية وتصدق في ما لا تصدق فيه الموجبة العدمية، وهو ما إذا كانوا كلهم أو بعضهم لا جائرين ولا عادلين وفي غير ذلك من الأقسام ما خلا كون الكل عادلين أو معدومين وتكذب في ما تكذب فيه السالبة البسيطة، وهو ما إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين وفي ما لا تكذب فيه وهو ما إذا كانوا كلهم معدومين وأما أنها غير موافقة للمخصوصات عرضاً ولا مقاطرة على الوجه المذكور، فمن جهة أنها جزئيات والجزئيات لا يمتنع اجتماعها في الصدق؛ لجواز أن يكون البعض المحكوم عليه بالإيجاب غير البعض المحكوم عليه بالسلب فلا تقابل ولا لها حكم التقاط . وأما المهملات فحكمها حكم الجزئيات الداخلة تحت المتضادات أيضاً وقد عرف.
As for the particular propositions that fall under contradictory terms, they agree with the universal propositions in lengthwise opposition and differ from them in breadthwise and pole-wise opposition between the simple affirmative, the two affirmatives (privative and negative), the privative negative, and the negative affirmative. Let their table be as follows: Some people are just. Not every man is just. [It is] a simple particular affirmative, true when all are just or some are just however the rest are, false in the rest. Not every man is not-just. [It is] a particular privative negative, true when all or some are just or lacking, false when all are just or lacking, true in the rest. Some people are not-just. [It is] a particular privative affirmative, false when all are just or lacking, true in the rest. Not every man is unjust. [It is] a particular negative negative, false when all are unjust, true in the rest. Some people are unjust. [It is] a particular negative affirmative, true when all are unjust or some, false in the rest. As for their agreement with universals in lengthwise opposition: the simple affirmative is true only when all are just or some, and the privative negative is true when all are lacking or some, and the negative negative is true in all except when all are unjust; thus the privative negative is broader in truth than the simple affirmative and narrower than the negative negative. Similarly, the privative affirmative is broader in truth than the negative affirmative and narrower than the simple negative, for it is true when all are unjust or some—which is where the negative affirmative is true—and it is true where the negative affirmative is not true, namely when all or some are neither unjust nor just, and in other divisions except when all are just or lacking; and it is false where the simple negative is false, namely when all are just, and where it is not false, namely when all are lacking. As for their disagreement with universals in breadthwise and pole-wise opposition as mentioned, it is because they are particulars, and particulars are not prevented from being jointly true, for it is possible that the part judged affirmatively is not the part judged negatively; thus there is no opposition and they do not have the ruling of poles. As for indeterminate propositions, their ruling is the ruling of particular propositions under contradictories, as has been known.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage examines the truth conditions and logical relations of particular propositions (juz'iyyat) that fall under contradictory terms (mutadadda). The author first states that these particular propositions agree with universal propositions (makhsusat) in 'lengthwise opposition' (musada'a tulan), but differ in 'breadthwise' ('ardan) and 'pole-wise' (qatran) opposition. He then presents a table listing six proposition forms: (1) simple affirmative: 'Some people are just'; (2) simple negative: 'Not every man is just'; (3) privative negative: 'Not every man is not-just'; (4) privative affirmative: 'Some people are not-just'; (5) negative negative: 'Not every man is unjust'; (6) negative affirmative: 'Some people are unjust'. For each, he specifies when it is true and false based on the distribution of justice/injustice and the intermediate state of 'lacking' (ma'dum, i.e., neither just nor unjust). For example, the simple affirmative is true if all are just or some are just (the rest could be anything), false otherwise. The privative negative is true if all or some are just or lacking, false only if all are just or all are lacking. The privative affirmative is false if all are just or all are lacking, true otherwise. The negative negative is false only if all are unjust, true otherwise. The negative affirmative is true if all are unjust or some are unjust, false otherwise. 

The author then explains why these particulars agree with universals 'lengthwise': the privative negative is broader in truth than the simple affirmative but narrower than the negative negative; similarly, the privative affirmative is broader than the negative affirmative but narrower than the simple negative. This creates a hierarchy. 

However, they differ from universals 'breadthwise' and 'pole-wise' because they are particulars. Two particular propositions can be simultaneously true if they refer to different individuals (e.g., 'Some people are just' and 'Some people are unjust' can both be true), so they do not have the strict opposition (taqabul) that universals have. There is no 'pole' (qutb) ruling for them. 

Finally, the author notes that indeterminate propositions (muhmalaat) follow the same rules as these particular propositions. 

The entire discussion uses examples from ethics ('adil = just, jair = unjust) which are Islamic in character. No secular examples appear.

Lengthwise and Breadthwise Opposition in Particular Propositions
Author explains agreement and difference of particulars with universals in opposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why particular propositions under contradictory terms agree with universals in lengthwise opposition but differ in breadthwise and pole-wise opposition.

Core Idea
Particular propositions form a truth hierarchy (lengthwise) but lack strict opposition (breadthwise/pole-wise) because they can be simultaneously true.

The author explains that particular propositions share a certain hierarchical relationship with universals (lengthwise opposition), but unlike universals, they do not exhibit strict contradictory or contrary relations. This is because particulars refer to potentially different subsets, allowing joint truth.

Guided Path
1
Recall the six proposition forms and their truth ranges
List the six particular propositions: simple affirmative, simple negative, privative affirmative, privative negative, negative affirmative, negative negative. Note the truth conditions from previous lesson.
2
Understand 'lengthwise opposition' (musada'a tulan)
The author shows a hierarchy: privative negative broader than simple affirmative but narrower than negative negative; privative affirmative broader than negative affirmative but narrower than simple negative. This is like subalternation in the square of opposition.
This hierarchy is called 'lengthwise' because it corresponds to vertical (subaltern) relations in the square.
3
Identify why particulars differ in breadthwise and pole-wise opposition
Universal propositions (all/no) have strict opposition: contradictory, contrary, subcontrary. But particular propositions (some/not all) can be jointly true; e.g., 'Some are just' and 'Some are unjust' can both be true if different individuals.
Breadthwise opposition refers to horizontal relations (e.g., contrary), pole-wise to contradictory. These do not apply to particulars in the same way.
4
Apply this to an Islamic example
Consider the universal 'All believers are just' and its contradictory 'Some believers are not just'. The universal's truth forces the particular's falsity. But two particulars like 'Some believers are just' and 'Some believers are unjust' can both be true if the community has both types.
This distinction is critical in theological debates about the moral status of individuals.
5
Note the ruling for indeterminate propositions
The author adds that indeterminate propositions (e.g., 'Man is just') follow the same rules as these particular propositions.

Source Text

وأما الجزئيات الداخلة تحت المتضادة فهي موافقة للمخصوصات في المضالعة طولاً ومخالفة لها عرضاً وقطراً بين الموجبة البسيطة والموجبتين المعدولة والعدمية والسالبة المعدولة والموجبة العدمية، وليكن لوحها هكذا:
بعض الناس عادلاً
ليس كل إنسان عادلاً
جزئية موجبة بسيطة تصدق إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين أو بعضهم والباقون كيف كانوا كلهم عادلين وتصدق كانوا، وتكذب في البواقي.
ليس كل إنسان هو غير عادل
في البواقي.
بعض الناس هو غير عادل
جزئية سالبة معدولة تصدق إذا كانوا كلهم أو بعضهم عادلين أو معدومين، كانوا كلهم عادلين أو معدومين وتكذب في البواقي.
وتصدق في البواقي.
ليس كل إنسان جائراً
جزئية سالبة عدمية تكذب إذا كانوا كلهم جائرين، وتصدق في البواقي.
بعض الناس جائر
جزئية موجبة عدمية تصدق إذا كانوا كلهم جائرين أو بعضهم، وتكذب في البواقي.
أما أنها موافقة للمخصوصات في المضالعة طولاً، فلان الموجبة البسيطة لا تصدق إلا إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين أو بعضهم والسالبة المعدولة تصدق مع كون كلهم معدومين أو بعضهم، والسالبة العدمية تصدق في الجميع إلا إذا كانوا كلهم جائرين فكانت السالبة المعدولة أعمّ صدقاً من الموجبة البسيطة وأخص من السالبة العدمية، وكذا الموجبة المعدولة أعم صدقاً من الموجبة العدمية وأخص من السالبة البسيطة، إذ هي تصدق عند كون الكل جائرين أو بعضهم، وهو ما تصدق فيه الموجبة العدمية وتصدق في ما لا تصدق فيه الموجبة العدمية، وهو ما إذا كانوا كلهم أو بعضهم لا جائرين ولا عادلين وفي غير ذلك من الأقسام ما خلا كون الكل عادلين أو معدومين وتكذب في ما تكذب فيه السالبة البسيطة، وهو ما إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين وفي ما لا تكذب فيه وهو ما إذا كانوا كلهم معدومين وأما أنها غير موافقة للمخصوصات عرضاً ولا مقاطرة على الوجه المذكور، فمن جهة أنها جزئيات والجزئيات لا يمتنع اجتماعها في الصدق؛ لجواز أن يكون البعض المحكوم عليه بالإيجاب غير البعض المحكوم عليه بالسلب فلا تقابل ولا لها حكم التقاط . وأما المهملات فحكمها حكم الجزئيات الداخلة تحت المتضادات أيضاً وقد عرف.
As for the particular propositions that fall under contradictory terms, they agree with the universal propositions in lengthwise opposition and differ from them in breadthwise and pole-wise opposition between the simple affirmative, the two affirmatives (privative and negative), the privative negative, and the negative affirmative. Let their table be as follows: Some people are just. Not every man is just. [It is] a simple particular affirmative, true when all are just or some are just however the rest are, false in the rest. Not every man is not-just. [It is] a particular privative negative, true when all or some are just or lacking, false when all are just or lacking, true in the rest. Some people are not-just. [It is] a particular privative affirmative, false when all are just or lacking, true in the rest. Not every man is unjust. [It is] a particular negative negative, false when all are unjust, true in the rest. Some people are unjust. [It is] a particular negative affirmative, true when all are unjust or some, false in the rest. As for their agreement with universals in lengthwise opposition: the simple affirmative is true only when all are just or some, and the privative negative is true when all are lacking or some, and the negative negative is true in all except when all are unjust; thus the privative negative is broader in truth than the simple affirmative and narrower than the negative negative. Similarly, the privative affirmative is broader in truth than the negative affirmative and narrower than the simple negative, for it is true when all are unjust or some—which is where the negative affirmative is true—and it is true where the negative affirmative is not true, namely when all or some are neither unjust nor just, and in other divisions except when all are just or lacking; and it is false where the simple negative is false, namely when all are just, and where it is not false, namely when all are lacking. As for their disagreement with universals in breadthwise and pole-wise opposition as mentioned, it is because they are particulars, and particulars are not prevented from being jointly true, for it is possible that the part judged affirmatively is not the part judged negatively; thus there is no opposition and they do not have the ruling of poles. As for indeterminate propositions, their ruling is the ruling of particular propositions under contradictories, as has been known.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage examines the truth conditions and logical relations of particular propositions (juz'iyyat) that fall under contradictory terms (mutadadda). The author first states that these particular propositions agree with universal propositions (makhsusat) in 'lengthwise opposition' (musada'a tulan), but differ in 'breadthwise' ('ardan) and 'pole-wise' (qatran) opposition. He then presents a table listing six proposition forms: (1) simple affirmative: 'Some people are just'; (2) simple negative: 'Not every man is just'; (3) privative negative: 'Not every man is not-just'; (4) privative affirmative: 'Some people are not-just'; (5) negative negative: 'Not every man is unjust'; (6) negative affirmative: 'Some people are unjust'. For each, he specifies when it is true and false based on the distribution of justice/injustice and the intermediate state of 'lacking' (ma'dum, i.e., neither just nor unjust). For example, the simple affirmative is true if all are just or some are just (the rest could be anything), false otherwise. The privative negative is true if all or some are just or lacking, false only if all are just or all are lacking. The privative affirmative is false if all are just or all are lacking, true otherwise. The negative negative is false only if all are unjust, true otherwise. The negative affirmative is true if all are unjust or some are unjust, false otherwise. 

The author then explains why these particulars agree with universals 'lengthwise': the privative negative is broader in truth than the simple affirmative but narrower than the negative negative; similarly, the privative affirmative is broader than the negative affirmative but narrower than the simple negative. This creates a hierarchy. 

However, they differ from universals 'breadthwise' and 'pole-wise' because they are particulars. Two particular propositions can be simultaneously true if they refer to different individuals (e.g., 'Some people are just' and 'Some people are unjust' can both be true), so they do not have the strict opposition (taqabul) that universals have. There is no 'pole' (qutb) ruling for them. 

Finally, the author notes that indeterminate propositions (muhmalaat) follow the same rules as these particular propositions. 

The entire discussion uses examples from ethics ('adil = just, jair = unjust) which are Islamic in character. No secular examples appear.

Key Terms
جزئية
المتضادة
معدولة
عدمية
المضالعة

Checkpoint
Why do particular propositions NOT have breadthwise or pole-wise opposition according to the passage?
A) Because they are too many to have opposition.
B) Because they cannot be false at the same time.
C) Because they can be jointly true if they refer to different individuals.
D) Because they are not part of the square of opposition.

Key Takeaway
Particular propositions can be jointly true, so they lack the strict opposition relations found in universals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the universal claim 'Every act is either obligatory or prohibited' (assuming no middle). Construct the corresponding particular propositions (some, not all, etc.) and check which pairs can be true together under the author's rules.
?
In a theological debate about divine attributes, suppose someone says 'Some of God's attributes are eternal' and 'Some of God's attributes are temporal'. According to the author's teaching, can both be true? Explain why or why not, referencing the concept of joint truth of particulars.

Truth Conditions of Particular Privative Propositions
Explains truth conditions of particular privative propositions and compares them with modified and simple ones

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of any particular privative proposition and compare its truth range with modified and simple propositions.

Core Idea
Truth conditions in categorical logic depend on quantifier, quality, and predicate type.

This passage clarifies the truth conditions of particular negative and affirmative privative propositions, and then compares their truth ranges with modified and simple propositions. Understanding these distinctions is essential for analyzing logical relations like opposition and for correctly interpreting propositions in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four proposition types
From the examples, identify the four types: particular negative privative (جُزئية سالبة عَدَمية), particular affirmative privative (جُزئية موجبة عَدَمية), modified negative (سالبة معدولة), and modified affirmative (موجبة معدولة). Note their quantifiers, qualities, and whether the predicate is privative or modified.
A privative predicate indicates absence (e.g., 'unjust'), a modified predicate uses negation (e.g., 'non-existent').
2
Extract truth conditions for each type
From the text, list exactly when each proposition is true and when false. For example, the particular negative privative 'ليس كل إنسان جائراً' is false only if all humans are unjust, true otherwise.
3
Construct a comparison of truth ranges
The author states that the modified negative is more general in truth than the simple affirmative and more specific than the privative negative. Map out the truth ranges of all four types in a table, noting where they overlap.
Use the passage's descriptions of when each type is true/false.
4
Explain why particulars avoid opposition
The passage explains that two particular propositions are not in opposition because they can both be true if they refer to different subsets. Understand this principle: the contradictories and contraries require universal quantifiers.
This is crucial for understanding the square of opposition.

الفن الأول: في القضايا الحملية ليس كل إنسان جائراً جزئية سالبة عدمية تكذب إذا كانوا كلهم جائرين، وتصدق في البواقي. بعض الناس جائر جزئية موجبة عدمية تصدق إذا كانوا كلهم جائرين أو بعضهم، وتكذب في البواقي. أما أنها موافقة للمخصوصات في المضالعة طولاً، فلان الموجبة البسيطة لا تصدق إلا إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين أو بعضهم والسالبة المعدولة تصدق مع كون كلهم معدومين أو بعضهم، والسالبة العدمية تصدق في الجميع إلا إذا كانوا كلهم جائرين فكانت السالبة المعدولة أعمّ صدقاً من الموجبة البسيطة وأخص من السالبة العدمية، وكذا الموجبة المعدولة أعم صدقاً من الموجبة العدمية وأخص من السالبة البسيطة، إذ هي تصدق عند كون الكل جائرين أو بعضهم، وهو ما تصدق فيه الموجبة العدمية وتصدق في ما لا تصدق فيه الموجبة العدمية، وهو ما إذا كانوا كلهم أو بعضهم لا جائرين ولا عادلين وفي غير ذلك من الأقسام ما خلا كون الكل عادلين أو معدومين وتكذب في ما تكذب فيه السالبة البسيطة، وهو ما إذا كانوا كلهم عادلين وفي ما لا تكذب فيه وهو ما إذا كانوا كلهم معدومين وأما أنها غير موافقة للمخصوصات عرضاً ولا مقاطرة على الوجه المذكور، فمن جهة أنها جزئيات والجزئيات لا يمتنع اجتماعها في الصدق؛ لجواز أن يكون البعض المحكوم عليه بالإيجاب غير البعض المحكوم عليه بالسلب فلا تقابل ولا لها حكم التقاط . وأما المهملات فحكمها حكم الجزئيات الداخلة تحت المتضادات أيضاً وقد عرف.
The first art: On categorical propositions. 'Not every human is unjust' is a particular negative privative proposition; it is false if all of them are unjust, and true in the remaining cases. 'Some humans are unjust' is a particular affirmative privative proposition; it is true if all of them are unjust or some of them, and false in the remaining cases. As for it being in agreement with the particulars in opposition in length, because the simple affirmative is not true except if all of them are just or some of them, and the modified negative is true with all of them being non-existent or some of them, and the privative negative is true in all except if all of them are unjust. Thus the modified negative is more general in truth than the simple affirmative and more specific than the privative negative. Similarly, the modified affirmative is more general in truth than the privative affirmative and more specific than the simple negative, for it is true when all are unjust or some, which is what the privative affirmative is true in, and it is true in what the privative affirmative is not true in, which is when all or some of them are neither unjust nor just, and in other divisions except when all are just or non-existent. And it is false in what the simple negative is false in, which is when all of them are just, and in what it is not false in, which is when all of them are non-existent. As for it not agreeing with the particulars in width or in opposition in the mentioned manner, it is because they are particulars, and particulars are not prevented from being jointly true, because it is possible that the part judged positively is not the part judged negatively, so they are not in opposition and they do not have the rule of opposition. As for indefinite propositions, their rule is the rule of particulars falling under contraries as well. And it is known.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage defines and compares the truth conditions of various types of categorical propositions, specifically focusing on particular propositions with privative (عدمية) and modified (معدولة) terms. The author begins by giving examples: 'Not every human is unjust' is a particular negative privative (جزئية سالبة عدمية), which is false only if all humans are unjust, and true otherwise. Conversely, 'Some humans are unjust' is a particular affirmative privative (جزئية موجبة عدمية), true if all or some are unjust, false otherwise. The author then explains that these propositions align with the 'particulars' (المخصوصات) in terms of opposition in a certain way. The simple affirmative (موجبة بسيطة) is true only when all or some are just. The modified negative (سالبة معدولة) is true when all or some are non-existent (معدومين). The privative negative (سالبة عدمية) is true in all cases except when all are unjust. Thus, the modified negative has a wider truth scope than the simple affirmative but narrower than the privative negative. Similarly, the modified affirmative (موجبة معدولة) has a wider truth scope than the privative affirmative but narrower than the simple negative. The author details the ranges of truth and falsehood for each type. He then notes that these propositions are not in agreement with the particulars in width or in opposition because they are particulars; two particular propositions can both be true if they refer to different subjects, so they are not in a relation of opposition. Finally, indefinite propositions (مهملات) follow the same rule as particulars regarding contraries.

The Three Materials of Propositions
Defines the three materials of propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a categorical proposition is necessary (ضروري), impossible (ممتنع), or possible (ممكن) by analysing its material.

Core Idea
Every proposition has a material that determines whether its truth is necessary, impossible, or merely possible.

In logic, not all propositions are equal. Some are always true, some always false, and some may be either. The material of a proposition is the state of the predicate relative to the subject that decides this permanence. This lesson will help you classify any categorical proposition into one of three materials, using examples from Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
Extract the subject (موضوع) and predicate (محمول) of the proposition. For example, in 'Human is an animal', subject = human, predicate = animal.
2
Determine the relation's permanence
Ask: Is the predicate always true of the subject? Always false? Or sometimes true and sometimes false? This gives the material.
Permanent truth = material of affirmation (مادة الإيجاب); permanent falsehood = material of impossibility (مادة الامتناع); neither = material of possibility (مادة الإمكان).
3
Classify the proposition
Assign the material based on your answer. For example, 'Human is an animal' is always true → material of affirmation. 'Stone is human' is always false → material of impossibility. 'Human writes' is sometimes true, sometimes false → material of possibility.
Use the provided examples: animal/human for affirmation, human/stone for impossibility, writing/human for possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في مواد القضايا وجهاتها وإطلاقها وتلازم الجهات وتعاندها أما مادة القضية فهي حالة المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع، من دوام صدق أو كذب، أو لا دوامهما، فعلى هذا نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع إما أن يكون بالإيجاب دائماً ويجب صدق إيجابها ، أو بالسلب دائماً ويجب صدق سلبه، أو أنه لا دوام ولا يجب صدق أحدهما، فإن كان الأول فحالة المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع تسمى مادة) (الإيجاب كحال الحيوان أو الناطق بالنسبة إلى الإنسان، وإن كان الثاني فتسمى مادة الامتناع كحال الإنسان والفرس بالنسبة إلى الحجر، وإن كان الثالث فتسمى مادة (الإمكان كحال الكتابة والضحك بالنسبة إلى الإنسان، فهذه هي مواد القضايا، وربما سميت (عناصر) بالنسبة إليها. وأما جهات القضايا وربما سميت أنواعاً، فربما قال بعض المفسرين لكلام المعلم الأول إنها الظنون، والاعتقادات الحقيقية الصادقة على الأحوال الوجودية للأشياء أنفسها، لا أنها بحسب القول واللفظ، إذ الأسماء إنما هي أولاً للمعلومات، وثانياً لموجودات، فلو كانت جهات الأحوال الموجودة هي الأسماء لقد كان لها من حيث هي موجودة أسماء، ومن حيث هي معلومة أسماء، والأمر بخلافه وهو إن كان ما أشار إليه حقاً من أن اعتقاد المحمول من الدوام، ولا دوام زائد على الحال في نفسه وأنه مطابق له ودال عليه، وأن وضع الأسماء أولاً إنما هو على الاعتقادات الدالة على الأشياء الموجودة، وثانياً للموجودات، فغير ممتنع أن تكون دالة عليها وجهة لها باعتبار دلالتها عليها، وإن كانت دلالتها عليها دلالة ثانية. فالحق أن الجهة ما كانت دالة على الحال الموجودة في نفس الأمر مطابقة لها، وذلك قد يكون اعتقاداً كاعتقاد دوام الحيوان للإنسان دوام نفيه عن الحجر.
Chapter Five: On the materials of propositions, their modalities, their absoluteness, the interrelations of modalities, and their contrariety. As for the material of a proposition, it is the state of the predicate in relation to the subject, of permanence of truth or falsehood, or their non-permanence. Accordingly, the relation of the predicate to the subject is either in affirmation always and its affirmation is necessarily true, or in negation always and its negation is necessarily true, or it is not permanent and it is not necessary that either be true. If the first, then the state of the predicate in relation to the subject is called the material of affirmation (مادة الإيجاب), like the state of 'animal' or 'rational' in relation to 'human'. If the second, it is called the material of impossibility (مادة الامتناع), like the state of 'human' and 'horse' in relation to 'stone'. If the third, it is called the material of possibility (مادة الإمكان), like the state of 'writing' and 'laughing' in relation to 'human'. These are the materials of propositions, and they are sometimes called 'elements' (عناصر) in relation to them. As for the modalities of propositions, sometimes called 'types', some interpreters of the First Teacher's words say that they are opinions and true beliefs concerning the existential states of things themselves, not according to speech and utterance, since names are first for known things and second for existents. If the modalities of existing states were names, then they would have names as existing and names as known, but it is not so. And if what he pointed out is true—that belief of the predicate in terms of permanence or non-permanence is additional to the state in itself, and it is in accordance with it and indicative of it, and that the placing of names is first for beliefs indicative of existing things and second for existents—then it is not impossible that they (the modalities) be indicative of them and a modality for them by virtue of their indication of them, even if their indication of them is a secondary indication. So the truth is that the modality is that which indicates the state existing in actuality in conformity with it, and that may be a belief, like the belief of the permanence of 'animal' for 'human' and the permanence of its negation for 'stone'.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage defines the key concepts of the material (مادة) and modality (جهة) of categorical propositions, and clarifies a debate about the nature of modalities. The author begins by stating that the material of a proposition is the state of the predicate relative to the subject in terms of permanent truth or falsehood. Three possibilities are distinguished: (1) The predicate always applies to the subject (necessary affirmation), e.g., 'Human is an animal' or 'Human is rational'; this is called the material of affirmation (مادة الإيجاب). (2) The predicate is always negated of the subject (necessary negation), e.g., 'Stone is human' or 'Stone is horse'; this is the material of impossibility (مادة الامتناع). (3) The predicate may apply or not (possibility), e.g., 'Human writes' or 'Human laughs'; this is the material of possibility (مادة الإمكان). These materials are also called 'elements' (عناصر). Then the author turns to modalities (جهات) and reports a view from some interpreters of Aristotle (the First Teacher): they hold that modalities are true beliefs (ظنون واعتقادات حقيقية) about the actual existential states of things, not merely linguistic expressions. They argue that names (أسماء) primarily refer to known things (معلومات) and secondarily to existents (موجودات). If modalities were merely names of existing states, then every existing state would have two names (as existing and as known). They conclude that modalities are beliefs that indicate the actual states. The author refines this: it is true that belief in permanence or non-permanence is additional to the state itself, but it is in accordance with it. The placement of names is first for beliefs that indicate existing things, then for the existents themselves. Therefore, it is not impossible for modalities to indicate the actual states, though their indication is secondary. The correct view, according to the author, is that the modality is that which indicates the actual state in conformity with it. This can be a belief, like believing that 'animal' permanently applies to 'human' and permanently does not apply to 'stone'.

Key Terms
مادة القضية
مادة الإيجاب
مادة الامتناع
مادة الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Material of affirmation
B) Material of impossibility
C) Material of possibility
D) Material of necessity

Key Takeaway
Every categorical proposition has a material (مادة) that indicates whether its truth is necessary, impossible, or possible, based on the permanence of the predicate's relation to the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Actually it is necessarily true, so affirmation), 'A human is a stone' (material of impossibility), 'A human writes' (material of possibility). Justify each using the criterion of permanence.
?

The Debate on the Nature of Modality
Discusses the nature of modalities and resolves a debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the debate about whether modalities are beliefs or linguistic expressions, and restate the author's refined position that modalities indicate actual states through secondary indication.

Core Idea
Modalities are not mere words; they indicate actual states, even if through secondary indication.

After distinguishing materials, the author turns to modalities (جهات) and reports a debate among interpreters of Aristotle. Some say modalities are true beliefs about existential states, not linguistic expressions. The author refines this by arguing that while belief is additional, modalities can still indicate actual states, albeit secondarily. This lesson unpacks that nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the interpreters' claim
Some interpreters of Aristotle (المعلم الأول) say that modalities (جهات) are 'opinions and true beliefs' (ظنون واعتقادات حقيقية) about the actual existential states of things, not according to speech and utterance.
They argue that names are first for known things (معلومات) and second for existents (موجودات).
2
Understand the objection they raise
They ask: if modalities were names of existing states, then each state would have two names (one as existing, one as known). They claim this is not the case, so modalities cannot be names of existing states.
3
Follow the author's refinement
The author agrees that belief in permanence or non-permanence is additional to the state itself, but it is in accordance with it. The placing of names is first for beliefs that indicate existing things, then for the existents themselves.
Therefore, it is not impossible for modalities to indicate actual states, though their indication is secondary.
4
State the author's final position
The modality is that which indicates the state existing in actuality in conformity with it. This can be a belief, like believing that 'animal' permanently applies to 'human' and permanently does not apply to 'stone'.
This resolves the debate by allowing modalities to both be beliefs and indicate actual states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس في مواد القضايا وجهاتها وإطلاقها وتلازم الجهات وتعاندها أما مادة القضية فهي حالة المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع، من دوام صدق أو كذب، أو لا دوامهما، فعلى هذا نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع إما أن يكون بالإيجاب دائماً ويجب صدق إيجابها ، أو بالسلب دائماً ويجب صدق سلبه، أو أنه لا دوام ولا يجب صدق أحدهما، فإن كان الأول فحالة المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع تسمى مادة) (الإيجاب كحال الحيوان أو الناطق بالنسبة إلى الإنسان، وإن كان الثاني فتسمى مادة الامتناع كحال الإنسان والفرس بالنسبة إلى الحجر، وإن كان الثالث فتسمى مادة (الإمكان كحال الكتابة والضحك بالنسبة إلى الإنسان، فهذه هي مواد القضايا، وربما سميت (عناصر) بالنسبة إليها. وأما جهات القضايا وربما سميت أنواعاً، فربما قال بعض المفسرين لكلام المعلم الأول إنها الظنون، والاعتقادات الحقيقية الصادقة على الأحوال الوجودية للأشياء أنفسها، لا أنها بحسب القول واللفظ، إذ الأسماء إنما هي أولاً للمعلومات، وثانياً لموجودات، فلو كانت جهات الأحوال الموجودة هي الأسماء لقد كان لها من حيث هي موجودة أسماء، ومن حيث هي معلومة أسماء، والأمر بخلافه وهو إن كان ما أشار إليه حقاً من أن اعتقاد المحمول من الدوام، ولا دوام زائد على الحال في نفسه وأنه مطابق له ودال عليه، وأن وضع الأسماء أولاً إنما هو على الاعتقادات الدالة على الأشياء الموجودة، وثانياً للموجودات، فغير ممتنع أن تكون دالة عليها وجهة لها باعتبار دلالتها عليها، وإن كانت دلالتها عليها دلالة ثانية. فالحق أن الجهة ما كانت دالة على الحال الموجودة في نفس الأمر مطابقة لها، وذلك قد يكون اعتقاداً كاعتقاد دوام الحيوان للإنسان دوام نفيه عن الحجر.
Chapter Five: On the materials of propositions, their modalities, their absoluteness, the interrelations of modalities, and their contrariety. As for the material of a proposition, it is the state of the predicate in relation to the subject, of permanence of truth or falsehood, or their non-permanence. Accordingly, the relation of the predicate to the subject is either in affirmation always and its affirmation is necessarily true, or in negation always and its negation is necessarily true, or it is not permanent and it is not necessary that either be true. If the first, then the state of the predicate in relation to the subject is called the material of affirmation (مادة الإيجاب), like the state of 'animal' or 'rational' in relation to 'human'. If the second, it is called the material of impossibility (مادة الامتناع), like the state of 'human' and 'horse' in relation to 'stone'. If the third, it is called the material of possibility (مادة الإمكان), like the state of 'writing' and 'laughing' in relation to 'human'. These are the materials of propositions, and they are sometimes called 'elements' (عناصر) in relation to them. As for the modalities of propositions, sometimes called 'types', some interpreters of the First Teacher's words say that they are opinions and true beliefs concerning the existential states of things themselves, not according to speech and utterance, since names are first for known things and second for existents. If the modalities of existing states were names, then they would have names as existing and names as known, but it is not so. And if what he pointed out is true—that belief of the predicate in terms of permanence or non-permanence is additional to the state in itself, and it is in accordance with it and indicative of it, and that the placing of names is first for beliefs indicative of existing things and second for existents—then it is not impossible that they (the modalities) be indicative of them and a modality for them by virtue of their indication of them, even if their indication of them is a secondary indication. So the truth is that the modality is that which indicates the state existing in actuality in conformity with it, and that may be a belief, like the belief of the permanence of 'animal' for 'human' and the permanence of its negation for 'stone'.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage defines the key concepts of the material (مادة) and modality (جهة) of categorical propositions, and clarifies a debate about the nature of modalities. The author begins by stating that the material of a proposition is the state of the predicate relative to the subject in terms of permanent truth or falsehood. Three possibilities are distinguished: (1) The predicate always applies to the subject (necessary affirmation), e.g., 'Human is an animal' or 'Human is rational'; this is called the material of affirmation (مادة الإيجاب). (2) The predicate is always negated of the subject (necessary negation), e.g., 'Stone is human' or 'Stone is horse'; this is the material of impossibility (مادة الامتناع). (3) The predicate may apply or not (possibility), e.g., 'Human writes' or 'Human laughs'; this is the material of possibility (مادة الإمكان). These materials are also called 'elements' (عناصر). Then the author turns to modalities (جهات) and reports a view from some interpreters of Aristotle (the First Teacher): they hold that modalities are true beliefs (ظنون واعتقادات حقيقية) about the actual existential states of things, not merely linguistic expressions. They argue that names (أسماء) primarily refer to known things (معلومات) and secondarily to existents (موجودات). If modalities were merely names of existing states, then every existing state would have two names (as existing and as known). They conclude that modalities are beliefs that indicate the actual states. The author refines this: it is true that belief in permanence or non-permanence is additional to the state itself, but it is in accordance with it. The placement of names is first for beliefs that indicate existing things, then for the existents themselves. Therefore, it is not impossible for modalities to indicate the actual states, though their indication is secondary. The correct view, according to the author, is that the modality is that which indicates the actual state in conformity with it. This can be a belief, like believing that 'animal' permanently applies to 'human' and permanently does not apply to 'stone'.

Identifying the Three Matters of Propositions
Author defines matter of propositions and its three divisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition into one of the three matters (wujub, imtina', imkan) based on the permanence of truth of its predicate for the subject.

Core Idea
The matter of a proposition reveals whether its truth is necessary, impossible, or merely possible.

In logic, every proposition has a 'matter' (madda) that describes the permanence of the predicate's truth relative to the subject. This distinction is crucial for understanding certainty in theological and legal arguments. In this passage, the author defines matter and divides it into three types: necessary affirmation (wujub), necessary negation (imtina'), and possibility (imkan). Identifying the matter helps you assess the modal force of a claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify subject and predicate
Start with a categorical proposition. Determine its subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul). For example, in 'Animal is true of human', the subject is 'human' and the predicate is 'animal'.
The predicate is the term being affirmed or denied of the subject.
2
Assess permanence of truth
Ask: Is the predicate always true of the subject? Always false? Or sometimes true and sometimes false? The answer determines the matter.
Permanence means the truth value does not change under any circumstances.
3
Classify into wujub, imtina', or imkan
If always true, it is matter of wujub (necessary affirmation). If always false, it is matter of imtina' (necessary negation). If neither, it is matter of imkan (possibility).
Use the examples from the passage: animal/human (wujub), human/stone (imtina'), writing/human (imkan).
4
Distinguish matter from modality
The matter is the objective state, not the linguistic modality. A proposition may lack a modal word but still have a matter. For instance, 'Man is rational' is a wujub even without saying 'necessarily'.
This avoids confusion between the real relation and its verbal expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما مادة القضية فهي حالة المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع، من دوام صدق أو كذب، أو لا دوامهما، فعلى هذا نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع إما أن يكون بالإيجاب دائماً ويجب صدق إيجابها، أو بالسلب دائماً ويجب صدق سلبه، أو أنه لا دوام ولا يجب صدق أحدهما، فإن كان الأول فحالة المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع تسمى مادة الإيجاب كحال الحيوان أو الناطق بالنسبة إلى الإنسان، وإن كان الثاني فتسمى مادة الامتناع كحال الإنسان والفرس بالنسبة إلى الحجر، وإن كان الثالث فتسمى مادة الإمكان كحال الكتابة والضحك بالنسبة إلى الإنسان، فهذه هي مواد القضايا، وربما سميت عناصر بالنسبة إليها.
As for the matter of a proposition, it is the state of the predicate in relation to the subject, in terms of the permanence of truth or falsehood, or the lack of their permanence. So based on this, the relation of the predicate to the subject is either by affirmation permanently and the truth of its affirmation is necessary, or by negation permanently and the truth of its negation is necessary, or it does not persist and the truth of neither is necessary. If it is the first, then the state of the predicate in relation to the subject is called the matter of affirmation, like the state of 'animal' or 'rational' in relation to 'human'; if it is the second, then it is called the matter of impossibility, like the state of 'human' and 'horse' in relation to 'stone'; if it is the third, then it is called the matter of possibility, like the state of 'writing' and 'laughter' in relation to 'human'. These are the matters of propositions, and they are sometimes called 'elements' in relation to them.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'matter' (madda) in propositions. It begins by stating that matter is the state of the predicate relative to the subject concerning permanence of truth or falsehood. The relation can be one of three types: necessary affirmation (wujub) where the predicate is always true, necessary negation (imtina') where it is always false, and possibility (imkan) where it is sometimes true and sometimes false. Examples illustrate each: 'animal' and 'rational' are always true of 'human', so these are matters of affirmation; 'human' and 'horse' are always false of 'stone', matters of impossibility; 'writing' and 'laughter' are sometimes true of 'human', matters of possibility. These three matters are also called 'elements' (anasir). All examples are rooted in essential or accidental attributes in the Islamic intellectual tradition.
