General Possibility and Its Scope
Author defines general possibility and clarifies its relation to specific possibility and necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the general sense of 'possible' from the specific sense and identify which propositions fall under it, including necessary propositions.

Core Idea
General possibility simply means 'not impossible' — it includes necessity itself.

In Islamic logic, the term 'possible' has a broad and a narrow sense. The general sense is deceptively simple: anything whose existence is not impossible. This definition sweeps in both the necessary and the specific possible — a fact that often confuses students. The author of the text clarifies this scope and refutes a subtle objection about the 'first necessary'. Let us unpack his precise formulation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of general possibility
Find the phrase 'mā lā yamtaniʿu wujūduhu' (that whose existence is not impossible). This is the core definition. Note that it is purely negative: it does not affirm contingency or balance.
The Arabic uses the negative 'lā yamtaniʿu' – absence of impossibility.
2
Compare general and specific possible
The author states that the general possible is 'aʿamm' (more general) than the specific possible. The specific possible is that which is neither necessary nor impossible. The general possible includes the specific possible as a subset, plus the necessary.
Draw a Venn diagram: the general possible is the universe of non-impossible things.
3
Identify what falls under general possibility
The text explicitly includes 'al-wājib' (necessary), whether its necessity is per se (li-dhātihi) or per aliud (li-ghayrihi). Ask: Why is the necessary not impossible? Because impossibility is the negation of existence; necessity implies existence, so it cannot be impossible.
Even contingent existence (specific possible) is not impossible, so it also qualifies.
4
Address the misconception about the first necessary
Some might think that calling a proposition 'possible in the general sense' would require ignorance of its predicate's modal relation to the subject, thus excluding the first necessary (al-ḍarūrī al-awwalī). The author refutes this: 'general possible' only notes non-impossibility, not a denial of necessity. The first necessary (e.g., 'God exists') is not impossible, so it qualifies.
The first necessary is a proposition whose truth is immediate and necessary; yet it is not impossible, so it falls under the general possible.
5
Confirm the author's correction
The author concludes that the term 'general possible' applies to the first necessary and the specific possible without conflict. Understand that this is a terminological clarification, not a metaphysical claim that necessity and contingency are the same.
This step ensures you can correctly classify modal propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بالمعنى العامي فهو ما لا يمتنع وجوده وهو أعم من الممكن بالمعنى الخاصي. ومن الواجب سواء كان وجوبه لذاته أو لغيره، إذ لا امتناع لوجودهما، وليس إطلاق ذلك على الضرورة أو الإمكان مستدعياً للجهل بحال محمول القضية في النسبة الإيجابية إلى الموضوع حتى إنه يخرج عنه الضروري الأولي كما ظن بعضهم، بل إطلاقه على ما أطلق عليه إنما هو باعتبار أنه ليس بممتنع، وذلك مما لا يخالف فيه الضروري الأولي، لما ليس بأوّلي ولا للممكن بالمعنى الخاصي؛
And as for [the possible] in the general sense, it is that whose existence is not impossible. It is more general than the possible in the specific sense and includes the necessary, whether its necessity is in itself or from another, since there is no impossibility for their existence. And applying that to necessity or possibility does not require ignorance of the state of the predicate of the proposition in the affirmative relation to the subject, such that it would exclude the first necessary as some have thought. Rather, its application to what it is applied to is only in consideration that it is not impossible, and that is something concerning which the first necessary does not differ, nor does the possible in the specific sense.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage defines the possible in the general sense (al-mumkin bi-al-maʿnā al-ʿāmmī). The author defines it simply as 'that whose existence is not impossible' (mā lā yamtaniʿu wujūduhu). This definition is broader than the specific possible because it includes both the possible in specific sense (which is neither necessary nor impossible) and the necessary (which cannot be impossible). The necessary, whether per se or per aliud, has existence that is not impossible, so it falls under general possibility. The author then addresses a potential confusion: some might think that calling a proposition 'possible' in a general sense would make one ignorant of the actual modal status of the predicate relative to the subject. Specifically, it might be thought that the first necessary (al-ḍarūrī al-awwalī) would be excluded from this general possibility. The author refutes this by clarifying that the term 'general possible' is applied only on the basis of non-impossibility, not as a denial of necessity. Thus, the first necessary (like 'God exists') is not impossible, so it qualifies. This does not contradict the fact that it is necessary. Similarly, the specific possible also qualifies. So the general sense encompasses both necessary and specific possible without contradiction. The author thus clarifies the scope and corrects a likely misinterpretation.

الممكن بالمعنى العامي
الضروري الأولي

Key Takeaway
The possible in the general sense is defined solely by non-impossibility, encompassing both necessary and contingent existents without contradiction.

The Three Modalities: Necessary, Impossible, and Possible (Special Sense)
Defines the three modalities and elaborates on the possible in the special sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three modal categories (wajib, mumtani', mumkin bi al-ma'na al-khassi) and classify propositions accordingly.

Core Idea
The logical possible is the middle ground between self-necessity and self-impossibility.

In this lesson, we begin with the fundamental modal division of propositions: necessary, impossible, and possible. The author defines each and focuses on the possible in the special sense (al-mumkin bi al-ma'na al-khassi), which is the core logical notion of contingency. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating modal arguments in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories in the Arabic text
Locate where the author defines wajib, mumtani', and mumkin. Look for the phrases 'ضروري الوجود' and 'ضروري العدم' and 'ما انتفت عنه الضرورة'.
The author presents them as a threefold division after defining the special possible.
2
Extract the definition of the possible (special sense)
The key phrase: 'ما لا ضرورة في وجوده ولا في عدمه لذاته'. Note that it applies to both existence and non-existence equally, and the condition that assuming either does not lead to impossibility.
This is the core logical contingency.
3
Understand the condition of no impossibility
The author adds 'بل لو فرض موجوداً أو معدوماً لم يلزم عنه لذاته محال'. This distinguishes the possible from the impossible (which leads to impossibility) and the necessary (which does not allow the opposite).
4
Apply the definition to examples
The author gives examples: perpetual (blackness of crow), majority (clear sky), minority (rain in summer), equal (rain in winter). Classify these as possible in the special sense. Note that even if something is always or usually the case, it is still possible if it is not necessary in itself.
Constant properties are included as long as they are not self-necessary.

وأما الممكن بالمعنى الخاصي فهو ما لا ضرورة في وجوده ولا في عدمه لذاته، بل لو فرض موجوداً أو معدوماً لم يلزم عنه لذاته محال، وسواء كان دائم الوجود أو العدم أو غير دائم بل أكثري أو أقلي أو مساو، فالدائم الوجود كالسواد للغراب، والدائم السلب كالسواد للفقنس، والأكثري أي ما وجوده أكثر من عدمه كصحو الجو، والأقلي أي ما عدمه أكثر من وجوده كالمطر في الصيف، والمساوي كالمطر الشتاء لا مطلقاً، بل بالنسبة إلى بعض البلاد، فالقسمة على هذا ثلاثية واجب أي ضروري الوجود وممتنع أي ضروري العدم وممكن وهو ما انتفت عنه الضرورة في وجوده وعدمه فهو مباين للواجب والممتنع، ولا دخول لواحد تحت الآخر لكنه وإن كان مبايناً للضرورة المطلقة فأعمّ من المشروط بكل اعتبار. وأما بالمعنى العامي فهو ما لا يمتنع وجوده وهو أعم من الممكن بالمعنى الخاصي ومن الواجب سواء كان وجوبه لذاته أو لغيره، إذ لا امتناع لوجودهما، وليس إطلاق ذلك على الضرورة أو الإمكان مستدعياً للجهل بحال محمول القضية في النسبة الإيجابية إلى الموضوع حتى إنه يخرج عنه الضروري الأولي كما ظن بعضهم، بل إطلاقه على ما أطلق عليه إنما هو باعتبار أنه ليس بممتنع، وذلك مما لا يخالف فيه الضروري الأولي، لما ليس بأوّلي ولا للممكن بالمعنى الخاصي؛ ولذلك سمي الإمكان العام أي لعمومه، وربما سمي بذلك لاصطلاح العامة عليه، فالقسمة إذ ذاك ثنائية، ممكن وممتنع، وقد يطلق الممكن بمعنى أخص من هذين المعنيين وهو ما انتفت عنه الضرورة المطلقة والوقتية، فيخرج عنه ثبوت الحياة الله تعالى والحيوان للإنسان والتنفس للحيوان والخسوف للقمر، ويدخل فيه ما ذلك كقعود زيد وقيامه ومشيته، فإنه ليس ضرورياً أن يكون له، ولا له ما يوجب سوقه إليه، بخلاف الضروري المطلق والوقتي أي المشروط بالوقت فهو أخص من الممكن بالمعنى الخاصيّ؛ لخروج الضرورة الوقتية عنه ودخولها تحت الخاصي، وهو أيضاً أخص من الممكن بالمعنى العامي؛ لخروج الضرورتين عنه ودخولهما تحت العامي. وربما أطلق الممكن بمعنى غير المعاني الثلاثة، وهو كل ما كان في مستقبل الحال، لا مستقبل آن مخصوص بل مطلقاً، لا ضرورة في سلبه وإيجابه بكلا الاعتبارين - أعني الضرورة المطلقة والمشروطة - وسواء كان في الحال موجوداً أم لا، وإن كان موجوداً فسواء كان ضرورياً له الوجود أو اتفاقياً فهو أخص من الممكن العامي والخاصي والأخصي؛ لصدقها عليه وعلى ما لا ضرورة في وجوده ولا في عدمه لذاته في الحال.
As for the possible in the special sense, it is that which has no necessity in its existence nor in its non-existence in itself. Rather, if it were assumed existent or non-existent, no impossibility would follow from it in itself. It may be perpetually existent or perpetually non-existent or not perpetual but rather majority, minority, or equal. Perpetually existent like blackness for the crow, perpetually absent like blackness for the phoenix, majority i.e. its existence is more than its non-existence like clear sky, minority i.e. its non-existence is more than its existence like rain in summer, equal like rain in winter, not absolutely but relative to some regions. Thus the division is threefold: necessary i.e. necessarily existent, impossible i.e. necessarily non-existent, and possible i.e. that which has no necessity in its existence or non-existence. So it is distinct from the necessary and the impossible, and none falls under the other. But although it is distinct from absolute necessity, it is more general than the conditional in every respect. And as for the general sense, it is that whose existence is not impossible, and it is more general than the possible in the special sense and than the necessary, whether its necessity is in itself or through another, since there is no impossibility in their existence. And applying this to necessity or possibility does not require ignorance of the state of the predicate of the proposition in the affirmative relation to the subject, such that even the primary necessary would be excluded, as some thought. Rather, its application to what it is applied to is only insofar as it is not impossible, and that is something that does not conflict with the primary necessary, nor with the possible in the special sense. Therefore it is called general possibility due to its generality, and perhaps it is so named due to the common usage of it. Hence the division then is binary: possible and impossible. And the possible is sometimes used in a sense more specific than these two meanings, which is that from which absolute and temporal necessity are negated. Accordingly, the existence of life for Allah, of animal for man, of respiration for animal, and of eclipse for the moon are excluded. And what enters into it is like Zayd's sitting, standing, and walking, for it is not necessary for him to have them, nor does he have anything that obliges him to it, contrary to the absolute and temporal necessary i.e. conditional upon time. So it is more specific than the possible in the special sense due to the exclusion of temporal necessity from it and its inclusion under the special, and it is also more specific than the possible in the general sense due to the exclusion of the two necessities from it and their inclusion under the general. And perhaps the possible is used in a meaning other than the three, which is everything that is in the future, not a specific future moment but absolutely, with no necessity in its negation or affirmation in both respects – I mean absolute and conditional necessity – whether it exists in the present or not, and if it exists, whether its existence is necessary for it or accidental. So it is more specific than the general, special, and more-specific possible due to their applying to it and to that which has no necessity in its existence or non-existence in itself in the present.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage defines the three modal categories: necessary (wajib), impossible (mumtani'), and possible (mumkin), and then elaborates on the different senses of 'possible'. The author begins with the 'special sense' (al-mumkin bi al-ma'na al-khassi), which is the logical possible: that which is not necessary in itself either to exist or not exist. This includes things that are always or usually existent, such as blackness for a crow (always) or clear sky (mostly), as well as lesser probabilities. The division here is threefold: necessary, impossible, and possible. The necessary and impossible are mutually exclusive with the possible. However, the possible in this sense is broader than the merely conditional necessity (like 'if the subject exists, then the predicate is necessary'). Next, the author introduces the 'general sense' (al-mumkin bi al-ma'na al-'ammi): whatever is not impossible. This is broader because it includes the necessary (since the necessary is not impossible) and the possible in the special sense. This leads to a binary division: possible (including necessary) vs. impossible. The author then presents a third, more specific sense (al-akhass): that which is free from both absolute and temporal necessity. For example, Allah's existence is absolutely necessary, animal for man is temporally necessary (necessary as long as man exists), but Zayd's sitting and standing are contingent. Finally, a fourth sense is mentioned: the future contingent, which applies to events that may or may not occur, regardless of present status. Each sense is defined and contrasted with examples. The author emphasizes that possibility does not imply ignorance but is a real modal property. Throughout, the use of examples from natural phenomena (rain, clear sky) and everyday actions (Zayd's sitting) helps clarify the abstract distinctions. The lesson is crucial for understanding the modal foundations of propositions in logic and their application in arguments.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the possible in the special sense (al-mumkin bi al-ma'na al-khassi)?
A) The existence of Allah
B) The non-existence of a partner for Allah
C) The clear sky (which usually occurs)
D) The eclipse of the moon (which is temporally necessary)

Key Takeaway
The possible in the special sense is defined by the absence of self-necessity in both existence and non-existence, and it includes constant, majority, minority, and equal cases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following as wajib, mumtani', or mumkin bi al-ma'na al-khassi: (a) Allah's existence, (b) The existence of another god alongside Allah, (c) The occurrence of rain tomorrow in a specific region, (d) The blackness of a crow (as a constant property). Explain your reasoning using the definition.

The Possible in the General Sense and the Binary Division
Defines the general possible and presents a binary division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the general possible (al-mumkin bi al-ma'na al-'ammi) and explain why it includes the necessary, leading to a binary division of propositions.

Core Idea
The general possible is whatever is not impossible, thus including the necessary.

While the special possible excludes the necessary and impossible, the general possible (al-mumkin bi al-ma'na al-'ammi) takes a broader scope: any proposition whose existence is not impossible. This includes the necessary (both self-necessary and caused-necessary) and the special possible. Consequently, the division becomes binary: possible vs. impossible. This sense is often used in dialectical contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of the general possible
Look for the phrase 'ما لا يمتنع وجوده' (that whose existence is not impossible). Contrast this with the special possible definition.
The general possible is defined purely negatively.
2
Understand why it includes the necessary
The necessary (wajib) - whether in itself (Allah) or through another - is not impossible. So it falls under the general possible. Similarly, the special possible is not impossible.
The author explicitly says it is more general than the special possible and the necessary.
3
Compare the binary division with the earlier threefold division
In the binary division, the term 'possible' now encompasses both the necessary and the special possible. The author states: 'فالقسمة إذ ذاك ثنائية، ممكن وممتنع'.
This is a different sense of 'possible', not conflicting with the earlier division.

وأما الممكن بالمعنى الخاصي فهو ما لا ضرورة في وجوده ولا في عدمه لذاته، بل لو فرض موجوداً أو معدوماً لم يلزم عنه لذاته محال، وسواء كان دائم الوجود أو العدم أو غير دائم بل أكثري أو أقلي أو مساو، فالدائم الوجود كالسواد للغراب، والدائم السلب كالسواد للفقنس، والأكثري أي ما وجوده أكثر من عدمه كصحو الجو، والأقلي أي ما عدمه أكثر من وجوده كالمطر في الصيف، والمساوي كالمطر الشتاء لا مطلقاً، بل بالنسبة إلى بعض البلاد، فالقسمة على هذا ثلاثية واجب أي ضروري الوجود وممتنع أي ضروري العدم وممكن وهو ما انتفت عنه الضرورة في وجوده وعدمه فهو مباين للواجب والممتنع، ولا دخول لواحد تحت الآخر لكنه وإن كان مبايناً للضرورة المطلقة فأعمّ من المشروط بكل اعتبار. وأما بالمعنى العامي فهو ما لا يمتنع وجوده وهو أعم من الممكن بالمعنى الخاصي ومن الواجب سواء كان وجوبه لذاته أو لغيره، إذ لا امتناع لوجودهما، وليس إطلاق ذلك على الضرورة أو الإمكان مستدعياً للجهل بحال محمول القضية في النسبة الإيجابية إلى الموضوع حتى إنه يخرج عنه الضروري الأولي كما ظن بعضهم، بل إطلاقه على ما أطلق عليه إنما هو باعتبار أنه ليس بممتنع، وذلك مما لا يخالف فيه الضروري الأولي، لما ليس بأوّلي ولا للممكن بالمعنى الخاصي؛ ولذلك سمي الإمكان العام أي لعمومه، وربما سمي بذلك لاصطلاح العامة عليه، فالقسمة إذ ذاك ثنائية، ممكن وممتنع، وقد يطلق الممكن بمعنى أخص من هذين المعنيين وهو ما انتفت عنه الضرورة المطلقة والوقتية، فيخرج عنه ثبوت الحياة الله تعالى والحيوان للإنسان والتنفس للحيوان والخسوف للقمر، ويدخل فيه ما ذلك كقعود زيد وقيامه ومشيته، فإنه ليس ضرورياً أن يكون له، ولا له ما يوجب سوقه إليه، بخلاف الضروري المطلق والوقتي أي المشروط بالوقت فهو أخص من الممكن بالمعنى الخاصيّ؛ لخروج الضرورة الوقتية عنه ودخولها تحت الخاصي، وهو أيضاً أخص من الممكن بالمعنى العامي؛ لخروج الضرورتين عنه ودخولهما تحت العامي. وربما أطلق الممكن بمعنى غير المعاني الثلاثة، وهو كل ما كان في مستقبل الحال، لا مستقبل آن مخصوص بل مطلقاً، لا ضرورة في سلبه وإيجابه بكلا الاعتبارين - أعني الضرورة المطلقة والمشروطة - وسواء كان في الحال موجوداً أم لا، وإن كان موجوداً فسواء كان ضرورياً له الوجود أو اتفاقياً فهو أخص من الممكن العامي والخاصي والأخصي؛ لصدقها عليه وعلى ما لا ضرورة في وجوده ولا في عدمه لذاته في الحال.
As for the possible in the special sense, it is that which has no necessity in its existence nor in its non-existence in itself. Rather, if it were assumed existent or non-existent, no impossibility would follow from it in itself. It may be perpetually existent or perpetually non-existent or not perpetual but rather majority, minority, or equal. Perpetually existent like blackness for the crow, perpetually absent like blackness for the phoenix, majority i.e. its existence is more than its non-existence like clear sky, minority i.e. its non-existence is more than its existence like rain in summer, equal like rain in winter, not absolutely but relative to some regions. Thus the division is threefold: necessary i.e. necessarily existent, impossible i.e. necessarily non-existent, and possible i.e. that which has no necessity in its existence or non-existence. So it is distinct from the necessary and the impossible, and none falls under the other. But although it is distinct from absolute necessity, it is more general than the conditional in every respect. And as for the general sense, it is that whose existence is not impossible, and it is more general than the possible in the special sense and than the necessary, whether its necessity is in itself or through another, since there is no impossibility in their existence. And applying this to necessity or possibility does not require ignorance of the state of the predicate of the proposition in the affirmative relation to the subject, such that even the primary necessary would be excluded, as some thought. Rather, its application to what it is applied to is only insofar as it is not impossible, and that is something that does not conflict with the primary necessary, nor with the possible in the special sense. Therefore it is called general possibility due to its generality, and perhaps it is so named due to the common usage of it. Hence the division then is binary: possible and impossible. And the possible is sometimes used in a sense more specific than these two meanings, which is that from which absolute and temporal necessity are negated. Accordingly, the existence of life for Allah, of animal for man, of respiration for animal, and of eclipse for the moon are excluded. And what enters into it is like Zayd's sitting, standing, and walking, for it is not necessary for him to have them, nor does he have anything that obliges him to it, contrary to the absolute and temporal necessary i.e. conditional upon time. So it is more specific than the possible in the special sense due to the exclusion of temporal necessity from it and its inclusion under the special, and it is also more specific than the possible in the general sense due to the exclusion of the two necessities from it and their inclusion under the general. And perhaps the possible is used in a meaning other than the three, which is everything that is in the future, not a specific future moment but absolutely, with no necessity in its negation or affirmation in both respects – I mean absolute and conditional necessity – whether it exists in the present or not, and if it exists, whether its existence is necessary for it or accidental. So it is more specific than the general, special, and more-specific possible due to their applying to it and to that which has no necessity in its existence or non-existence in itself in the present.

Commentary
This passage defines the three modal categories: necessary (wajib), impossible (mumtani'), and possible (mumkin), and then elaborates on the different senses of 'possible'. The author begins with the 'special sense' (al-mumkin bi al-ma'na al-khassi), which is the logical possible: that which is not necessary in itself either to exist or not exist. This includes things that are always or usually existent, such as blackness for a crow (always) or clear sky (mostly), as well as lesser probabilities. The division here is threefold: necessary, impossible, and possible. The necessary and impossible are mutually exclusive with the possible. However, the possible in this sense is broader than the merely conditional necessity (like 'if the subject exists, then the predicate is necessary'). Next, the author introduces the 'general sense' (al-mumkin bi al-ma'na al-'ammi): whatever is not impossible. This is broader because it includes the necessary (since the necessary is not impossible) and the possible in the special sense. This leads to a binary division: possible (including necessary) vs. impossible. The author then presents a third, more specific sense (al-akhass): that which is free from both absolute and temporal necessity. For example, Allah's existence is absolutely necessary, animal for man is temporally necessary (necessary as long as man exists), but Zayd's sitting and standing are contingent. Finally, a fourth sense is mentioned: the future contingent, which applies to events that may or may not occur, regardless of present status. Each sense is defined and contrasted with examples. The author emphasizes that possibility does not imply ignorance but is a real modal property. Throughout, the use of examples from natural phenomena (rain, clear sky) and everyday actions (Zayd's sitting) helps clarify the abstract distinctions. The lesson is crucial for understanding the modal foundations of propositions in logic and their application in arguments.

Key Takeaway

The More Specific Possible and the Future Contingent
Defines two additional senses of the possible: the more specific and the future contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two more restrictive senses of the possible: al-mumkin al-akhass (free from absolute and temporal necessity) and the future contingent (al-mustaqbal).

Core Idea
The more specific possible excludes all necessity, even temporal, leaving only genuine contingency.

After the special and general senses, the author introduces two more restrictive meanings. The 'more specific' possible (al-mumkin al-akhass) excludes both absolute and temporal necessity, thus removing propositions that are necessary as long as the subject exists (like animal for man). The future contingent sense applies to future events with no necessity in either direction, regardless of present status. These finer distinctions are important for precise modal analysis.

Guided Path
1
Define the more specific possible (al-akhass)
Find the phrase: 'ما انتفت عنه الضرورة المطلقة والوقتية'. Absolute necessity is self-necessity (like Allah's existence), and temporal necessity is necessity conditional on time (like the eclipse for the moon during its cycle).
This sense is more restrictive than the special possible.
2
Identify what is excluded and included
Excluded: existence of life for Allah (absolute), man's animality (temporal), respiration for animal (temporal), eclipse of moon (temporal). Included: Zayd's sitting, standing, walking – they are contingent.
The examples are explicit in the text.
3
Define the future contingent sense
The author says: 'كل ما كان في مستقبل الحال ... لا ضرورة في سلبه وإيجابه بكلا الاعتبارين'. This applies to future events that are not determined, even if they currently exist.
This is the most specific sense.
4
Compare the four senses in terms of generality
The future contingent is the most specific, then the more specific, then the special, then the general. Understand the hierarchy from the author's descriptions.
The author indicates each sense is 'أخص' (more specific) than the previous.

وأما الممكن بالمعنى الخاصي فهو ما لا ضرورة في وجوده ولا في عدمه لذاته، بل لو فرض موجوداً أو معدوماً لم يلزم عنه لذاته محال، وسواء كان دائم الوجود أو العدم أو غير دائم بل أكثري أو أقلي أو مساو، فالدائم الوجود كالسواد للغراب، والدائم السلب كالسواد للفقنس، والأكثري أي ما وجوده أكثر من عدمه كصحو الجو، والأقلي أي ما عدمه أكثر من وجوده كالمطر في الصيف، والمساوي كالمطر الشتاء لا مطلقاً، بل بالنسبة إلى بعض البلاد، فالقسمة على هذا ثلاثية واجب أي ضروري الوجود وممتنع أي ضروري العدم وممكن وهو ما انتفت عنه الضرورة في وجوده وعدمه فهو مباين للواجب والممتنع، ولا دخول لواحد تحت الآخر لكنه وإن كان مبايناً للضرورة المطلقة فأعمّ من المشروط بكل اعتبار. وأما بالمعنى العامي فهو ما لا يمتنع وجوده وهو أعم من الممكن بالمعنى الخاصي ومن الواجب سواء كان وجوبه لذاته أو لغيره، إذ لا امتناع لوجودهما، وليس إطلاق ذلك على الضرورة أو الإمكان مستدعياً للجهل بحال محمول القضية في النسبة الإيجابية إلى الموضوع حتى إنه يخرج عنه الضروري الأولي كما ظن بعضهم، بل إطلاقه على ما أطلق عليه إنما هو باعتبار أنه ليس بممتنع، وذلك مما لا يخالف فيه الضروري الأولي، لما ليس بأوّلي ولا للممكن بالمعنى الخاصي؛ ولذلك سمي الإمكان العام أي لعمومه، وربما سمي بذلك لاصطلاح العامة عليه، فالقسمة إذ ذاك ثنائية، ممكن وممتنع، وقد يطلق الممكن بمعنى أخص من هذين المعنيين وهو ما انتفت عنه الضرورة المطلقة والوقتية، فيخرج عنه ثبوت الحياة الله تعالى والحيوان للإنسان والتنفس للحيوان والخسوف للقمر، ويدخل فيه ما ذلك كقعود زيد وقيامه ومشيته، فإنه ليس ضرورياً أن يكون له، ولا له ما يوجب سوقه إليه، بخلاف الضروري المطلق والوقتي أي المشروط بالوقت فهو أخص من الممكن بالمعنى الخاصيّ؛ لخروج الضرورة الوقتية عنه ودخولها تحت الخاصي، وهو أيضاً أخص من الممكن بالمعنى العامي؛ لخروج الضرورتين عنه ودخولهما تحت العامي. وربما أطلق الممكن بمعنى غير المعاني الثلاثة، وهو كل ما كان في مستقبل الحال، لا مستقبل آن مخصوص بل مطلقاً، لا ضرورة في سلبه وإيجابه بكلا الاعتبارين - أعني الضرورة المطلقة والمشروطة - وسواء كان في الحال موجوداً أم لا، وإن كان موجوداً فسواء كان ضرورياً له الوجود أو اتفاقياً فهو أخص من الممكن العامي والخاصي والأخصي؛ لصدقها عليه وعلى ما لا ضرورة في وجوده ولا في عدمه لذاته في الحال.
As for the possible in the special sense, it is that which has no necessity in its existence nor in its non-existence in itself. Rather, if it were assumed existent or non-existent, no impossibility would follow from it in itself. It may be perpetually existent or perpetually non-existent or not perpetual but rather majority, minority, or equal. Perpetually existent like blackness for the crow, perpetually absent like blackness for the phoenix, majority i.e. its existence is more than its non-existence like clear sky, minority i.e. its non-existence is more than its existence like rain in summer, equal like rain in winter, not absolutely but relative to some regions. Thus the division is threefold: necessary i.e. necessarily existent, impossible i.e. necessarily non-existent, and possible i.e. that which has no necessity in its existence or non-existence. So it is distinct from the necessary and the impossible, and none falls under the other. But although it is distinct from absolute necessity, it is more general than the conditional in every respect. And as for the general sense, it is that whose existence is not impossible, and it is more general than the possible in the special sense and than the necessary, whether its necessity is in itself or through another, since there is no impossibility in their existence. And applying this to necessity or possibility does not require ignorance of the state of the predicate of the proposition in the affirmative relation to the subject, such that even the primary necessary would be excluded, as some thought. Rather, its application to what it is applied to is only insofar as it is not impossible, and that is something that does not conflict with the primary necessary, nor with the possible in the special sense. Therefore it is called general possibility due to its generality, and perhaps it is so named due to the common usage of it. Hence the division then is binary: possible and impossible. And the possible is sometimes used in a sense more specific than these two meanings, which is that from which absolute and temporal necessity are negated. Accordingly, the existence of life for Allah, of animal for man, of respiration for animal, and of eclipse for the moon are excluded. And what enters into it is like Zayd's sitting, standing, and walking, for it is not necessary for him to have them, nor does he have anything that obliges him to it, contrary to the absolute and temporal necessary i.e. conditional upon time. So it is more specific than the possible in the special sense due to the exclusion of temporal necessity from it and its inclusion under the special, and it is also more specific than the possible in the general sense due to the exclusion of the two necessities from it and their inclusion under the general. And perhaps the possible is used in a meaning other than the three, which is everything that is in the future, not a specific future moment but absolutely, with no necessity in its negation or affirmation in both respects – I mean absolute and conditional necessity – whether it exists in the present or not, and if it exists, whether its existence is necessary for it or accidental. So it is more specific than the general, special, and more-specific possible due to their applying to it and to that which has no necessity in its existence or non-existence in itself in the present.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage defines the three modal categories: necessary (wajib), impossible (mumtani'), and possible (mumkin), and then elaborates on the different senses of 'possible'. The author begins with the 'special sense' (al-mumkin bi al-ma'na al-khassi), which is the logical possible: that which is not necessary in itself either to exist or not exist. This includes things that are always or usually existent, such as blackness for a crow (always) or clear sky (mostly), as well as lesser probabilities. The division here is threefold: necessary, impossible, and possible. The necessary and impossible are mutually exclusive with the possible. However, the possible in this sense is broader than the merely conditional necessity (like 'if the subject exists, then the predicate is necessary'). Next, the author introduces the 'general sense' (al-mumkin bi al-ma'na al-'ammi): whatever is not impossible. This is broader because it includes the necessary (since the necessary is not impossible) and the possible in the special sense. This leads to a binary division: possible (including necessary) vs. impossible. The author then presents a third, more specific sense (al-akhass): that which is free from both absolute and temporal necessity. For example, Allah's existence is absolutely necessary, animal for man is temporally necessary (necessary as long as man exists), but Zayd's sitting and standing are contingent. Finally, a fourth sense is mentioned: the future contingent, which applies to events that may or may not occur, regardless of present status. Each sense is defined and contrasted with examples. The author emphasizes that possibility does not imply ignorance but is a real modal property. Throughout, the use of examples from natural phenomena (rain, clear sky) and everyday actions (Zayd's sitting) helps clarify the abstract distinctions. The lesson is crucial for understanding the modal foundations of propositions in logic and their application in arguments.

Key Terms
الممكن الأخص
المستقبل الحال
الضرورة الوقتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the more specific possible (al-mumkin al-akhass)?
A) The existence of life for Allah
B) The existence of animality for man
C) Zayd's sitting down
D) The eclipse of the moon

Key Takeaway
The more specific possible (al-akhass) excludes all necessity, absolute and temporal, leaving only genuine contingency, while the future contingent further restricts to events that are undetermined in the future.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Determine whether each of the following falls under the more specific possible (al-akhass) or not: (a) The existence of the throne of Allah (al-arsh), (b) The motion of a falling stone when released, (c) Your decision to read this lesson. Provide reasons.

General Possibility (al-Imkān al-ʿĀmm)
Author defines the most general meaning of possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general meaning of 'possible' (al-mumkin al-ʿāmm) and apply the binary division of possible and impossible.

Core Idea
General possibility is simply the negation of impossibility; it is the broadest sense used in logic.

The first and most basic meaning of 'possible' is the general possibility (al-imkān al-ʿāmm). According to the author, this sense yields a binary division of all propositions into possible and impossible. Anything that is not impossible is possible in this sense. This foundational meaning is essential for understanding modal logic in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Define general possibility
General possibility is the negation of impossibility. That is, what is not impossible is possible in this sense.
2
Determine the division
This meaning results in a binary classification: every proposition is either possible or impossible.
There is no third category.
3
Apply to examples
Consider any proposition: if it is not impossible, it is possible in the general sense. For example, 'Zayd is standing' is possible because it is not impossible.
This includes many things that might be necessary in other senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دقائق الحقائق: الجزء الأول / المقالة الثالثة: في بارمنياس ولذلك سمي الإمكان العام أي لعمومه، وربما سمي بذلك لاصطلاح العامة عليه، فالقسمة إذ ذاك ثنائية، ممكن وممتنع، وقد يطلق الممكن بمعنى أخص من هذين المعنيين وهو ما انتفت عنه الضرورة المطلقة والوقتية، فيخرج عنه ثبوت الحياة الله تعالى والحيوان للإنسان والتنفس للحيوان والخسوف للقمر، ويدخل فيه ما ذلك كقعود زيد وقيامه ومشيته، فإنه ليس ضرورياً أن يكون له، ولا له ما يوجب سوقه إليه، بخلاف الضروري المطلق والقتي أي المشروط بالقوت فهو أخص من الممكن بالمعنى الخاصيّ؛ لخروج الضرورة الوقتية عنه ودخولها تحت الخاصي، وهو أيضاً أخص من الممكن بالمعنى العامي؛ لخروج الضرورتين عنه ودخولهما تحت العامي. سوی وربما أطلق الممكن بمعنى غير المعاني الثلاثة، وهو كل ما كان في مستقبل الحال، لا مستقبل آن مخصوص بل مطلقاً، لا ضرورة في سلبه وإيجابه بكلا الاعتبارين - أعني الضرورة المطلقة والمشروطة - وسواء كان في الحال موجوداً أم لا، وإن كان موجوداً فسواء كان ضرورياً له الوجود أو اتفاقياً فهو أخص من الممكن العامي والخاصي والأخصي؛ لصدقها عليه وعلى ما لا ضرورة في وجوده ولا في عدمه لذاته في الحال. وربما استعمل لفظ الإمكان بمعنى الاحتمال على ما جهل منه حال المحمول نفس الأمر ولا يكون ذلك بحسب الأمر في ذاته، بل بحسب رأينا إلى حين ظهور دليل الحال في نفسه، وذلك كالحكم على ما نراه من الأشباح المتباعدة أنه ممكن أن يكون حيواناً أي يحتمل، وإلا ففي نفس الأمر إن كان جماداً فضروري ألا يكون حيواناً، ومن ظن أن ألا وجود شرط ممكن الوجود وإلا فهو ضروري الوجود فقد ظن خطأ، فإن ما هو ممكن أن يكون هو بعينه ممكن ألا يكون فإن كان لا كون شرط ممكن الكون لضرورة أن الكون يخرج إلى ضرورة الكون، فالكون شرط ممكن ألا كون، وإلا فلا كون يخرجه إلى ضرورة لا كون، ويلزم منه أن يكون الكون ولا كون في شيء واحد معاً وهو ممتنع أو كان لا كون لا يخرجه إلى ضرورة لا كون فالكون لا يخرجه إلى ضرورة الكون.
Daqaiq al-Haqaiq: Part One / Article Three: On Parmenides. Therefore it was called general possibility because of its generality, and perhaps it was called that due to conventional usage, so the division then is binary: possible and impossible. And 'possible' may be used in a sense more specific than these two meanings, which is that from which absolute and temporal necessity are negated; thus the existence of life for God, and animal for human, and respiration for animal, and eclipse for the moon are excluded, and included is that like Zayd's sitting, standing, and walking, for it is not necessary for him nor does he have something that necessitates his being in that state, unlike the absolute necessary and the conditioned necessary (i.e., conditioned by time). So it is more specific than the possible in the special sense because temporal necessity is excluded from it and included under the special, and it is also more specific than the possible in the general sense because both necessities are excluded from it and included under the general. Another: And sometimes 'possible' is used in a meaning other than the three meanings, which is everything that is in the future, not a specific future moment but absolutely, with no necessity in its negation or affirmation in both respects — meaning absolute and conditioned necessity — regardless of whether it exists in the present or not, and if it exists then whether its existence is necessary or accidental. It is more specific than the general, special, and more special possible because these apply to it and to that which has no necessity in its existence or non-existence in itself in the present. And sometimes the word 'possibility' is used in the sense of probability regarding that whose state of the predicate in reality is unknown, and that is not according to the matter itself, but according to our opinion until evidence of the matter in itself appears, such as judging what we see of distant shapes as possibly being an animal — i.e., it is probable — otherwise, in reality if it is inanimate, it is necessarily not an animal. And whoever thinks that non-existence is a condition for possible existence, otherwise it is necessary existence, has thought erroneously. For what is possible to be is exactly what is possible not to be. If non-existence is a condition for possible existence because of the necessity that existence leads to the necessity of existence, then existence is a condition for possible non-existence, otherwise non-existence does not lead to the necessity of non-existence, and it would follow that existence and non-existence are in one thing together, which is impossible; or if non-existence does not lead to the necessity of non-existence, then existence does not lead to the necessity of existence.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage defines the various meanings of 'possible' (mumkin) as used in logic, starting from the most general to the most specific. The author begins by stating that general possibility (al-imkān al-ʿāmm) is called thus due to its generality, and it yields a binary division into possible and impossible. He then gives a more specific meaning (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ) which negates both absolute and temporal necessity, excluding states like life for God (which is necessary) and including contingent actions like Zayd's sitting. This special possible is narrower than the general possible because it excludes temporally necessary things. Next, he presents an even more specific meaning (al-imkān al-akhaṣṣ) which applies to future events with no necessity at all, including present accidental existents. Finally, he discusses a fourth usage equating possibility with epistemic probability (iḥtimāl), where we judge something as possible based on ignorance of its true nature. The passage ends with a critical note on a mistaken view about the condition of non-existence for possibility, showing the logical implications lead to contradiction.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
General possibility is the widest sense: everything not impossible is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Special Possible (al-Mumkin al-Khāṣṣī)
Author defines the special possible, excluding absolute and temporal necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the special possible (al-mumkin al-khāṣṣī) as that which negates both absolute and temporal necessity, and distinguish it from the general possible.

Core Idea
Special possible excludes everything that is necessary at all times or at a specific time.

The second meaning of 'possible' is more specific than the general one. The author defines it as that from which both absolute necessity (al-ḍarūra al-muṭlaqa) and temporal necessity (al-ḍarūra al-waqtiyya) are negated. This excludes things that are always necessary (like God's existence) and things that are necessary at a specific time (like animal respiration). It includes contingent actions such as Zayd's sitting or standing. Understanding this sense is key for discussions of contingency in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Define special possible
Special possible is that which is not absolutely necessary and not temporally necessary.
2
Identify what is excluded
Excluded are: the existence of life for God (absolute necessity), the animal for humans (temporal necessity?), respiration for animals (temporal), and the eclipse of the moon (temporal).
The passage lists these as examples.
3
Identify what is included
Included are contingent actions like Zayd's sitting, standing, and walking, which are not necessary for him at any time.
4
Compare with general possible
Special possible is narrower because it excludes temporally necessary things that are still possible in the general sense.
For example, the eclipse is possible generally but not special-possible.

Daqaiq al-Haqaiq: Part One / Article Three: On Parmenides. Therefore it was called general possibility because of its generality, and perhaps it was called that due to conventional usage, so the division then is binary: possible and impossible. And 'possible' may be used in a sense more specific than these two meanings, which is that from which absolute and temporal necessity are negated; thus the existence of life for God, and animal for human, and respiration for animal, and eclipse for the moon are excluded, and included is that like Zayd's sitting, standing, and walking, for it is not necessary for him nor does he have something that necessitates his being in that state, unlike the absolute necessary and the conditioned necessary (i.e., conditioned by time). Another: And sometimes 'possible' is used in a meaning other than the three meanings, which is everything that is in the future, not a specific future moment but absolutely, with no necessity in its negation or affirmation in both respects — meaning absolute and conditioned necessity — regardless of whether it exists in the present or not, and if it exists then whether its existence is necessary or accidental. And sometimes the word 'possibility' is used in the sense of probability regarding that whose state of the predicate in reality is unknown, and that is not according to the matter itself, but according to our opinion until evidence of the matter in itself appears, such as judging what we see of distant shapes as possibly being an animal — i.e., it is probable — otherwise, in reality if it is inanimate, it is necessarily not an animal. Which of the following does the passage explicitly classify as excluded from the special possible?

B) The existence of life for God
C) The possibility of a future event
D) A distant shape being an animal

Key Takeaway
Special possible excludes anything necessary at all times or at a specific time; it includes only purely contingent actions.

More Special Possible (al-Mumkin al-Akhaṣṣī)
Author introduces the third, most specific meaning of possible for future events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the more special possible (al-mumkin al-akhaṣṣī) as applying to future events with no necessity in either affirmation or negation, and explain its relationship to the other meanings.

Core Idea
More special possible applies to future events regardless of present necessity, making it the narrowest of the three modal senses.

The author introduces a third meaning of 'possible' that is even more specific than the special possible. This is the more special possible (al-mumkin al-akhaṣṣī), which applies to anything that is in the future (not a specific moment) and has no necessity in its affirmation or negation, whether absolute or conditioned. It includes both present accidental existents and future contingencts. Understanding this sense is important for discussions of future events in kalam and logic.

Guided Path
1
Define more special possible
It applies to everything in the future, not a specific future moment, with no necessity in its being or not being, considering both absolute and conditioned necessity.
It does not matter if it exists now or not, or if its present existence is necessary or accidental.
2
Identify its scope
It includes all future contingent events, as well as present accidental things. It excludes only those things that are necessary in the present (but note: this is not due to temporal necessity being included; the more special possible excludes all necessity).
The passage says: 'it is more specific than the general, special, and more special possible because these apply to it and to that which has no necessity in its existence or non-existence in itself in the present.'
3
Compare with special possible
The more special possible is narrower than the special possible because it excludes temporally necessary things that the special possible includes? Actually, the passage says it is more specific than the special possible because temporal necessity is excluded from it and included under the special. Wait, careful: The passage says: 'So it is more specific than the possible in the special sense because temporal necessity is excluded from it and included under the special.' This refers to the special possible? Let's check the Arabic: 'فهو أخص من الممكن بالمعنى الخاصيّ؛ لخروج الضرورة الوقتية عنه ودخولها تحت الخاصي' This line is actually describing the special possible (the one that negates both necessities) being more specific than the general. Then he says: 'وهو أيضاً أخص من الممكن بالمعنى العامي؛ لخروج الضرورتين عنه ودخولهما تحت العامي.' That is still the special possible. Then 'سوی وربما أطلق الممكن بمعنى غير المعاني الثلاثة...' So the more special possible is the third meaning. The passage says: 'فهو أخص من الممكن العامي والخاصي والأخصي؛ لصدقها عليه وعلى ما لا ضرورة في وجوده ولا في عدمه لذاته في الحال.' This means the more special possible is more specific than the general, special, and even the more special? That seems like a typo: it says 'الأخصي' which is itself. Actually, the Arabic says: 'فهو أخص من الممكن العامي والخاصي والأخصي' – that would mean it is more specific than the general, special, and the more special? That can't be. Possibly 'والأخصي' is a mistake, or it refers to another term. Given the context, it likely means it is more specific than the general and special, and also more specific than the previous ones. But we can note the subtlety. For the lesson, we'll present it as the most specific of the three.
The passage has a possible textual issue, but the intended meaning is that this meaning is the most specific.

Source Text

دقائق الحقائق: الجزء الأول / المقالة الثالثة: في بارمنياس ولذلك سمي الإمكان العام أي لعمومه، وربما سمي بذلك لاصطلاح العامة عليه، فالقسمة إذ ذاك ثنائية، ممكن وممتنع، وقد يطلق الممكن بمعنى أخص من هذين المعنيين وهو ما انتفت عنه الضرورة المطلقة والوقتية، فيخرج عنه ثبوت الحياة الله تعالى والحيوان للإنسان والتنفس للحيوان والخسوف للقمر، ويدخل فيه ما ذلك كقعود زيد وقيامه ومشيته، فإنه ليس ضرورياً أن يكون له، ولا له ما يوجب سوقه إليه، بخلاف الضروري المطلق والقتي أي المشروط بالقوت فهو أخص من الممكن بالمعنى الخاصيّ؛ لخروج الضرورة الوقتية عنه ودخولها تحت الخاصي، وهو أيضاً أخص من الممكن بالمعنى العامي؛ لخروج الضرورتين عنه ودخولهما تحت العامي. سوی وربما أطلق الممكن بمعنى غير المعاني الثلاثة، وهو كل ما كان في مستقبل الحال، لا مستقبل آن مخصوص بل مطلقاً، لا ضرورة في سلبه وإيجابه بكلا الاعتبارين - أعني الضرورة المطلقة والمشروطة - وسواء كان في الحال موجوداً أم لا، وإن كان موجوداً فسواء كان ضرورياً له الوجود أو اتفاقياً فهو أخص من الممكن العامي والخاصي والأخصي؛ لصدقها عليه وعلى ما لا ضرورة في وجوده ولا في عدمه لذاته في الحال. وربما استعمل لفظ الإمكان بمعنى الاحتمال على ما جهل منه حال المحمول نفس الأمر ولا يكون ذلك بحسب الأمر في ذاته، بل بحسب رأينا إلى حين ظهور دليل الحال في نفسه، وذلك كالحكم على ما نراه من الأشباح المتباعدة أنه ممكن أن يكون حيواناً أي يحتمل، وإلا ففي نفس الأمر إن كان جماداً فضروري ألا يكون حيواناً، ومن ظن أن ألا وجود شرط ممكن الوجود وإلا فهو ضروري الوجود فقد ظن خطأ، فإن ما هو ممكن أن يكون هو بعينه ممكن ألا يكون فإن كان لا كون شرط ممكن الكون لضرورة أن الكون يخرج إلى ضرورة الكون، فالكون شرط ممكن ألا كون، وإلا فلا كون يخرجه إلى ضرورة لا كون، ويلزم منه أن يكون الكون ولا كون في شيء واحد معاً وهو ممتنع أو كان لا كون لا يخرجه إلى ضرورة لا كون فالكون لا يخرجه إلى ضرورة الكون.
Daqaiq al-Haqaiq: Part One / Article Three: On Parmenides. Therefore it was called general possibility because of its generality, and perhaps it was called that due to conventional usage, so the division then is binary: possible and impossible. And 'possible' may be used in a sense more specific than these two meanings, which is that from which absolute and temporal necessity are negated; thus the existence of life for God, and animal for human, and respiration for animal, and eclipse for the moon are excluded, and included is that like Zayd's sitting, standing, and walking, for it is not necessary for him nor does he have something that necessitates his being in that state, unlike the absolute necessary and the conditioned necessary (i.e., conditioned by time). So it is more specific than the possible in the special sense because temporal necessity is excluded from it and included under the special, and it is also more specific than the possible in the general sense because both necessities are excluded from it and included under the general. Another: And sometimes 'possible' is used in a meaning other than the three meanings, which is everything that is in the future, not a specific future moment but absolutely, with no necessity in its negation or affirmation in both respects — meaning absolute and conditioned necessity — regardless of whether it exists in the present or not, and if it exists then whether its existence is necessary or accidental. It is more specific than the general, special, and more special possible because these apply to it and to that which has no necessity in its existence or non-existence in itself in the present. And sometimes the word 'possibility' is used in the sense of probability regarding that whose state of the predicate in reality is unknown, and that is not according to the matter itself, but according to our opinion until evidence of the matter in itself appears, such as judging what we see of distant shapes as possibly being an animal — i.e., it is probable — otherwise, in reality if it is inanimate, it is necessarily not an animal. And whoever thinks that non-existence is a condition for possible existence, otherwise it is necessary existence, has thought erroneously. For what is possible to be is exactly what is possible not to be. If non-existence is a condition for possible existence because of the necessity that existence leads to the necessity of existence, then existence is a condition for possible non-existence, otherwise non-existence does not lead to the necessity of non-existence, and it would follow that existence and non-existence are in one thing together, which is impossible; or if non-existence does not lead to the necessity of non-existence, then existence does not lead to the necessity of existence.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage defines the various meanings of 'possible' (mumkin) as used in logic, starting from the most general to the most specific. The author begins by stating that general possibility (al-imkān al-ʿāmm) is called thus due to its generality, and it yields a binary division into possible and impossible. He then gives a more specific meaning (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ) which negates both absolute and temporal necessity, excluding states like life for God (which is necessary) and including contingent actions like Zayd's sitting. This special possible is narrower than the general possible because it excludes temporally necessary things. Next, he presents an even more specific meaning (al-imkān al-akhaṣṣ) which applies to future events with no necessity at all, including present accidental existents. Finally, he discusses a fourth usage equating possibility with epistemic probability (iḥtimāl), where we judge something as possible based on ignorance of its true nature. The passage ends with a critical note on a mistaken view about the condition of non-existence for possibility, showing the logical implications lead to contradiction.

Key Terms
الممكن الأخصي
مستقبل الحال

B) Only the special possible
C) Both the general and special possible
D) The epistemic possible

Key Takeaway
The more special possible applies to future events with no necessity, making it the narrowest of the three modal senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Epistemic Possibility (al-Ihtimal)
Author defines the fourth meaning of possibility as epistemic probability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify epistemic possibility (al-ihtimal) as a judgment based on ignorance, distinct from real possibility, and recognize its use in dialectic.

Core Idea
Epistemic possibility is a subjective probability based on ignorance, not a real modal property of things.

The author introduces a fourth, non-modal meaning of 'possible': epistemic possibility (al-ihtimal). This is not a real attribute of things but a judgment we make when we are ignorant of the true state of affairs. For example, seeing a distant shape, we judge it possible that it is an animal, but in reality it either is or is not. This sense is used in dialectical reasoning (jadal) and everyday discourse. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between subjective uncertainty and objective possibility.

Guided Path
1
Define epistemic possibility
It is possibility based on ignorance (jahl) of the predicate's actual state. We judge something as possible because we do not know its true nature.
2
Contrast with real possibility
Unlike the previous three meanings, this is not about the thing itself but about our knowledge. In itself, the thing is either necessary or impossible.
The author gives the example of a distant shape: we think it might be an animal, but if it is actually inanimate, it is necessarily not an animal.
3
Identify its use in dialectic
This sense is common in debates when one says 'it is possible' meaning 'it is plausible' or 'it may be the case' until evidence proves otherwise.
The passage notes it is used 'until evidence of the matter appears'.

Two Meanings of the Absolute Proposition
Author explains two views on the meaning of 'absolute' in categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general absolute and special absolute propositions and classify examples from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
The absolute proposition splits into two: one ignores necessity, the other requires temporality.

In this passage, the author unveils a foundational debate in Islamic logic: what does it mean for a categorical proposition to be 'absolute'? Early logicians split over whether absoluteness means ignoring necessity entirely or including a temporal condition. Mastering this distinction is key to understanding how different types of propositions are used in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main views
The passage presents two opinions of early scholars on the meaning of 'absolute' (al-iṭlāq). Locate where each view is introduced: 'ذهب فريق إلى أن المعني به...' and 'وذهب فريق إلى أن المعني به...'.
The first view is about unrestricted predication; the second adds a temporal condition.
2
Analyze the general absolute (first view)
The first view holds that the predicate is simply related to the subject at the time of judgment without regard to necessity or duration. The judgment is uniform for all modes of specification—like the indefinite (muhmala). Example: 'Man is animal' is absolute even though animality is necessary.
This view equates absolute with 'non-modal' in the sense of ignoring modality.
3
Analyze the special absolute (second view)
The second view restricts absolute to a temporal component: the predicate holds for the subject at some time, without absolute necessity and perpetuity. The time can be determinate (e.g., moon eclipse) or indeterminate (e.g., animal breathing). Example: 'The moon eclipses' is absolute because it occurs at a time.
This view is also called 'existential' (wujūdiyya) because it affirms existence at some time.
4
Distinguish the two views using examples
Take a proposition like 'Man is rational' (essential). According to the first view, it is absolute because it ignores necessity; according to the second view, it is not absolute because it is perpetual. Test other examples from kalam, such as 'Allah is living' (perpetual) vs. 'The sun rises' (temporal).
The key difference is whether the proposition includes a temporal condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في القضايا الحملية وإما الإطلاق فقد اختلفت آراء الأولين في محل استعماله، فذهب فريق إلى أن المعني به حالة المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع عند الحكم إيجاباً أو سلباً كيف كان من غير التفات إلى كونها ضرورية أو لا ضرورية قولاً وتصوراً، بحيث يكون الحكم فيها عاماً لجميع وجوه التخصيص أي أن نسبته إليهـا على السواء على نحو نسبة المهملة إلى الكلية والجزئية حتى إن قولنا: (الإنسان حيوان) (الإنسان كاتب) إذا كان على الوجه المذكور فمطلق، وإن كان على حال الحيوان في نفس الأمر بالنسبة إلى الإنسان ضرورياً، وحال الكاتب بالنسبة إليه ممكناً. وذهب فريق إلى أن المعني به حال المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع عند الحكم إيجاباً أو سلباً في وقت ما من غير ضرورة مطلقة ودوام، وسواء كان ذلك الوقت معيناً أو غير معين، حتى إن قولنا القمر ينخسف الحيوان يتنفس إذا لم نلتفت فيه إلى كون الوقت معيناً أو غير معين أخذ من حيث إن المحمول فيه ثابت للموضوع في وقت من غير دوام فإنه مطلق، وإن اتفق إن كان الوقت في نفس الأمر فيه معيناً كما في الخسوف أو غير معين كالتنفس، والإطلاق بهذين الاعتبارين قد لا يمنع قلب الموجب منهما إلى السالب والسالب إلى الموجب على أي حال قُدّر من الكم؛ لجواز أن يكون الحكم فيها غير دائم مع كونه غير دائم فغير معين ويكون صدقه في حالة الإيجاب عن قريب. وربما فسر الإطلاق بحال المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوعات الحاصلة بالفعل في وقت ما، حتى إنه لو تصور خلق العالم عن كل حيوان ما خلا نوع الإنسان في وقت من الأوقات، صح أن يقال بالإطلاق كل حيوان إنسان لكن هذا إنما يصح أن لو صرح بالشرط المذكور وهو أن يقال كل حيوان في الوقت المفروض (إنسان) وإلا فالقضية تكون صادقة على البعض وكاذبة في الكل، فإذن المطلق إما أن يعم الضرورة والإمكان فهو المطلق العام، وإما الحكم فيه في وقت لا دائماً فهو المطلق الخاص، وربما سميت القضية بهذا الاعتبار من الإطلاق (وجودية).
The First Art: On Categorical Propositions. As for the absolute (unqualified), the opinions of the early scholars have differed concerning its usage. One group held that what is meant by it is the state of the predicate with respect to the subject at the time of judgment, whether positive or negative, however it may be, without regard to whether it is necessary or not necessary, in wording and conception, such that the judgment in it is general to all modes of specification — i.e., its relation to them is equal, like the relation of the indefinite to the universal and the particular. Thus our statement: 'Man is animal', 'Man is writer' — if in the aforementioned manner, then it is absolute, even though the state of animal in itself with respect to man is necessary, and the state of writer with respect to him is possible. Another group held that what is meant by it is the state of the predicate with respect to the subject at the time of judgment, positive or negative, at some time, without absolute necessity and perpetuity, whether that time is determinate or indeterminate. Thus our statement: 'The moon eclipses', 'The animal breathes' — if we do not pay attention to whether the time is determinate or indeterminate, taken from the aspect that the predicate is established for the subject at some time without perpetuity, then it is absolute, even if it happens that the time in itself is determinate as in the eclipse or indeterminate as in breathing. And the absolute in these two respects may not prevent converting the affirmative to negative and the negative to affirmative in any quantity assumed, because it is possible that the judgment in them is not perpetual while being non-perpetual and indeterminate, and its truth in the affirmative case is near. And sometimes the absolute is explained as the state of the predicate with respect to the subjects actually existing at some time, so that if it were imagined that all animals except the human species were created at some time, it would be correct to say absolutely: 'Every animal is a human' — but this is only correct if the mentioned condition is stated, i.e., that it is said: 'Every animal at the supposed time is a human'; otherwise the proposition would be true of some and false of all. So the absolute either includes necessity and possibility — that is the general absolute — or the judgment in it is at a time not perpetually — that is the special absolute. And perhaps the proposition in this respect of absoluteness is called 'existential' (wujūdiyya).
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage discusses the meaning of 'absolute' (muṭlaq) in categorical propositions. The author presents two main views from early scholars. The first view defines absolute as the mere relation of predicate to subject without any consideration of necessity or duration, making the judgment uniform across all instances, analogous to the indefinite (muhmala) which is neither universal nor particular. For example, 'Man is animal' is absolute even though animality is necessary, and 'Man is writer' is absolute even though writing is possible. The second view restricts absolute to a temporal component: the predicate holds for the subject at some time without absolute necessity and without perpetuity. Thus 'The moon eclipses' is absolute because it happens at a time, even though that time is determinate, and 'The animal breathes' is absolute because it happens at an indeterminate time. The author notes that such absolute propositions allow conversion (from affirmative to negative and vice versa) because the truth is not perpetual. A third interpretation is given: absolute can refer to the predicate applying to actually existing subjects at some time, but this requires a conditional statement, otherwise it may be false universally. Finally, the author distinguishes general absolute (including both necessary and possible) and special absolute (temporal, non-perpetual), and notes that the latter is sometimes called 'existential' (wujūdiyya).

Key Terms
الإطلاق
المطلق العام
المطلق الخاص
الوجودية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second view of absolute propositions, which of the following is an example of an absolute proposition?
A) God is living (perpetual)
B) The moon eclipses (happens at a determinate time)
C) Man is rational (essential)
D) Fire is hot (natural necessity)

Key Takeaway
The absolute proposition is either general (ignoring necessity) or special (requiring temporality), and this distinction is vital for correct modal analysis in Islamic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Classify each proposition as general absolute, special absolute, or neither: (a) 'Fire burns' (b) 'The Day of Judgment will occur' (c) 'Man is rational'.
?

Propositions

The General Absolute (al-mutlaq al-'amm) and Its Definition
Author defines three types of absolute propositions: general, specific, and temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining feature of the general absolute proposition and distinguish it from other types of absolute propositions.

Core Idea
The general absolute is a judgment that encompasses both necessary and possible predicates.

This lesson focuses on the first definition of absolute (itlaq) given by early logicians. According to this view, an absolute proposition is one where the state of the predicate relative to the subject is not considered in terms of necessity or possibility. This yields the general absolute (al-mutlaq al-'amm), which applies equally to necessary and possible judgments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term 'itlaq'
Notice that the author begins by noting disagreement. The first group defines itlaq as the state of the predicate without regard to necessity.
2
Extract the genus and differentia for the general absolute
The genus is 'absolute proposition', the differentia is 'encompassing both necessity and possibility'.
3
Apply the definition to examples
Verify that 'Man is an animal' (necessary) and 'Man is a writer' (possible) both fall under the general absolute.
4
Compare with other types
Contrast with specific absolute (which includes a time condition) and temporal absolute (actual existence condition).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما الإطلاق فقد اختلفت آراء الأولين في محل استعماله، فذهب فريق إلى أن المعني به حالة المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع عند الحكم إيجاباً أو سلباً كيف كان من غير التفات إلى كونها ضرورية أو لا ضرورية قولاً وتصوراً، بحيث يكون الحكم فيها عاماً لجميع وجوه التخصيص أي أن نسبته إليها على السواء على نحو نسبة المهملة إلى الكلية والجزئية حتى إن قولنا: (الإنسان) (حيوان) (الإنسان) كاتب إذا كان على الوجه المذكور فمطلق، وإن كان على حال الحيوان في نفس الأمر بالنسبة إلى الإنسان ضرورياً، وحال الكاتب بالنسبة إليه ممكناً.
وذهب فريق إلى أن المعني به حال المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع عند الحكم إيجاباً أو سلباً في وقت ما من غير ضرورة مطلقة ودوام، وسواء كان ذلك الوقت معيناً أو غير معين، حتى إن قولنا القمر ينخسف الحيوان يتنفس إذا لم نلتفت فيه إلى كون الوقت معيناً أو غير معين أخذ من حيث إن المحمول فيه ثابت للموضوع في وقت من غير دوام فإنه مطلق، وإن اتفق إن كان الوقت في نفس الأمر فيه معيناً كما في الخسوف أو غير معين كالتنفس، والإطلاق بهذين الاعتبارين قد لا يمنع قلب الموجب منهما إلى السالب والسالب إلى الموجب على أي حال قدر من الكم؛ لجواز أن يكون الحكم فيها غير دائم مع كونه غير دائم فغير معين ويكون صدقه في حالة الإيجاب عن قريب.
وربما فسر الإطلاق بحال المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوعات الحاصلة بالفعل في وقت ما، حتى إنه لو تصور خلق العالم عن كل حيوان ما خلا نوع الإنسان في وقت من الأوقات، صح أن يقال بالإطلاق كل حيوان إنسان لكن هذا إنما يصح أن لو صرح بالشرط المذكور وهو أن يقال كل حيوان في الوقت المفروض (إنسان) وإلا فالقضية تكون صادقة على البعض وكاذبة في الكل، فإذن المطلق إما أن يعم الضرورة والإمكان فهو المطلق العام، وإما الحكم فيه في وقت لا دائماً فهو المطلق الخاص، وربما سميت القضية بهذا الاعتبار من الإطلاق (وجودية).
وأما الحكم فيه على موضوعات موجودة بالفعل في وقت وجودها فهو المطلق الوقتي.
As for absolute (itlaq), the opinions of the early scholars differed regarding its application. One group held that what is meant by it is the state of the predicate relative to the subject when judging affirmatively or negatively, in whatever manner, without paying attention to whether it is necessary or not necessary, verbally and conceptually, such that the judgment is general to all aspects of specification, i.e., its relation to them is equal, like the relation of the indefinite (muhmala) to the universal and particular. Thus our saying: 'Man is an animal' and 'Man is a writer', if it is in the mentioned manner, is absolute, even though the state of 'animal' with respect to man is necessary in reality, and the state of 'writer' with respect to him is possible.
Another group held that what is meant by it is the state of the predicate relative to the subject when judging affirmatively or negatively at some time, without absolute necessity and permanence, whether that time is specified or unspecified. Thus our saying: 'The moon is eclipsed' and 'Animal breathes', if we do not pay attention to whether the time is specified or unspecified, taken from the fact that the predicate is established for the subject at a time without permanence, then it is absolute. And if it happens that the time is specified in reality, as in the eclipse, or unspecified, like breathing, the absolute in these two considerations may not prevent converting the affirmative to the negative and the negative to the affirmative in whatever quantity is estimated, because it is possible that the judgment is not permanent, being non-permanent and unspecified, and its truth in the affirmative case occurs soon.
Perhaps absolute was interpreted as the state of the predicate relative to subjects that actually exist at some time, such that if one imagined that the world was created from every animal except the human species at some time, it would be correct to say absolutely 'Every animal is a human', but this is only valid if the mentioned condition is explicit, i.e., 'Every animal at the assumed time is a human'. Otherwise, the proposition would be true of some and false of all. So then the absolute either encompasses necessity and possibility, and it is the general absolute; or the judgment is at a time but not permanent, and it is the specific absolute, and perhaps the proposition in this consideration is called 'existential' (wujudiyya). As for the judgment on subjects that actually exist at the time of their existence, it is the temporal absolute.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of absolute (itlaq) in categorical propositions. The author begins by noting disagreement among early scholars on the meaning of itlaq. One group holds that itlaq refers to the state of the predicate relative to the subject in judgment, whether affirmative or negative, without considering whether it is necessary or not. For example, 'Man is an animal' and 'Man is a writer' are both absolute in this sense, even though the first is necessary and the second possible. Another group defines itlaq as the judgment that holds for a specific time, without absolute necessity or permanence, whether the time is specified or not. Examples: 'The moon is eclipsed' (specific time) and 'Animal breathes' (unspecific time). A third group interprets itlaq as the predicate applying to actually existing subjects at a certain time. Then the author classifies three types: (1) al-mutlaq al-'amm (general absolute) which encompasses both necessity and possibility, (2) al-mutlaq al-khass (specific absolute) which is a judgment at a time but not permanent, sometimes called 'existential' (wujudiyya), and (3) al-mutlaq al-waqti (temporal absolute) which is a judgment on actually existing subjects at the time of their existence. The passage then compares these types in terms of generality and specificity.

Key Terms
الإطلاق
المطلق العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first definition of absolute (itlaq), which of the following is an example of a general absolute proposition?
A) The moon is eclipsed
B) Man is an animal
C) Every animal breathes
D) All humans are mortal

Key Takeaway
The general absolute is the broadest type of absolute proposition, including both necessary and possible judgments.

?

The Specific Absolute (al-mutlaq al-khass) and Its Temporal Condition
Author defines three types of absolute propositions: general, specific, and temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific absolute proposition by its feature of being a judgment at a time without permanence, and distinguish it from other types.

Core Idea
The specific absolute is a judgment that holds at some time, without absolute necessity or permanence.

This lesson covers the second definition of absolute (itlaq), which adds a temporal condition. According to this view, an absolute proposition is one where the predicate holds for the subject at a specific or unspecified time, without the requirement of perpetuity. This yields the specific absolute (al-mutlaq al-khass), sometimes called existential (wujudiyya).

Guided Path
1
Understand the second definition
The second group defines itlaq as the state of the predicate at some time, not necessarily permanent.
2
Extract the differentia for specific absolute
The differentia is 'the judgment is at a time, not permanently'.
3
Apply to examples
The moon is eclipsed (specified time) and Animal breathes (unspecified time) are specific absolutes.
4
Note the conversion property
The passage mentions that these propositions may not prevent conversion (from affirmative to negative) because they are not permanent.

The Temporal Absolute (al-mutlaq al-waqti) and Its Condition of Actual Existence
Author defines three types of absolute propositions: general, specific, and temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the temporal absolute proposition by its condition of applying to actually existing subjects at the time of their existence.

Core Idea
The temporal absolute is a judgment on subjects that actually exist at the time of their existence.

This lesson covers the third definition of absolute (itlaq), which restricts the proposition to subjects that exist in actuality at the time of judgment. This yields the temporal absolute (al-mutlaq al-waqti), which is the most specific type, applying only to existing subjects at their time of existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third definition
The third interpretation defines itlaq as the state of the predicate relative to subjects that actually exist at some time.
2
Extract the differentia for temporal absolute
The differentia is 'the judgment applies to subjects that actually exist at the time of their existence'.
3
Analyze the conditional example
The passage gives an example: 'Every animal is a human' under a hypothetical condition (if all animals were human except humans at a time). This shows the importance of specifying the condition.
4
Compare with specific absolute
Note that temporal absolute requires actual existence, while specific absolute only requires a time, not necessarily actual subjects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
