Thus our saying: 'The moon is eclipsed' and 'Animal breathes', if we do not pay attention to whether the time is specified or unspecified, taken from the fact that the predicate is established for the subject at a time without permanence, then it is absolute. And if it happens that the time is specified in reality, as in the eclipse, or unspecified, like breathing, the absolute in these two considerations may not prevent converting the affirmative to the negative and the negative to the affirmative in whatever quantity is estimated, because it is possible that the judgment is not permanent, being non-permanent and unspecified, and its truth in the affirmative case occurs soon.
Perhaps absolute was interpreted as the state of the predicate relative to subjects that actually exist at some time, such that if one imagined that the world was created from every animal except the human species at some time, it would be correct to say absolutely 'Every animal is a human', but this is only valid if the mentioned condition is explicit, i.e., 'Every animal at the assumed time is a human'. Otherwise, the proposition would be true of some and false of all. So then the absolute either encompasses necessity and possibility, and it is the general absolute; or the judgment is at a time but not permanent, and it is the specific absolute, and perhaps the proposition in this consideration is called 'existential' (wujudiyya). As for the judgment on subjects that actually exist at the time of their existence, it is the temporal absolute.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of absolute (itlaq) in categorical propositions. The author begins by noting disagreement among early scholars on the meaning of itlaq. One group holds that itlaq refers to the state of the predicate relative to the subject in judgment, whether affirmative or negative, without considering whether it is necessary or not. For example, 'Man is an animal' and 'Man is a writer' are both absolute in this sense, even though the first is necessary and the second possible. Another group defines itlaq as the judgment that holds for a specific time, without absolute necessity or permanence, whether the time is specified or not. Examples: 'The moon is eclipsed' (specific time) and 'Animal breathes' (unspecific time). A third group interprets itlaq as the predicate applying to actually existing subjects at a certain time. Then the author classifies three types: (1) al-mutlaq al-'amm (general absolute) which encompasses both necessity and possibility, (2) al-mutlaq al-khass (specific absolute) which is a judgment at a time but not permanent, sometimes called 'existential' (wujudiyya), and (3) al-mutlaq al-waqti (temporal absolute) which is a judgment on actually existing subjects at the time of their existence. The passage then compares these types in terms of generality and specificity.

Key Terms
الإطلاق
المطلق الوقتي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the third definition of absolute, what condition must the subject satisfy?
A) It must be necessary
B) It must be possible
C) It must actually exist at the time of judgment
D) It must be permanent

Key Takeaway
The temporal absolute is the most restricted type, requiring the subject to actually exist at the time of judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why Absolute (Itlaq) Is Not a Modality
Author denies itlaq as modality and discusses logical equivalences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a genuine modality (jiha) and absolute (itlaq) and evaluate whether a given proposition is modal or absolute.

Core Idea
Modality indicates a predicate's state relative to the subject; the absolute says nothing.

The author argues that absolute (itlaq) is not a modality (jiha) because a modality necessarily indicates persistence, impossibility, or non-persistence of the predicate. The absolute does not indicate any such state. If someone insists on calling it a modality, the dispute becomes merely verbal. In this lesson, we will dissect the definition of modality and see why itlaq falls outside it.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of jiha (modality)
From the passage, identify what a modality indicates: the state of the predicate relative to the subject—either permanence of affirmation (dawam al-ijab), impossibility (imtina'), or non-permanence (la dawam). Write down the key features.
The definition is given in the first sentence: 'الجهة قد بان أنها تدل على حال المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع من دوام الإيجاب أو امتناع ولا دوام'.
2
Define itlaq (absolute) as presented
The author says the absolute 'does not address any of that' (الإطلاق لا تعرض فيه لشيء من ذلك). So the absolute is a proposition that carries no modal qualification—it simply asserts the predicate without indicating its temporal necessity or impossibility.
An absolute proposition is like 'Zaid is standing' without any modal force.
3
Compare the two definitions
Since the absolute lacks the essential feature of a modality (it does not indicate a state of the predicate), it is not a modality. The author concludes: 'وليس الإطلاق بكل اعتبار جهة'.
The phrase 'بكل اعتبار' indicates that in no consideration is it a modality.
4
Assess the verbal dispute possibility
If someone still calls the absolute a modality but does not mean by it the standard definition—only that it 'does not indicate some states'—then the disagreement is merely verbal (منازعة في الألفاظ). The author dismisses it as irrelevant.
The phrase 'لا مشاحة فيه' means 'no need to quibble' over words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليس الإطلاق بكل اعتبار جهة، إذ الجهة قد بان أنها تدل على حال المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع من دوام الإيجاب أو امتناع ولا دوام والإطلاق لا تعرض فيه لشيء من ذلك، ومن جعل الإطلاق جهة للقضية ولم يرد به غير أنه لا دلالة له على بعض أحوال المحمول بالنسبة إلى الموضوع، فحاصل نزاعه إنما يرجع إلى المنازعة في الألفاظ ولا مشاحة فيه، وإلا فالجهات على التحقيق إنما هي واجب أن تكون وممتنع أن يكون، وممكن أن يكون على ما عرف فقولنا واجب أن يكون في قوة قولنا ممتنع أن لا يكون وليس ممكناً أن لا يكون بالمعنى العام، ويلزم كل واحد منها الآخر لزوماً معاكساً.
فإن قيل : قولنا ليس ممكناً أن لا يكون بالمعنى العام معناه أنه ليس ليس بممتنع لا يكون، ومعنى ليس ليس بممتنع أن لا يكون ممتنع أن لا يكون، فإذن ممتنع أن لا يكون هو نفس قولنا ليس ممكناً أن لا يكون بالمعنى العام، فلا يكون أحدهما لازماً للآخر بل عينه.
قلنا: الممكن العام وإن كان عبارة عن سلب الامتناع، وأن سلب الإمكان العام هو سلب سلب الامتناع، فسلب سلب الامتناع إنما يمتنع أن يكون لازماً للامتناع أن لو كان هو هو وليس كذلك، فإنه لو كان سلب الإمكان العام هو نفس الامتناع والإمكان فقد كان هو سلب الامتناع للزم منه أن يكون كل واحد من الأمرين سلب الآخر وهو محال، وقولنا ممتنع أن يكون في قوة قولنا واجب أن لا
And the absolute is not a modality in every consideration, because the modality has been shown to indicate the state of the predicate relative to the subject in terms of permanence of affirmation or impossibility and non-permanence, and the absolute does not address any of that. If someone considers the absolute a modality for the proposition and does not mean by it anything other than that it does not indicate some states of the predicate relative to the subject, the outcome of his dispute is merely a dispute over words, and there is no quibbling over terminology. Otherwise, the modalities in truth are only: necessary to be, impossible to be, and possible to be, as known. So our saying 'necessary to be' is equivalent to our saying 'impossible not to be' and 'not possible not to be' in the general sense, and each of them is entailed by the other in a reversed entailment.
If it is said: Our saying 'not possible not to be' in the general sense means that it is not the case that it is not impossible not to be, and the meaning of 'not the case that it is not impossible not to be' is 'impossible not to be'. Therefore, 'impossible not to be' is the same as our saying 'not possible not to be' in the general sense. So one is not entailed by the other; rather they are identical.
We say: The general possible, even though it is the negation of impossibility, and the negation of general possibility is the negation of the negation of impossibility, so the negation of the negation of impossibility cannot be an entailment of impossibility, if it were identical to it and it is not so. For if the negation of general possibility were the same as impossibility and possibility, then it would be the negation of impossibility, which would entail that each of the two is the negation of the other, and that is impossible. And our saying 'impossible to be' is equivalent to our saying 'necessary not to...'
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage addresses whether the absolute (itlaq) should be considered a modality (jiha). The author argues it is not, because a modality indicates permanence of affirmation or impossibility or non-permanence, whereas the absolute does not indicate any of these. If someone calls itlaq a modality merely meaning that it does not indicate certain states, the dispute is merely verbal. The true modalities are necessary, impossible, and possible. The author states that 'necessary to be' is equivalent to 'impossible not to be' and 'not possible not to be' in the general sense, and these are mutually entailing in a reversed order. An objector argues that 'not possible not to be' is the same as 'impossible not to be' by double negation, so they are identical rather than entailing. The author responds by clarifying that the general possible is the negation of impossibility (salb al-imtina'), and the negation of general possibility is the negation of the negation of impossibility, which is not identical to impossibility itself; they are distinct. The response is cut off but continues the argument.

Modal Equivalences: Necessity and Impossibility
Author discusses logical equivalences between modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a statement of necessity into its equivalent forms using impossibility and possibility, and vice versa, applying the rules of immediate inference.

Core Idea
Necessity to be is the same as impossibility not to be; they are logically equivalent, not identical.

The author establishes that 'necessary to be' (wajib an yakun) is equivalent to 'impossible not to be' (mumtani' an la yakun) and also to 'not possible not to be' (la mumkin an la yakun) in the general sense. He then defends this equivalence against an objection that would collapse it into identity. In this lesson, we will unpack these equivalences and understand how they constitute immediate inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three primary modalities
From the passage, list the three modalities: wajib an yakun (necessary to be), mumtani' an yakun (impossible to be), mumkin an yakun (possible to be).
These are the 'true modalities' according to the author.
2
Extract the equivalence statements
Find where the author says 'قولنا واجب أن يكون في قوة قولنا ممتنع أن لا يكون وليس ممكناً أن لا يكون بالمعنى العام'. This means 'necessary to be' is equivalent to 'impossible not to be' and 'not possible not to be' in the general sense.
The 'general sense' of possibility (al-mumkin al-'amm) is defined as the negation of impossibility.
3
Understand the objector's reasoning
The objector argues that 'not possible not to be' is identical to 'impossible not to be' because double negation yields identity: 'not not impossible = impossible'. Thus, they claim the author's equivalence is actually an identity, not entailment.
The objector uses the principle that the negation of a negation returns the original.
4
Follow the author's response
The author clarifies that 'general possible' is the negation of impossibility (salb al-imtina'). Therefore, the negation of general possibility is the negation of the negation of impossibility (salb salb al-imtina'). This is not identical to impossibility itself; rather it is the double negation that amounts to the original impossibility? Wait, the author seems to argue that if they were identical, then each would be the negation of the other, which is impossible. The key is that 'general possible' is defined as 'not impossible', so 'not (not impossible)' is not the same as 'impossible' without further assumptions. The author insists that the equivalence is one of entailment (luzum), not identity.
The passage is cut off, but the point is that the relationship is a true equivalence (mutual entailment) rather than synonymy.
5
Apply the equivalence to 'impossible to be'
The author begins: 'قولنا ممتنع أن يكون في قوة قولنا واجب أن لا...' which, when completed, means 'impossible to be' is equivalent to 'necessary not to be'. This mirrors the first equivalence.
This completes the set: necessity and impossibility are interdefinable through negation.

Opposites and Converse Entailments of Modalities
Author defines three modalities, their opposites, converse and non-converse entailments, and provides a diagram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the opposite of each of the three modalities and determine when two modal expressions mutually entail each other (converse).

Core Idea
Every modality has a clear opposite, and 'possible to be' and 'possible not to be' are converse.

This passage defines the three fundamental modalities—necessary, impossible, and possible—and their logical opposites. It also introduces the concept of converse entailment, where two modalities mutually imply each other. Pay close attention to the distinction between the general and specific senses of possibility, as the converse relation applies only to the general sense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three modalities
Find the three modal terms defined in the passage: 'necessary to be' (واجب أن يكون), 'impossible to be' (ممتنع أن يكون), and 'possible to be' (ممكن أن يكون).
These are the primary modalities in Islamic logic.
2
Find the opposite of each modality
The passage lists the opposite of each modality. For example, the opposite of 'necessary to be' is 'not necessary to be' (ليس واجباً أن يكون). List all three opposites.
The opposite is a contradictory negation, not a contrary.
3
Locate the converse entailment
The passage states that 'possible to be' and 'possible not to be' in the general sense mutually entail each other (لزوم معاكس). Note that this is a two-way implication.
This converse relation holds only in the general sense, not the specific sense.
4
Understand the conversion operation
The passage shows that the opposite of 'necessary to be' converts to 'not impossible not to be' and 'possible not to be' in the general sense. This shows how opposites can be expressed in equivalent forms.
Conversion here means logical equivalence through negation and term swapping.

Commentary
This passage defines the three main modalities (necessary, impossible, possible) and their logical relations. It begins by noting that 'possible in the general sense' is equivalent to 'possible not to be' and they mutually entail each other. Then it lists the opposites of each modality: the opposite of 'necessary to be' is 'not necessary to be' (i.e., contingent), which converts to 'not impossible not to be' and 'possible not to be' in the general sense. Similarly for the other two. The author then explains non-converse entailments: for example, 'necessary to be' entails the negation of impossibility and possibility, but the reverse does not hold because denying possibility does not necessarily imply impossibility—it could be necessary. The passage concludes by introducing a diagram (a 'lawh') to illustrate these relationships in a table format for easier comprehension. The diagram itself is partially garbled in the OCR but shows pairs of modalities with 'متلازمة متعاكس' (converse entailment) and 'متقابلات' (opposites). The author's aim is to clarify the precise logical connections between modal propositions.

Non-Converse Entailments of the Modalities
Author defines three modalities, their opposites, converse and non-converse entailments, and provides a diagram.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the non-converse entailments of each of the three modalities and explain why the reverse entailment does not hold.

Core Idea
Necessity implies non-impossibility and non-possibility, but not vice versa, because a contingent thing does not require its own necessity.

The second part of the passage details what each modality entails without the reverse holding. For example, 'necessary to be' entails that it is not impossible and not possible (in specific sense), but the negation of possibility does not entail necessity. This section is crucial for precise logical reasoning in Islamic theology, especially when proving attributes of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the non-converse entailments for 'necessary to be'
The passage lists: 'not impossible to be', 'not necessary not to be', 'possible to be in the general sense', 'not possible to be in the specific sense', and 'not possible not to be in the specific sense'. Write them down.
These are all one-way entailments from necessity.
2
Understand why the reverse does not hold for necessity
The passage explains: if we only know that something is not impossible, it could be necessary or possible; likewise, negating specific possibility could mean it is necessary or impossible. So these negations do not entail necessity.
This shows the direction of implication is strict.
3
Apply the same analysis to 'impossible to be'
Impossible entails: 'not necessary to be', 'not impossible not to be', 'possible not to be' (general), 'not possible to be' (specific), 'not possible not to be' (specific). Note the similar pattern.
Impossibility negates necessity and possibility in specific senses.
4
Apply the same to 'possible to be'
Possible (general) entails: 'not necessary to be', 'not impossible not to be', 'possible not to be' (general), 'not impossible to be', 'not necessary not to be', 'possible to be' (general). Notice it does not entail specific possibility.
General possibility includes both necessary and specific possible.

Two Senses of Possibility: General vs. Specific
Author refutes an objection regarding the possibility of the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two senses of possibility (general and specific) and explain why the Necessary Being is not possible in the specific sense but is possible in the general sense.

Core Idea
Necessity is a third status distinct from possibility and impossibility.

In this passage, the author refutes an opponent's argument that the Necessary Being must be either possible or impossible. The opponent's error lies in conflating two distinct meanings of 'possible'. By distinguishing general possibility (not impossible) from specific possibility (contingency), the author shows that the Necessary Being is not impossible but also not contingent, thus preserving its necessity.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the opponent's dilemma
The opponent claims: 'The necessary is either possible or not possible.' If possible, then it could not exist (contradicting necessity). If not possible, then it is impossible (also contradicting necessity). The opponent concludes that the necessary cannot be necessary.
This is a false dilemma because it assumes only two options.
2
Distinguish general vs. specific possibility
The author explains that 'possible' has two senses: general (not impossible) and specific (contingent, capable of both existence and nonexistence). The necessary is possible in the general sense but not in the specific sense.
The opponent used 'possible' ambiguously.
3
Apply the distinction to resolve the dilemma
When the opponent says 'possible' (general), the necessary qualifies, but it does not entail possible not to be. When 'not possible' (specific), the necessary is excluded, but that does not make it impossible; it makes it necessary.
The dilemma is resolved by showing that necessity is a third option.
4
Learn the modal principle
Negating one modality does not force the remaining two. From 'not possible in the specific sense', one cannot infer impossibility, because necessity is a distinct modality.
This principle is crucial in modal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حقق معي الجهات ولازمها علم أن قد أبعد من قال إن الواجب إما ممكن أو ليس بممكن، فإن كان الأول فواجب الوجود ممكن، وما هو ممكن أن يكون فهو ممكن أن لا يكون فواجب الوجود ممكن أن لا يكون، وإن كان الثاني فليس بواجب، إذ الممكن ما ليس بممتنع، فما ليس بممكن ممتنع، فالواجب ممتنع وهو محال، ولم يعلم أن الواجب إن قيل له ممكن فليس بغير المعنى العام، وما هو ممكن أن يكون بالمعنى العام فقد لا يصدق عليه ممكن أن لا يكون، وإن قيل له غير ممكن فليس إلا بالمعنى الخاص، وما ليس ممكناً بالمعنى الخاص فلا يلزم أن يكون ممتنعاً، بل جاز أن يكون واجباً، فإنه ليس يلزم من سلب جهة من جهات أن يتعين واحد من المتبقي كما عرف بل لو كان الأمر بالعكس لقد كان ما ثلاث
And whoever investigates with me the modalities and their entailments knows that he has gone far astray who said that the necessary is either possible or not possible. For if the first, then the Necessary Existent is possible, and whatever is possible to be is possible not to be, so the Necessary Existent is possible not to be. And if the second, then it is not necessary, for the possible is that which is not impossible, so what is not possible is impossible, so the necessary would be impossible, which is absurd. And he did not know that the necessary, if it is said to be possible, it is only in the general sense, and what is possible to be in the general sense may not be described as possible not to be. And if it is said to be not possible, it is only in the specific sense, and what is not possible in the specific sense does not entail that it be impossible; rather it is permissible that it be necessary. For it is not entailed from the negation of one modality that one of the remaining two is determined, as is known. Rather if the matter were the opposite, then there would be three...
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of an erroneous argument about the Necessary Being (واجب الوجود). The error arises from conflating the two senses of possibility. The opponent argues: 'The necessary is either possible or not possible.' If it is possible, then it could not exist (since possible implies possibility of non-existence), which contradicts necessity. If it is not possible, then it is impossible, which also contradicts necessity. The author clarifies that when we say the necessary is 'possible', we mean in the general sense (المعنى العام), where 'possible to be' does not necessarily entail 'possible not to be'. And when we say it is 'not possible', we mean in the specific sense (المعنى الخاص), where 'not possible' means not contingent, but that does not imply impossibility; it could be necessary. In other words, necessity is a third status distinct from possibility and impossibility. The mistake is to think that only two options exist. The author concludes by stating that negating one modality does not force the remaining two (i.e., from 'not possible' you cannot conclude 'impossible' because 'necessary' is also an option). The final phrase 'بل لو كان الأمر بالعكس لقد كان ما ثلاث' suggests that if the opposite were true, the number of modalities would be three (i.e., necessary, possible, impossible) but the opponent's argument falsely reduces them to two.

الواجب
الممكن
الممتنع
المعنى العام
المعنى الخاص

Checkpoint

D) He thinks necessity is a type of possibility.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being is not contingent (specific possibility) but is possible in the general sense (not impossible), and negating specific possibility does not entail impossibility.

Distinguishing the Two Senses of 'Possible' in Modal Logic
Author refutes the claim that the necessary is possible, clarifying modal distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the general and specific senses of 'possible' (mumkin) and apply this distinction to correctly characterize the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud).

Core Idea
The necessary can be called 'possible' in the general sense but not in the specific sense; confusion leads to contradiction.

This passage addresses a common logical error: someone claims that the Necessary Being is either possible or not possible, and presses both options to absurd conclusions. The author refutes this by showing that 'possible' has two distinct meanings. Understanding this distinction is vital for precise theological reasoning about the Necessary Being and for correctly interpreting modal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's dilemma
The opponent argues: if the necessary is possible, then it can be non-existent (contradiction to necessity); if it is not possible, then it is impossible (also contradiction). Thus the necessary cannot exist. Recognize this as a false dilemma that ignores the ambiguity of 'possible'.
2
Spot the two meanings of 'possible'
The author explains that 'possible' has a general sense (al-ma'na al-'amm) that includes the necessary (i.e., not impossible), and a specific sense (al-ma'na al-khass) that excludes the necessary (i.e., capable of both existence and non-existence). The opponent mistakenly uses the specific sense for both prongs.
In the specific sense, 'possible' implies potentiality for non-existence, which does not apply to the necessary.
3
Apply the distinction to the dilemma
The Necessary Being is possible in the general sense (it is not impossible), but not possible in the specific sense (it cannot be non-existent). Thus the opponent's second horn would be 'not possible in the specific sense', which does not entail impossibility. The dilemma is resolved.
The author also notes that negating one mode does not automatically determine the other two modes.
4
Extend to modal propositions
The author further distinguishes between 'necessary of negation' (wajibat al-salb) and 'negative of necessity' (salibat al-wujub), and similarly for 'possible of negation' and 'negative of possibility'. This shows that modal operators must be carefully ordered to avoid confusion.
For example, 'necessary of negation' means the negation is necessary (universal negative), whereas 'negative of necessity' means it is not necessary, which is weaker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حقق معي الجهات ولازمها علم أن قد أبعد من قال إن الواجب إما ممكن أو ليس بممكن، فإن كان الأول فواجب الوجود ممكن، وما هو ممكن أن يكون فهو ممكن أن لا يكون فواجب الوجود ممكن أن لا يكون، وإن كان الثاني فليس بواجب، إذ الممكن ما ليس بممتنع، فما ليس بممكن ممتنع، فالواجب ممتنع وهو محال، ولم يعلم أن الواجب إن قيل له ممكن فليس بغير المعنى العام، وما هو ممكن أن يكون بالمعنى العام فقد لا يصدق عليه ممكن أن لا يكون، وإن قيل له غير ممكن فليس إلا بالمعنى الخاص، وما ليس ممكناً بالمعنى الخاص فلا يلزم أن يكون ممتنعاً، بل جاز أن يكون واجباً، فإنه ليس يلزم من سلب جهة من جهات أن يتعين واحد من المتبقي كما عرف بل لو كان الأمر بالعكس لقد كان ما تخيله متجهاً، بل وعلم أيضاً أن واجبة السلب غير سالبة الوجوب، وممكنة السلب غیر سالبة الإمكان، إذ الواجبة السلب مما لا يتصور معها الإيجاب وإلا كانت واجبة أو ممكنة، وخرجت عن أن تكون واجبة السلب ولا كذلك سالبة الوجب فإنها غير ممتنع أن تكون موجبة لجواز الإمكان، والممكنة السالبة ما لا ضرورة فيها في طرفي الوجود والعدم كما عرف، ولا كذلك سالبة الإمكان، إذ من ضرورة سلب إمكان ضرورة الوجود أو العدم.
And whoever verifies with me the modes and their implications knows that he has gone far who said that the necessary is either possible or not possible. If the first, then the necessary of existence is possible, and whatever is possible to be is possible not to be, so the necessary of existence is possible not to be. If the second, then it is not necessary, for the possible is what is not impossible, so what is not possible is impossible, so the necessary is impossible, which is absurd. And he did not know that the necessary if called possible is only in the general sense, and what is possible to be in the general sense may not be described as possible not to be. And if called not possible, it is only in the specific sense, and what is not possible in the specific sense does not entail that it be impossible; rather it may be necessary. For it does not follow from the negation of one mode that one of the remaining two is determined, as is known. Rather if the opposite were the case, what he imagined would be directed. And it is also known that the necessary of negation (i.e., universal negative) is not the negative of necessity, and the possible of negation is not the negative of possibility. For the necessary of negation is that with which affirmation is inconceivable; otherwise it would be necessary or possible, and would exit from being necessary of negation. And it is not so for the negative of necessity, for it is not impossible that it be affirmative due to the permissibility of possibility. And the possible of negation is that in which there is no necessity in either side of existence and non-existence, as is known. And it is not so for the negative of possibility, for from the negation of possibility there necessarily follows necessity of existence or non-existence.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents a nuanced argument regarding modal propositions, specifically defending the position that the Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud) is not 'possible' in the sense that implies the possibility of non-existence. The author begins by refuting an opponent who claims the necessary is either possible or not possible. If it were possible, then it could not be necessary because whatever is possible can be non-existent. If it were not possible, then it would be impossible, contradicting its necessity. The author clarifies that the opponent misunderstands the two senses of 'possible': the general sense (al-ma'na al-'amm) which includes the necessary, and the specific sense (al-ma'na al-khass) which excludes the necessary. The necessary can be called possible in the general sense but not in the specific sense. The author then discusses the implications for modal propositions, distinguishing between 'necessary of negation' (wajibat al-salb) and 'negative of necessity' (salibat al-wujub), and similarly for possibility. The key insight is that the negation of one mode does not automatically determine another mode, contrary to what some might think. This is a critical refinement of modal logic, showing the independence of modalities.

The Necessary Being is not 'possible' in the sense that implies potential non-existence; modal terms must be used with precision, distinguishing general and specific senses.

Distinguishing Modal Propositions: Wajiba al-Salb and Mumkina al-Salb
Author distinguishes wajiba al-salb from saliba al-wujub and similar modal pairs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between wajiba al-salb, saliba al-wujub, mumkina al-salb, and saliba al-imkan, and explain why they are not interchangeable.

Core Idea
Modal propositions are not symmetrical: negating a necessity is not the same as a necessary negation.

In this passage, the author clarifies subtle but crucial distinctions between four types of modal propositions. These distinctions are essential for correctly interpreting arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh where modality matters. Without them, one might conflate a necessary negation with a mere negation of necessity, leading to invalid reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four terms
The passage introduces four modal proposition types: wajiba al-salb, saliba al-wujub, mumkina al-salb, and saliba al-imkan. Note their Arabic formulations and literal meanings: 'necessary negation', 'negative of necessary', 'possible negation', 'negative of possible'.
2
Understand the first pair
Wajiba al-salb means the negation is necessary; affirmation is impossible. Saliba al-wujub means the negation of a necessary proposition; it does not itself entail impossibility of affirmation. The author states that in wajiba al-salb, affirmation cannot be conceived, while in saliba al-wujub, it may be possible.
3
Understand the second pair
Mumkina al-salb means the negation is possible; no necessity on either side of existence or non-existence. Saliba al-imkan means the negation of possibility, which entails necessity of existence or non-existence. The author notes that mumkina al-salb has no necessity, while saliba al-imkan implies necessity.
4
Contrast the pairs
The first pair contrasts a modalized negation (the negation itself is necessary) vs a negation of a modal (denying necessity). The second pair contrasts a possible negation vs a negation of possibility (implying necessity). Understand why they differ logically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تخيله متجهاً، بل وعلم أيضاً أن واجبة السلب غير سالبة الوجوب، وممكنة السلب غير سالبة الإمكان، إذ الواجبة السلب مما لا يتصور معها الإيجاب وإلا كانت واجبة أو ممكنة، وخرجت عن أن تكون واجبة السلب ولا كذلك سالبة الوجب فإنها غير ممتنع أن تكون موجبة لجواز الإمكان، والممكنة السالبة ما لا ضرورة فيها في طرفي الوجود والعدم كما عرف، ولا كذلك سالبة الإمكان، إذ من ضرورة سلب إمكان ضرورة الوجود أو العدم.
You imagine it directed? Rather, know also that wajiba al-salb (necessary negation) is not saliba al-wujub (negative of necessary), and mumkina al-salb (possible negation) is not saliba al-imkan (negative of possible). For wajiba al-salb is such that affirmation cannot be conceived with it, otherwise it would be necessary or possible, and would cease to be wajiba al-salb. But saliba al-wujub is not like that, for it is not impossible that it be affirmative due to possibility. And mumkina al-saliba is that which has no necessity in either existence or non-existence, as known. And saliba al-imkan is not like that, for from necessity of negating possibility comes necessity of existence or non-existence.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between different modal propositions. The author clarifies that 'necessary negation' (wajiba al-salb) is not the same as the negative of necessary (saliba al-wujub). Similarly, 'possible negation' (mumkina al-salb) is not the same as the negative of possible (saliba al-imkan). The reasoning is given: in wajiba al-salb, affirmation is impossible; if it were possible, the proposition would be possible or necessary, contradicting its nature. But saliba al-wujub can be affirmative if it is possible. For mumkina al-saliba, it has no necessity in either side, whereas saliba al-imkan implies necessity because negating possibility of existence leads to necessity of non-existence, and vice versa.

سالبة الوجوب
ممكنة السلب
سالبة الإمكان

C) Wajiba al-salb is about possibility
D) Saliba al-wujub is about necessity of negation

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is that wajiba al-salb and mumkina al-salb are modalized negations, while saliba al-wujub and saliba al-imkan are negations of modal propositions, and they are not equivalent.

The Fundamental Principle of Predication
Author clarifies the fundamental condition of predication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an affirmative predication is valid by checking whether the subject and predicate refer to the same described thing, not whether their essences are identical.

Core Idea
Predication requires identity of the described thing, not identity of essences.

The author opens the chapter by stating that every predicate is either affirmed or denied of a subject. He then warns against a common misunderstanding: predication does not require that the essence of the predicate be the same as the essence of the subject. Rather, it only requires that the thing described by the subject is exactly the thing described by the predicate. This distinction is fundamental to understanding how propositions function in logic and in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of any proposition
In any affirmative proposition, locate the subject term (mawdu') and the predicate term (mahmul). For example, in 'The human is laughing,' the subject is 'human' and the predicate is 'laughing.'
This step is the basis for all further analysis.
2
Understand the common misunderstanding
The author states that if predication required identity of essences, then only synonymous terms like 'human' and 'mortal' would work. Most real predications would be invalid. So clearly that is not what predication means.
3
Apply the correct criterion
Instead, ask: Is the thing described by the subject the same as the thing described by the predicate? In 'The human is laughing,' the thing described (the person) is both human and laughing. The referent is identical.
4
Test with a counterexample
Consider 'The horse is a tree.' The thing described by 'horse' and the thing described by 'tree' are different, so predication fails. Use the criterion to explain why.
This shows the practical use of the criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السادس في تحقيق نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع نقول : كل محمول نُسب إلى موضوع فإما بإيجاب أو سلب، فإن كان بإيجاب فسواء كان الموضوع شخصياً أو مهملاً أو محصوراً، فله شرائط في جانب الموضوع، وأخرى في جانب المحمول، فأما ما يرجع إلى الموضوع فإنا لا نريد به أن حقيقة الموضوع هي حقيقة المحمول وإلا لما صح الحمل في غير الأسماء المترادفة كقولنا : (الإنسان) (بشر) ونحوه، بل إنما نعني به أن الشيء الموصوف بكذا هو الشيء الموصوف بكذا، وسواء كانت حقيقة ذلك الشيء هي نفس حقيقة الموضوع أو المحمول أو حقيقتهما أو حقيقة ثالث غيرهما، أما الأول فكما في قولنا: الإنسان ضاحك فإن حقيقة ذلك الشيء الذي وصفناه بالإنسان والضاحك هي حقيقة الموضوع وهو الإنسان لا أن تلك الشيئية متقومة والإنسان عارض لها كما عرفت، وأما الثاني فكقولنا: الضاحك إنسان فإن حقيقة ذلك الشيء الموصوف بالموضوع وهو الضاحك هي حقيقة المحمول وهو الإنسان، والموضوع عارض له، وأما الثالث فكقولنا: الإنسان بشر فإن حقيقة ذلك الشيء الموصوف بالإنسان والبشر هي حقيقتيهما، لا أن حقيقة ذلك الشيء متقومة وحقيقة الإنسان والبشر عارض لها، والاختلاف بين الموضوع والمحمول ليس غير التسمية. وأما الرابع فكقولنا: الضاحك كاتب فإن حقيقة ذلك الشيء الموصوف بكونه ضاحكاً وكاتباً إنما هي نفس حقيقة الإنسان لا نفس حقيقة الضاحك والكاتب، بل الضاحك والكاتب عارضان لحقيقة ذلك الشيء، ولا نريد أيضاً أن كذا كذا أي أنه مهما دام كذا موصوفاً فهو كذا، بل ما هو موصوف بكذا فهو كذا، وسواء كان وصف الموضوع دائماً بدوامه كما في نسبة الحيوان إلى الإنسان أو غير دائم بدوامه كما في نسبة التغير إلى المتحرك، وتفريق البصر إلى الأبيض، ولا نريد أيضاً ما يصح أن يوصف بكذا بل ما هو موصوف بكذا بالفعل إما عيناً أو عقلاً
Chapter six: On verifying the relation of the predicate to the subject. We say: Every predicate attributed to a subject is either by affirmation or negation. If by affirmation, then whether the subject is individual, general, or quantified, it has conditions on the subject side and others on the predicate side. As for what relates to the subject: we do not mean by it that the essence of the subject is the essence of the predicate, otherwise predication would not be correct except in synonymous names, like our saying 'human' and 'mortal,' etc. Rather, we mean by it that the thing described by such is the thing described by such. And whether the essence of that thing is the same as the essence of the subject or the predicate, or both, or a third essence other than them. As for the first, it is like our saying: 'The human is laughing' – for the essence of that thing which we described as human and laughing is the essence of the subject, which is the human, not that that thinghood is subsistent and the human accidens to it, as you know. As for the second, it is like our saying: 'The laughing is human' – for the essence of that thing described by the subject (which is laughing) is the essence of the predicate (which is human), and the subject is accidens to it. As for the third, it is like our saying: 'The human is mortal' – for the essence of that thing described by human and mortal is both of their essences, not that the essence of that thing is subsistent and the essences of human and mortal are accidens to it, and the difference between subject and predicate is nothing but naming. As for the fourth, it is like our saying: 'The laughing is a writer' – for the essence of that thing described as laughing and writing is the very essence of the human, not the essence of laughing and writing, rather laughing and writing are accidents to the essence of that thing. And we also do not mean that 'such is such' means that as long as it is described as such, it is such, but rather that what is described as such is such. And whether the description of the subject is permanent as in the relation of animal to human, or not permanent as in the relation of change to the moving, and the distinction of sight to the white. And we also do not mean what is possible to be described as such, but rather what is actually described as such, either externally or mentally.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage opens the sixth chapter on verifying the relation of predicate to subject. The author first states that every predication is either affirmative or negative. For affirmative predication, whether the subject is individual, general, or quantified, there are conditions on both subject and predicate sides. The main point is that predication does not require identity of essences; it only requires that the thing described by the subject is the same thing described by the predicate. The author then lists four cases: (1) The essence of the thing is the same as the essence of the subject (e.g., 'Human is laughing' – the thing is essentially human, laughter is an accident). (2) The essence of the thing is the same as the essence of the predicate (e.g., 'The laughing is human' – the thing is essentially human, laughter is the subject term but accidental). (3) The essence of the thing is both essences together (e.g., 'Human is mortal' – both subject and predicate are essential to the thing, but the difference is nominal). (4) The essence of the thing is a third essence (e.g., 'The laughing is a writer' – the thing is essentially human, while both laughing and writing are accidents). The author also clarifies that predication does not mean 'as long as it is described, it is so' but rather 'what is described is so'; it does not require permanence; and it refers to actual description, not potential.

نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع
الحمل
الموضوع
المحمول
الشيء الموصوف

B) The thing described by the subject is the thing described by the predicate.
C) The subject term must be essential and the predicate accidental.
D) The predicate must always be permanent.

Key Takeaway
Predication in logic is about the identity of the referent, not the identity of the essences of the terms.

The Four Types of Essence Identity in Predication
Author enumerates four types of essence identity in predication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify an affirmative proposition into one of four types based on whether the described thing's essence matches the essence of the subject, the predicate, both, or a third essence.

Core Idea
The described thing's essence in predication can be that of the subject, predicate, both, or a third entity.

After establishing that predication requires identity of the described thing, the author explains four possibilities for what the essence of that described thing actually is. In the first type, the described thing's essence is the subject's essence (e.g., 'Human is laughing'); in the second, it is the predicate's essence (e.g., 'Laughing is human'); in the third, it is both (e.g., 'Human is mortal'); and in the fourth, it is a third essence (e.g., 'Laughing is writer'). This classification helps us understand the ontological commitment behind statements and is used in Islamic sciences like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four examples from the passage
The author gives four examples: (1) Human is laughing; (2) Laughing is human; (3) Human is mortal; (4) Laughing is writer. Note that the described thing in all cases is the same individual human.
Keep in mind that the described thing is the referent of both terms.
2
Identify the essence of the described thing in each example
For (1), the essence is human (the subject's essence). For (2), the essence is also human (the predicate's essence). For (3), the essence is both human and mortal (both essences). For (4), the essence is human (a third essence, since neither laughing nor writing is the essence).
The essence is what the described thing fundamentally is.
3
Link the pattern to the wording
In (1), the subject term is essential; in (2), the predicate term is essential; in (3), both terms are essential; in (4), neither term is essential – they are accidents.
This reveals the relationship between subject/predicate and the described thing's essence.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take 'This salt is haraam' (if the salt is impure). The described thing is the salt sample; its essence is saltiness, not haraamness. So this is type 1. If we reverse: 'The haraam substance is salt,' then the described thing's essence is salt (type 2).

Chapter six: On verifying the relation of the predicate to the subject. We say: Every predicate attributed to a subject is either by affirmation or negation. If by affirmation, then whether the subject is individual, general, or quantified, it has conditions on the subject side and others on the predicate side. As for what relates to the subject: we do not mean by it that the essence of the subject is the essence of the predicate, otherwise predication would not be correct except in synonymous names, like our saying 'human' and 'mortal,' etc. Rather, we mean by it that the thing described by such is the thing described by such. And whether the essence of that thing is the same as the essence of the subject or the predicate, or both, or a third essence other than them. As for the first, it is like our saying: 'The human is laughing' – for the essence of that thing which we described as human and laughing is the essence of the subject, which is the human, not that that thinghood is subsistent and the human accidens to it, as you know. As for the second, it is like our saying: 'The laughing is human' – for the essence of that thing described by the subject (which is laughing) is the essence of the predicate (which is human), and the subject is accidens to it. As for the third, it is like our saying: 'The human is mortal' – for the essence of that thing described by human and mortal is both of their essences, not that the essence of that thing is subsistent and the essences of human and mortal are accidens to it, and the difference between subject and predicate is nothing but naming. As for the fourth, it is like our saying: 'The laughing is a writer' – for the essence of that thing described as laughing and writing is the very essence of the human, not the essence of laughing and writing, rather laughing and writing are accidents to the essence of that thing. And we also do not mean that 'such is such' means that as long as it is described as such, it is such, but rather that what is described as such is such. And whether the description of the subject is permanent as in the relation of animal to human, or not permanent as in the relation of change to the moving, and the distinction of sight to the white. And we also do not mean what is possible to be described as such, but rather what is actually described as such, either externally or mentally.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage opens the sixth chapter on verifying the relation of predicate to subject. The author first states that every predication is either affirmative or negative. For affirmative predication, whether the subject is individual, general, or quantified, there are conditions on both subject and predicate sides. The main point is that predication does not require identity of essences; it only requires that the thing described by the subject is the same thing described by the predicate. The author then lists four cases: (1) The essence of the thing is the same as the essence of the subject (e.g., 'Human is laughing' – the thing is essentially human, laughter is an accident). (2) The essence of the thing is the same as the essence of the predicate (e.g., 'The laughing is human' – the thing is essentially human, laughter is the subject term but accidental). (3) The essence of the thing is both essences together (e.g., 'Human is mortal' – both subject and predicate are essential to the thing, but the difference is nominal). (4) The essence of the thing is a third essence (e.g., 'The laughing is a writer' – the thing is essentially human, while both laughing and writing are accidents). The author also clarifies that predication does not mean 'as long as it is described, it is so' but rather 'what is described is so'; it does not require permanence; and it refers to actual description, not potential.

Key Terms
نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع
الحمل
الموضوع
المحمول
الشيء الموصوف

Key Takeaway
In predication, the described thing may be essentially the subject, essentially the predicate, essentially both, or essentially a third entity – each reveals a different logical structure.

The True Meaning of 'Kull' in Universal Propositions
Clarification of 'كل' as distributive, not totality or universal concept.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three meanings of 'كل' (totality, universal concept, distributive individual) in universal propositions and apply the correct interpretation to avoid errors.

Core Idea
'كل' in a universal proposition does not mean 'all together' or 'the universal'—it means each and every individual.

When you see the word 'كل' in a logical statement like 'كل إنسان حيوان', your instinct might be to think of all humans as a group or the abstract concept of humanity. But the logician means something else: each single human individually. This lesson will clarify this crucial distinction using the author's own examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three candidates for the meaning of 'كل'.
The author lists three possible referents: the totality (الكل جملة), the universal concept (المعنى الكلي), and every single individual (كل واحد واحد). Your task is to notice that the first two are not the intended meaning in universal propositions.
2
Test with an affirmative example.
Take 'كل إنسان حيوان' (Every human is an animal). Check if it's true for the totality: is the group 'all humans' an animal? No. Check if it's true for the universal concept: is 'humanity' an animal? That's debatable but not the intent. The author says it means each individual human is an animal. Confirm that this matches the logical usage.
3
Test with a negative example.
Take 'لا واحد من الإنسان حجر' (No human is a stone). Same test: totality? No; universal concept? No; each individual? Yes. Use the author's additional examples to solidify the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ربما لم يكن الموضوع ملتفتاً إليه من حيث هو موجود في العين، هذا ما تشترك فيه الشخصيات والمهملات والمحصورات وما يخص المقول على الكل فإنا إذا قلنا (كل إنسان حيوان) مثلاً فإنا لا نريد به الكل جملة ولا المعنى الكلي، فليس معنى قولنا: (كل) إنسان كل الناس ولا الإنسان الكلي، فإنه ربما صدق على الجملة ما لا يصدق على الأفراد وكذا بالعكس، وكذا قد يصدق على الكلي ما لا يصدق على جزئياته وكذا بالعكس، أما الأول فكما في قولنا : (كل) موجود - أي الكل جملة - ليس بقديم ولا حادث فإنه وإن كان صادقاً لضرورة إن بعضه قديم وبعضه حادث، ولا يصدق على كل واحد من الأفراد حتى يقال كل واحد واحد من آحاد الموجود لا قديماً ولا حادثاً، وأما الثاني فكما في قولنا كل إنسان – إذا أردنا به الإنسان الكلي - صالح لاشتراك كثيرين فيه فإنه يصدق عليه ولا يصدق ذلك على جزئياته كـ زيد وعمرو) ونحوه.
وأما ما يرجع إلى المحمول فمختلف باختلاف القضايا المطلقة والموجهة، فإن كانت القضية موجهة زيد فيها ذكر الضرورة أو الإمكان كما عرف وإلا فلا زيادة على ما أشرنا إليه في جانب الموضوع، هذا إن كانت القضية موجبة، ومن قياسها يعرف حكم السالبة من شرط الموضوع والمحمول كانت شخصية أو مهملة أو محصورة على اختلاف أحوالها، فإنا إذا قلنا (ك) كذا ليس هو كذا لا نريد به أن حقيقة كذا ليس هو حقيقة كذا، بل ما هو موصوف بكذا ليس هو موصوف بكذا، ولا وصف أعم من لا حقيقة، ولا نريد به ما هو موصوف بكذا دائماً، بل ما هو موصوف بكذا كيف اتفق وهو أعم من الدوام، ولا ما يصح أن يوصف بكذا بل ما هو موصوف به بالفعل إما عيناً أو عقلاً.
وإن كانت القضية محصورة كليّة سالبة كما في قولنا: (لا) واحد من الإنسان حجراً) لم يكن المراد به كل الناس جملة، ولا الإنسان الكلي أيضاً، فإنه ربما صدق في السلب عن الجملة أو عن الكلي ما لا يصدق على الأجزاء أو الأفراد وكذا بالعكس، أما الأول فكما في قولنا: (لا) واحد من الحيوان إنساناً) فإنه يصدق إن أردنا جملة الحيوان ولا يصدق إن كان المراد به كل واحد واحد من آحاد الحيوان، والثاني فكما في قولنا: (لا) واحد من الإنسان مما يمتنع مفهومه من وقوع الشركة فإنه يصدق إن أردنا به الإنسان الكلي، ولا يصدق عند إرادة آحاده، لكن ربما تُوهم أن الحكم في السالبة الكلية يجب أن يدوم بدوام صفة الموضوع، بخلاف الموجبة الكلية، حتى إنه إذا قيل (لا) واحد من "ب" "أ") لو وجد بعض "ب" "أ" كان كاذباً، ولو قيل (كل) "ب" "أ") ووجد بعض ما هو "ب" وليس هو "أ" لم يكن كاذباً، وإنما يكذب (لا) واحد من "ب" "أ") إذا وجد بعض "ب" "أ" أن لو كان سلب "أ" دائماً عن كل ما يقال له "ب" وإلا فلا كذب؛ لجواز أن يكون الحكم بسلبه في وقت في غير دوام عن كل ما يقال له "ب" وما قيل من أنه لا قضية كلية إلا والمحمول فيها ضروري للموضوع ودائم ،له فغلط إذ كم من قضية كلية والحكم فيها على كل واحد واحد من آحاد الموضوع غير ضروري ولا دائم بل هو ممكن أن يكون وممكن أن لا يكون كما في قولنا: (كل إنسان كاتب بالفعل فإن الكتابة بالفعل غير ضرورية لكل واحد من آحاد الإنسان ولا دائمة بل ممكنة، وإن وُصف بالضرورة فليس معناه إلا أنه ضروري الإمكان لا ضروري النسبة.
نعم، إن كانت القضية كليّة والحكم فيها بالدوام، فلا تكون إلا بالضرورية؛ إذ الحكم الجزم بالدوام في الكليات ولا ضرورة محال بخلاف الجزئيات؛ إذ لا يمتنع أن يكون الدوام فيها اتفاقياً لا ضرورياً، كما في سلب السواد عن زيد وإيجابه لعمرو.
Perhaps the subject is not attended to insofar as it exists externally; this is what individual, indefinite, and quantified propositions share, and what pertains to the universal predication. For when we say 'Every human is an animal,' for example, we do not mean by it the totality nor the universal concept. So the meaning of our saying 'every human' is not 'all humans' nor 'the universal human.' For sometimes what is true of the totality is not true of the individuals, and vice versa; and likewise what is true of the universal may not be true of its particulars, and vice versa. As for the first: as in our saying 'Every existent—meaning the totality—is neither eternal nor originated,' which is true because necessarily some of it is eternal and some originated, but it is not true of each individual such that one would say 'every single individual existent is neither eternal nor originated.' As for the second: as in our saying 'Every human—if we mean the universal human—is fit for sharing by many,' which is true of it but not true of its particulars like Zayd and 'Amr and the like. As for what pertains to the predicate, it differs according to absolute and modal propositions. If the proposition is modal, the mention of necessity or possibility is added as known; otherwise there is no addition beyond what we indicated regarding the subject side. This if the proposition is affirmative; and by analogy the rule of the negative is known from the condition of the subject and predicate, whether it is individual, indefinite, or quantified according to its different cases. For when we say 'So-and-so is not such-and-such,' we do not mean that the reality of so-and-so is not the reality of such-and-such, but rather that what is described by so-and-so is not described by such-and-such, and there is no description more general than no reality; and we do not mean by it what is described by such-and-such perpetually, but what is described by such-and-such however it occurs—and this is more general than perpetuity—nor what is valid to be described by such-and-such, but what is described by it actually, either in external reality or in the mind. And if the proposition is a quantified universal negative, as in our saying 'No human is a stone,' the intended meaning is neither 'all humans' as a totality nor 'the universal human.' For sometimes what is true in negation about the totality or the universal is not true about the parts or individuals, and vice versa. As for the first: as in our saying 'No animal is human'—it is true if we intend the totality of animal, but not true if what is intended is every single individual of animal. As for the second: as in our saying 'No human is that whose concept prevents sharing'—it is true if we intend the universal human, but not true when intending its individuals. However, it might be imagined that in the universal negative the judgment must be perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject's attribute, unlike the universal affirmative, such that if one says 'No B is A,' if some B were A it would be false; and if one says 'Every B is A' and there exists some B that is not A, it would not be false. But 'No B is A' is only false if some B is A; that would be if the negation of A is perpetual concerning everything called B, otherwise it is not false, because it is permissible that the judgment of negation be at a time without perpetuity concerning everything called B. And what has been said—that there is no universal proposition except that the predicate therein is necessary for the subject and perpetual—this is a mistake. For how many a universal proposition has a judgment about each and every individual of the subject that is neither necessary nor perpetual, but is possible to be and possible not to be, as in our saying 'Every human is actually writing'—for actual writing is not necessary for each individual of humans, nor perpetual, but possible. And if it is described with necessity, its meaning is only that it is necessary in possibility, not necessary in relation. Yes, if the proposition is universal and the judgment therein is perpetual, then it can only be necessary; for a decisive judgment of perpetuity in universals without necessity is impossible, unlike particulars, for it is not impossible that perpetuity therein is accidental, not necessary, as in the negation of blackness from Zayd and its affirmation for 'Amr.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a detailed clarification of the meaning of universal quantifiers in categorical propositions, both affirmative and negative, and a refutation of the mistaken view that all universal propositions involve necessary predication. The author begins by noting that in a statement like 'Every human is an animal,' the word 'every' does not refer to the totality (all humans taken as a group) nor to the universal concept (the nature 'human' in the abstract). Instead, it indicates each individual. The author provides examples: 'Every existent (as a totality) is neither eternal nor originated' is true if we consider the total collection because some are eternal and some originated, but false if applied to each individual—since each individual is either eternal or originated. Similarly, 'Every human (as universal concept) is fit for sharing by many' is true of the universal human but false of particular humans like Zayd. The same distinctions apply to negative universals: 'No human is a stone' does not mean the totality or the universal concept. For instance, 'No animal is human' is true if we mean the totality of animals (since no animal species as a whole is human) but false if we mean each individual animal (since some animals are human). Next, the author turns to the predicate side. In absolute (non-modal) affirmative propositions, the predicate is simply affirmed without any temporal condition. In negative propositions, the negation is not of the reality (the essence) but of the description. For example, 'So-and-so is not such-and-such' means that what is described by so-and-so is not described by such-and-such, not that the essence of so-and-so is not the essence of such-and-such. Moreover, in a negative universal like 'No B is A,' the judgment does not require that the negation holds perpetually for every individual; it can be a temporary negation. The author then corrects a common mistake: some think that in universal negatives the predicate must be perpetually absent, while in universal affirmatives the predicate need not be perpetual. But the author says such a view is mistaken. As an example, 'Every human is actually writing' is not necessary or perpetual; it is possible. The universality here is in possibility, not necessity. Finally, the author acknowledges that if a universal proposition explicitly includes perpetuity (like 'always'), then it must be necessary; but in particular propositions, perpetuity can be accidental (e.g., 'Zayd is black' happening to be perpetual if Zayd is always black, but not necessary).

The universal quantifier 'كل' in a proposition refers to every single individual of the subject, not to the collective totality or the abstract universal nature.

Refuting the Myth of Necessary Predication in Universals
Refutation of the claim that all universal propositions have necessary or perpetual predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the mistaken view that all universal propositions require necessary or perpetual predication and refute it using examples of possible universal predication.

Core Idea
A universal proposition can be possible, not only necessary or perpetual—e.g., 'Every human is actually writing' is true by possibility.

Some logicians claimed that in a universal proposition, the predicate must be necessarily and perpetually true of the subject. The author vigorously denies this, showing that many universal judgments are about contingent matters. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
State the mistaken claim.
The author reports the view: 'لا قضية كلية إلا والمحمول فيها ضروري للموضوع ودائم له' (There is no universal proposition except that the predicate is necessary for the subject and perpetual). Identify this claim in the Arabic.
2
Find the author's counterexample.
The author gives the example 'كل إنسان كاتب بالفعل' (Every human is actually writing). Analyze: Is this universal? Yes. Is the predicate necessary? No, because some humans are not writing at the moment. Is it perpetual? No. The predicate is only possible.
3
Understand the distinction between necessity-in-possibility and necessity-in-relation.
The author explains that if the predicate is described as necessary, it might mean 'ضروري الإمكان' (necessary in possibility) not 'ضروري النسبة' (necessary in relation). For example, it is possible for any human to be writing, but it is not necessarily the case that each human is writing.
4
Apply to negative universals.
The author also addresses negative universals: 'لا واحد من ب أ' (No B is A). The mistaken view says this must be perpetually false if any B is A. But the author clarifies that the negation could be temporary. Think of an example: 'لا واحد من الطلاب حاضر اليوم' (No student is present today) might be true now but not forever.

وأما ما يرجع إلى المحمول فمختلف باختلاف القضايا المطلقة والموجهة، فإن كانت القضية موجهة زيد فيها ذكر الضرورة أو الإمكان كما عرف وإلا فلا زيادة على ما أشرنا إليه في جانب الموضوع، هذا إن كانت القضية موجبة، ومن قياسها يعرف حكم السالبة من شرط الموضوع والمحمول كانت شخصية أو مهملة أو محصورة على اختلاف أحوالها، فإنا إذا قلنا (ك) كذا ليس هو كذا لا نريد به أن حقيقة كذا ليس هو حقيقة كذا، بل ما هو موصوف بكذا ليس هو موصوف بكذا، ولا وصف أعم من لا حقيقة، ولا نريد به ما هو موصوف بكذا دائماً، بل ما هو موصوف بكذا كيف اتفق وهو أعم من الدوام، ولا ما يصح أن يوصف بكذا بل ما هو موصوف به بالفعل إما عيناً أو عقلاً.
وإن كانت القضية محصورة كليّة سالبة كما في قولنا: (لا) واحد من الإنسان حجراً) لم يكن المراد به كل الناس جملة، ولا الإنسان الكلي أيضاً، فإنه ربما صدق في السلب عن الجملة أو عن الكلي ما لا يصدق على الأجزاء أو الأفراد وكذا بالعكس، أما الأول فكما في قولنا: (لا) واحد من الحيوان إنساناً) فإنه يصدق إن أردنا جملة الحيوان ولا يصدق إن كان المراد به كل واحد واحد من آحاد الحيوان، والثاني فكما في قولنا: (لا) واحد من الإنسان مما يمتنع مفهومه من وقوع الشركة فإنه يصدق إن أردنا به الإنسان الكلي، ولا يصدق عند إرادة آحاده، لكن ربما تُوهم أن الحكم في السالبة الكلية يجب أن يدوم بدوام صفة الموضوع، بخلاف الموجبة الكلية، حتى إنه إذا قيل (لا) واحد من "ب" "أ") لو وجد بعض "ب" "أ" كان كاذباً، ولو قيل (كل) "ب" "أ") ووجد بعض ما هو "ب" وليس هو "أ" لم يكن كاذباً، وإنما يكذب (لا) واحد من "ب" "أ") إذا وجد بعض "ب" "أ" أن لو كان سلب "أ" دائماً عن كل ما يقال له "ب" وإلا فلا كذب؛ لجواز أن يكون الحكم بسلبه في وقت في غير دوام عن كل ما يقال له "ب" وما قيل من أنه لا قضية كلية إلا والمحمول فيها ضروري للموضوع ودائم ،له فغلط إذ كم من قضية كلية والحكم فيها على كل واحد واحد من آحاد الموضوع غير ضروري ولا دائم بل هو ممكن أن يكون وممكن أن لا يكون كما في قولنا: (كل إنسان كاتب بالفعل فإن الكتابة بالفعل غير ضرورية لكل واحد من آحاد الإنسان ولا دائمة بل ممكنة، وإن وُصف بالضرورة فليس معناه إلا أنه ضروري الإمكان لا ضروري النسبة.
نعم، إن كانت القضية كليّة والحكم فيها بالدوام، فلا تكون إلا بالضرورية؛ إذ الحكم الجزم بالدوام في الكليات ولا ضرورة محال بخلاف الجزئيات؛ إذ لا يمتنع أن يكون الدوام فيها اتفاقياً لا ضرورياً، كما في سلب السواد عن زيد وإيجابه لعمرو.
Perhaps the subject is not attended to insofar as it exists externally; this is what individual, indefinite, and quantified propositions share, and what pertains to the universal predication. For when we say 'Every human is an animal,' for example, we do not mean by it the totality nor the universal concept. So the meaning of our saying 'every human' is not 'all humans' nor 'the universal human.' For sometimes what is true of the totality is not true of the individuals, and vice versa; and likewise what is true of the universal may not be true of its particulars, and vice versa. As for the first: as in our saying 'Every existent—meaning the totality—is neither eternal nor originated,' which is true because necessarily some of it is eternal and some originated, but it is not true of each individual such that one would say 'every single individual existent is neither eternal nor originated.' As for the second: as in our saying 'Every human—if we mean the universal human—is fit for sharing by many,' which is true of it but not true of its particulars like Zayd and 'Amr and the like. As for what pertains to the predicate, it differs according to absolute and modal propositions. If the proposition is modal, the mention of necessity or possibility is added as known; otherwise there is no addition beyond what we indicated regarding the subject side. This if the proposition is affirmative; and by analogy the rule of the negative is known from the condition of the subject and predicate, whether it is individual, indefinite, or quantified according to its different cases. For when we say 'So-and-so is not such-and-such,' we do not mean that the reality of so-and-so is not the reality of such-and-such, but rather that what is described by so-and-so is not described by such-and-such, and there is no description more general than no reality; and we do not mean by it what is described by such-and-such perpetually, but what is described by such-and-such however it occurs—and this is more general than perpetuity—nor what is valid to be described by such-and-such, but what is described by it actually, either in external reality or in the mind. And if the proposition is a quantified universal negative, as in our saying 'No human is a stone,' the intended meaning is neither 'all humans' as a totality nor 'the universal human.' For sometimes what is true in negation about the totality or the universal is not true about the parts or individuals, and vice versa. As for the first: as in our saying 'No animal is human'—it is true if we intend the totality of animal, but not true if what is intended is every single individual of animal. As for the second: as in our saying 'No human is that whose concept prevents sharing'—it is true if we intend the universal human, but not true when intending its individuals. However, it might be imagined that in the universal negative the judgment must be perpetual with the perpetuity of the subject's attribute, unlike the universal affirmative, such that if one says 'No B is A,' if some B were A it would be false; and if one says 'Every B is A' and there exists some B that is not A, it would not be false. But 'No B is A' is only false if some B is A; that would be if the negation of A is perpetual concerning everything called B, otherwise it is not false, because it is permissible that the judgment of negation be at a time without perpetuity concerning everything called B. And what has been said—that there is no universal proposition except that the predicate therein is necessary for the subject and perpetual—this is a mistake. For how many a universal proposition has a judgment about each and every individual of the subject that is neither necessary nor perpetual, but is possible to be and possible not to be, as in our saying 'Every human is actually writing'—for actual writing is not necessary for each individual of humans, nor perpetual, but possible. And if it is described with necessity, its meaning is only that it is necessary in possibility, not necessary in relation. Yes, if the proposition is universal and the judgment therein is perpetual, then it can only be necessary; for a decisive judgment of perpetuity in universals without necessity is impossible, unlike particulars, for it is not impossible that perpetuity therein is accidental, not necessary, as in the negation of blackness from Zayd and its affirmation for 'Amr.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a detailed clarification of the meaning of universal quantifiers in categorical propositions, both affirmative and negative, and a refutation of the mistaken view that all universal propositions involve necessary predication. The author begins by noting that in a statement like 'Every human is an animal,' the word 'every' does not refer to the totality (all humans taken as a group) nor to the universal concept (the nature 'human' in the abstract). Instead, it indicates each individual. The author provides examples: 'Every existent (as a totality) is neither eternal nor originated' is true if we consider the total collection because some are eternal and some originated, but false if applied to each individual—since each individual is either eternal or originated. Similarly, 'Every human (as universal concept) is fit for sharing by many' is true of the universal human but false of particular humans like Zayd. The same distinctions apply to negative universals: 'No human is a stone' does not mean the totality or the universal concept. For instance, 'No animal is human' is true if we mean the totality of animals (since no animal species as a whole is human) but false if we mean each individual animal (since some animals are human). Next, the author turns to the predicate side. In absolute (non-modal) affirmative propositions, the predicate is simply affirmed without any temporal condition. In negative propositions, the negation is not of the reality (the essence) but of the description. For example, 'So-and-so is not such-and-such' means that what is described by so-and-so is not described by such-and-such, not that the essence of so-and-so is not the essence of such-and-such. Moreover, in a negative universal like 'No B is A,' the judgment does not require that the negation holds perpetually for every individual; it can be a temporary negation. The author then corrects a common mistake: some think that in universal negatives the predicate must be perpetually absent, while in universal affirmatives the predicate need not be perpetual. But the author says such a view is mistaken. As an example, 'Every human is actually writing' is not necessary or perpetual; it is possible. The universality here is in possibility, not necessity. Finally, the author acknowledges that if a universal proposition explicitly includes perpetuity (like 'always'), then it must be necessary; but in particular propositions, perpetuity can be accidental (e.g., 'Zayd is black' happening to be perpetual if Zayd is always black, but not necessary).

المعنى الكلي
القضية الكلية

A universal proposition does not automatically entail necessary or perpetual predication; it can be contingent and still be a valid universal.

Universal Judgments: Not All Are Necessary
Author refutes the claim that all universal judgments are necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a universal proposition is necessary or contingent by checking the perpetuity condition in the judgment.

Core Idea
Not every universal proposition is necessary; some are merely possible.

A common misconception holds that every universal proposition (e.g., 'Every B is A') must have a predicate that is necessary and perpetual for the subject. The author of this passage challenges that view, arguing that many universal judgments are contingent. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise logical analysis in Islamic sciences, where modal confusion can undermine arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misconception
Find the statement the author refutes: 'there is no universal proposition except that the predicate in it is necessary for the subject and perpetual.' Recognize that this is the target of critique.
2
Examine the counterexample
Look at the author's example: 'Every human is actually a writer.' This is a universal affirmative proposition, yet the predicate is not necessary or perpetual—it is possible.
3
Distinguish necessary from contingent universals
The author clarifies that a universal proposition only becomes necessary if it explicitly asserts perpetuity (e.g., 'Every human is always a writer'). Otherwise, it is contingent. Note the difference between 'necessary in possibility' and 'necessary in relation'.
The author's phrase 'ضروري الإمكان' means 'necessary in possibility', i.e., the possibility itself is necessary, not the predicate's actual persistence.
4
Apply the criterion
For any universal proposition, check if the judgment includes a perpetuity condition. If it does, the proposition is necessary; if not, it is contingent. Practice with examples from Islamic sciences.

نعم، إن كانت القضية كليّة والحكم فيها بالدوام، فلا تكون إلا بالضرورية؛ إذ الحكم الجزم بالدوام في الكليات ولا ضرورة محال بخلاف الجزئيات؛ إذ لا يمتنع أن يكون الدوام فيها اتفاقياً لا ضرورياً، كما في سلب السواد عن زيد وإيجابه لعمرو.
And it is the subtleties of realities: Part One / Article Three: On Parmenides. The animal, and the second as in our saying: 'No one of man [is something] whose concept precludes participation', for it is true if we intend the universal man, and not true when intending its individuals. But perhaps it is imagined that the judgment in the universal negative must be perpetual with the perpetuity of the attribute of the subject, contrary to the universal affirmative, such that if it is said 'No B is A', if some B is A it would be false, and if it is said 'Every B is A', and some B is not A, it would not be false. Rather, 'No B is A' is only false if some B is A, provided that the negation of A is perpetual over everything called B, otherwise it is not false, because the judgment of its negation might be at a time without perpetuity over everything called B. And what is said that there is no universal proposition except that the predicate in it is necessary for the subject and perpetual is a mistake, for how many universal propositions the judgment in which over each individual of the subject is neither necessary nor perpetual, but rather possible to be and possible not to be, as in our saying: 'Every human is actually a writer', for writing actually is not necessary for each individual of humans nor perpetual, but possible. And if it is described with necessity, its meaning is only necessary in possibility, not necessary in relation. Yes, if the proposition is universal and the judgment in it is with perpetuity, then it is only a necessary proposition; for the categorical judgment of perpetuity in universals without necessity is impossible, unlike particulars, for it is not impossible that perpetuity in them be accidental not necessary, as in the negation of blackness from Zayd and its affirmation to 'Amr.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a common misconception about universal judgments. The author argues that not all universal propositions entail necessity or perpetuity of the predicate for the subject. He begins by discussing universal negative propositions, noting that their truth condition is more subtle than commonly thought. He then directly attacks the claim that every universal proposition has a necessary predicate, providing the counterexample 'Every human is actually a writer' — a universal affirmative that is contingent, not necessary. He clarifies that if a universal proposition explicitly asserts perpetuity (e.g., 'Every human is always a writer'), then it must be necessary, but many universal propositions do not assert perpetuity and are merely possible. This distinction is crucial for logical analysis, as it prevents conflating modal and non-modal judgments. The author uses the logical variables ب and أ to represent terms, employing standard Arabic logical notation.
