Definition of Contradiction and Its Essential Condition
Author defines contradiction with the essential condition of mutual entailment by essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish genuine contradiction from accidental co-occurrence using the 'by itself' condition.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires that truth of one proposition by itself entails falsehood of the other.

In logic, not every pair of affirmative and negative propositions is contradictory. The author defines contradiction with a critical condition: the entailment must be by the proposition's own essence, not through an external link. This lesson unpacks that condition through examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find the author's definition of contradiction in the passage. Note the key phrase: 'difference of two propositions in quality in such a way that from the truth of one of them by itself the falsehood of the other necessarily follows, and from falsehood truth.'
2
Extract the essential condition
Highlight the term 'by itself' (li-dhatiha). This condition excludes cases where the entailment occurs through an intermediary or by accident.
The author later contrasts this with cases where falsehood follows via the converse of the contradictory.
3
Analyze the counterexamples
The author gives two pairs that are NOT contradictories: 'No human is a stone' & 'Some stone is human' (entailment via converse) and 'Every human is an animal' & 'No horse is an animal' (accidental). For each, explain why the 'by itself' condition fails.
The first fails because the falsehood follows through aks al-naqid, not directly. The second fails because the truth values do not entail each other at all.
4
Apply the test
Given a new pair of propositions, check if they meet the definition. For example: 'Every intoxicant is haram' and 'Some intoxicant is not haram' – do they contradict? (Hint: they differ in quantity and quality, but check the entailment.)
True contradiction requires both directions of entailment by themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع في التناقض. والتناقض هو اختلاف القضيتين بالكيف على وجه يلزم من صدق إحداهما لذاتها كذب الأخرى، ومن الكذب الصدق، لا كما في قولنا: (لا شيء من الإنسان حجراً) (بعض الحجر إنسان) (كل إنسان حيوان) (لا واحد من الفرس حيوان)، فإنه وإن لزم كذب (بعض الحجر إنسان) من صدق (لا شيء من الإنسان حجراً) فليس لذات ما فرض صادقاً، بل لكون الكذب عكس النقيض بالذات للصادق، إذ النقيض بالذات هو (بعض الإنسان حجر)، و(بعض الحجر إنسان) إنما هو عكسه ولازم له، وأما صدق إيجاب الحيوان للإنسان وكذب سلبه عن الفرس، فليس إلا بحكم الاتفاق وخصوص المادة، لا أن أحدهما لازم للآخر لا لذاته ولا بواسطة، ولهذا لو قدر كذب قولنا: الإنسان حيوان لما لزم صدق الفرس ليس بحيوان، وكذا لو قدر صدق الفرس ليس حيواناً لما كذب الإنسان حيوان. فإذن التكاذب على الوجه المخصوص على ما ذكرناه.
Chapter Seven on Contradiction. And contradiction is the difference of two propositions in quality in such a way that from the truth of one of them by itself the falsehood of the other necessarily follows, and from falsehood truth, not as in our statement: 'No human is a stone,' 'Some stone is human,' 'Every human is an animal,' 'No horse is an animal,' for although the falsehood of 'Some stone is human' follows from the truth of 'No human is a stone,' it is not by itself [the truth] that is assumed true, but because the falsehood is the converse of the contradictory by itself of the true, for the contradictory by itself is 'Some human is stone,' and 'Some stone is human' is only its converse and necessary consequence. And as for the truth of the affirmative of animal for human and the falsehood of its negative for horse, it is only by coincidence and specificity of matter, not that one is a necessary consequence of the other either by itself or by means of a mediator. Therefore, if we assumed the falsehood of our statement 'Human is animal' it would not necessitate the truth of 'Horse is not animal,' and likewise if we assumed the truth of 'Horse is not animal' it would not falsify 'Human is animal.' Thus, the mutual falsity is in the specific manner mentioned.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage defines the logical relation of contradiction (al-tanaqud). The author begins by stating that contradiction is the difference between two propositions in quality (affirmative vs. negative) such that the truth of one by itself (li-dhatiha) necessarily entails the falsehood of the other, and vice versa. The key condition is 'by itself' (li-dhatiha), meaning the entailment must be intrinsic, not accidental. To illustrate what does NOT count as true contradiction, the author provides examples: 'No human is a stone' and 'Some stone is human' are not true contradictories because the falsehood here follows via the converse of the contradictory (aks al-naqid), not directly. Similarly, the pair 'Every human is an animal' and 'No horse is an animal' are not contradictories because their truth values do not entail each other by themselves—it is merely coincidental that one is true and the other false. The author emphasizes that for genuine contradiction, the entailment must be without any intermediary and must hold in both directions: from truth to falsehood and from falsehood to truth. This definition is foundational for logical debates, as it prevents sophistical objections based on accidental co-occurrence.

Key Terms
التناقض
لذاتها
عكس النقيض

Three Subject-Related Causes of Apparent Contradiction
Author enumerates three ways the subject term may be misinterpreted, causing false contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the three subject-related causes (homonymy, part-whole ambiguity, condition neglect) is responsible for an apparent contradiction between two propositions.

Core Idea
Apparent contradictions often vanish when the subject term is not the same in meaning.

When two propositions appear to contradict, we must first examine whether the subject term truly refers to the same thing. The author of this passage teaches us three subtle ways the subject may differ: through homonymy, through the whole-versus-part distinction, and through hidden conditions. Mastering these distinctions is essential for resolving apparent contradictions in Islamic textual arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contradictory propositions
Find the pair of statements that seem to contradict each other. Note the exact wording, especially the subject term.
The passages give examples like 'The eye is seeing' and 'The eye is not seeing'.
2
Check for homonymy in the subject term
Determine if the subject word has multiple distinct meanings. If the word is used with different meanings in the two statements, the contradiction is only apparent.
Example: 'al-ayn' can mean the eye (organ), a spring, the pointer of a scale, etc.
3
Check for part-whole ambiguity (conformity vs. inclusion)
See if the subject term can refer to the whole thing or to a part of it. If one proposition affirms something of the whole and the other denies it of a part, they can both be true.
Example: 'The sphere is round' (whole) and 'The sphere is not round' (part) are both true.
4
Check for neglected conditions
Look for a condition (e.g., 'as long as it is moving') that might be implicitly present in one proposition but absent in the other. If the condition differs, the subjects are effectively different.
Example: 'The moving thing is changing' (while moving) and 'The moving thing is not changing' (while at rest) can both be true.

أما ما يرجع إلى الموضوع فبأن يكون الموضوع في إحداهما غير الموضوع في الأخرى ويظن اتحاده، وذلك بأسباب: الأول: أن يكون الاسم مشتركاً كقولنا: (العين باصرة) (العين ليست باصرة) فإنه لا يمتنع اجتماعهما في الصدق إن أريد بما أثبت البصر له العين بمعنى الجارحة المخصوصة، وما نفي عنه البصر العين الجارية أو عين الميزان أو الركبة أو الذهب، وكذا لا يمتنع اجتماعهما في الكذب لو عكس الحال، وإنما يتكاذبان على الوجه المخصوص لو توارد الإيجاب والسلب على موضوع واحد في المعنى لا في الاسم. الثاني: اتحاد الاسم واختلاف مدلوله بالمطابقة والتضمن كقولنا: (الفلك مستدير) (الفلك ليس بمستدير)، فإنه لا يمتنع اجتماعهما في الصدق إن أريد بلفظ الفلك كله في جانب الإيجاب وفي السلب بعضه، وكذا لا يمتنع اجتماعهما في الكذب أن لو عكس الحال، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان التوارد بالإيجاب والسلب على الكل أو البعض. الثالث: اختلاف الموضوع بسبب إهمال شرطه في إحدى القضيتين واعتباره في الأخرى كقولنا: (المتحرك متغير) (المتحرك ليس بمتغير)، فإنهما يصدقان إن نظر إلى في الموجبة إلى حالة التحرك، وفي السالبة إلى حالة عدمه، ويكذبان إن كان الأمر بالعكس، وعلى نحوه قولنا: (المجنوب يسعل) (المجنوب ليس يسعل)، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان السلب وارداً على ما ورد عليه الإيجاب بشرطه بأن يقال: المتحرك ما دام متحركاً متغير) المتحرك ما دام متحركاً ليس بمتغير).
As for what pertains to the subject, it is that the subject in one [proposition] is not the subject in the other, and it is thought to be the same, and that is due to causes: First: that the name is homonymous, like our statement: 'The eye is seeing' and 'The eye is not seeing,' for it is not impossible for both to be true if by what the vision is affirmed of is meant the eye as the specific organ, and what vision is negated of is the flowing water-eye or the eye of the balance or the knee or gold, and likewise it is not impossible for both to be false if the situation is reversed. They only truly contradict if the affirmation and negation apply to one subject in meaning, not in name. Second: the unity of the name and the difference of its meaning by conformity and inclusion, like our statement: 'The sphere is round' and 'The sphere is not round,' for it is not impossible for both to be true if by the term 'sphere' the whole is meant in the affirmative and a part of it in the negative, and likewise it is not impossible for both to be false if the situation is reversed. They only contradict if the affirmation and negation apply to the whole or the part. Third: the difference of the subject due to neglecting its condition in one proposition and considering it in the other, like our statement: 'The moving thing is changing' and 'The moving thing is not changing,' for both can be true if, in the affirmative, one looks at the state of motion, and in the negative, at the state of rest, and both can be false if the opposite. Similarly, our statement: 'The person with pleurisy coughs' and 'The person with pleurisy does not cough.' They only contradict if the negation applies to what the affirmation applied to with its condition, such that one says: 'The moving thing, as long as it is moving, is changing' and 'The moving thing, as long as it is moving, is not changing.'
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage classifies the ways an apparent contradiction can arise due to issues with the subject term of the propositions. The author lists three causes. The first is homonymy (al-ism al-mushtarak), where the same word has multiple meanings. For example, 'al-ayn' can mean the eye (organ), a spring of water, the scale's pointer, the knee, or gold. So the statement 'The eye is seeing' and 'The eye is not seeing' can both be true if one refers to the organ and the other to the spring. True contradiction requires that the subject be identical in meaning, not just in word. The second cause is when the term has one name but its meaning differs by conformity (al-mutabaqa) and inclusion (al-tadammun). For instance, 'al-falak' can refer to the entire celestial sphere or a part of it. Thus 'The sphere is round' and 'The sphere is not round' can both be true if the first affirms roundness of the whole and the second denies it of a part. True contradiction arises only if both apply to the same whole or the same part. The third cause is neglecting a condition (shart) attached to the subject in one proposition while considering it in the other. For example, 'The moving thing is changing' and 'The moving thing is not changing' can both be true if the first considers the thing while it is moving and the second while it is not moving (at rest). To have genuine contradiction, the condition must be unified, e.g., 'The moving thing, as long as it is moving, is changing' versus 'The moving thing, as long as it is moving, is not changing.' The example of a person with pleurisy (al-majnub) coughing follows the same logic. This classification teaches the student to carefully examine the subject's identity, intension, and conditions before judging whether two propositions truly contradict each other.

الاسم المشترك
المطابقة والتضمن
الشرط

D) 'The fasting person is rewarded' and 'The fasting person is not rewarded' due to different intentions.

Key Takeaway
True contradiction requires that the subject be identical in meaning, not merely in wording; homonymy, part-whole ambiguity, and hidden conditions can make subjects different.

Predicate-Related Causes of Apparent Contradiction
Author lists predicate-related causes of apparent contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three predicate-related causes that make two propositions appear contradictory but are not: homonymy, potential/actual distinction, and relational attribution.

Core Idea
A predicate word may hide different meanings; apparent contradiction dissolves when you check homonymy, potential/actual, or relation.

When two propositions seem to contradict each other, we must examine the predicate. The author lists three causes that make the predicate actually different: homonymy, potential/actual distinction, and relational terms. This lesson will help you recognise each cause so you can avoid false contradictions in argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
Find the pair of statements that appear to contradict each other. Write them down and note the predicate used in both.
In the example: 'The one who undertakes the acceptance side in a sale is the buyer' and '...is not the buyer'.
2
Check the predicate word for homonymy
Does the predicate word have multiple distinct meanings? If so, the two statements can both be true if each uses a different meaning.
Example: 'al-mushtari' can mean 'buyer' or 'planet Jupiter'.
3
Check for potential vs. actual distinction
Could the predicate be true in one sense (potential) and false in another (actual)? If so, the contradiction is only apparent.
Example: 'Wine in the cask is intoxicating' (potentially) vs. 'Wine in the cask is not intoxicating' (actually).
4
Check if the predicate is relational
Does the predicate require a relation to something else? If so, the affirmation and negation may apply to different relations.
Example: 'Zayd is a father' (of some child) vs. 'Zayd is not a father' (of another child). Text cuts off here.
5
Determine if they truly contradict
Only if the predicate is exactly the same in meaning and aspect do we have a real contradiction. Otherwise, the propositions are compatible.
The author stresses: 'They only truly contradict if what is affirmed is the same as what is negated'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يرجع إلى المحمول فبأن يكون المحمول في إحداهما غير المحمول في الأخرى ونظن اتحاده، وذلك أيضاً بأسباب: الأول: أن يكون الاسم مشتركاً كما في قولنا: (المتولي لطرف القبول في البيع هو المشتري) (المتولي لطرف القبول في البيع ليس هو المشتري) فإنهما يصدقان إن أريد بالمشتري في الموجبة ما هو المقابل للبائع، وفي السالبة المشتري الذي هو الكوكب، ويكذبان إن عكس الأمر، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان ما أثبت هو نفس ما نفي، وما نفي هو نفس ما أثبت وفي معنى هذا ما اتحد اسمه واختلف معناه بالقوة والفعل كقولنا: الخمر في الدنّ مسكر) (الخمر في الدن ليست مسكرة) فإنهما يصدقان إن أريد أنها مسكرة بالقوة ولا مسكرة بالفعل، ويكذبان بالعكس، وعلى هذا النحو قولنا: (الصبي عارف بالأشكال الهندسية) (الصبي ليس عارفاً بالأشكال الهندسية، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان التوارد بالنفي والإثبات على أحد الطرفين - أعني القوة والفعل - وفي معنى هذا أن يكون الخبر مضافاً لم يتوارد السلب والإيجاب عليه من جهة ما هو مضاف إليه كقولنا : زيد) (أب) (زيد ليس باب فإنهما
As for what pertains to the predicate, it is that the predicate in one [proposition] is not the predicate in the other, and we think it is the same, and that is also due to causes: First: that the name is homonymous, as in our statement: 'The one who undertakes the acceptance side in a sale is the buyer' and 'The one who undertakes the acceptance side in a sale is not the buyer,' for both can be true if by 'buyer' in the affirmative is meant the one who is the counterpart of the seller, and in the negative the planet Jupiter (al-mushtari), and both can be false if the opposite. They only truly contradict if what is affirmed is the same as what is negated, and what is negated is the same as what is affirmed. And of similar meaning is what has one name but its meaning differs in potentiality and actuality, like our statement: 'Wine in the cask is intoxicating' and 'Wine in the cask is not intoxicating,' for both can be true if it is meant that it is intoxicating potentially but not actually, and both can be false conversely. And of this type is our statement: 'The boy knows geometric shapes' and 'The boy does not know geometric shapes,' and they only contradict if the affirmation and negation apply to one of the two aspects — namely potentiality and actuality. And of similar meaning is that the predicate is a relative [term]; the negation and affirmation do not apply to it from the aspect of what it is related to, like our statement: 'Zayd is a father' and 'Zayd is not a father,' for they...
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of false contradictions, now focusing on the predicate (al-mahmul). The author gives three sub-cases within the first cause (homonymy) and introduces two more distinct causes. The first predicate-related cause is homonymy: the same word has different meanings. The example uses 'al-mushtari' which can mean 'buyer' or the planet Jupiter. So 'The one who undertakes the acceptance in a sale is the buyer' and '...is not the buyer' can both be true if one refers to the person and the other to the planet. True contradiction requires that the affirmed predicate be exactly the same as the negated one. The second sub-case is when the term is the same but its meaning differs between potentiality (bil-quwwa) and actuality (bil-fi'l). For instance, 'Wine in the cask is intoxicating' and '...is not intoxicating' can both be true if the first means potentially intoxicating and the second means not actually intoxicating. The same applies to 'The boy knows geometric shapes' – he may know potentially but not actually. The third sub-case, introduced at the end, is when the predicate is a relative term (mudaf). The example 'Zayd is a father' and 'Zayd is not a father' can both be true if Zayd is a father to some children but not to others, though the text cuts off. The author stresses that for genuine contradiction, the affirmation and negation must apply to the same aspect. This passage teaches the careful identification of predicate meaning, especially in cases of homonymy, potential vs. actual, and relational contexts.

Identifying False Contradictions from the Subject
Author enumerates two causes of fallacy from the subject: homonymy and omission of condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two seemingly contradictory propositions do not truly contradict due to homonymy or omitted conditions in the subject.

Core Idea
Two propositions that look contradictory may both be true if the subject term has different meanings or conditions.

In logic, contradiction requires that two propositions cannot both be true or both false. However, as the author shows, apparent contradictions often arise from ambiguity in the subject term. This lesson unpacks two subtle ways this happens: when the same word signifies different things (by whole or part) and when a condition of the subject is neglected. Mastering these distinctions is crucial for sound reasoning in Islamic legal debates, where terms like 'prayer' or 'travel' carry multiple meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
Locate the affirmative and negative statements given. Write them side by side. Example: 'The sphere is round' and 'The sphere is not round.'
2
Check for homonymy in the subject
Ask: Does the subject word have more than one meaning? Specifically, consider the distinction between meaning by agreement (the whole) and meaning by implication (a part). If the first proposition uses the whole and the second uses a part, both can be true.
This is called ittihad al-ism (unity of the name with different denotations).
3
Check for omitted conditions
Determine whether a condition (e.g., time, state) applies to the subject in one proposition but is ignored in the other. For example, 'the moving thing is changing' is true during motion; 'the moving thing is not changing' is true when at rest. The condition 'during motion' is omitted in the second.
The author calls this ihmal shartihi (neglecting its condition).
4
Decide if true contradiction occurs
True contradiction requires that the subject and predicate be applied to exactly the same thing under the same conditions. If the subject differs in meaning or condition, the propositions are not contradictory—they can both be true (or both false).
Only when the subject is identical in every respect will one be true and the other false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: اتحاد الاسم واختلاف مدلوله بالمطابقة والتضمن كقولنا: (الفلك مستدير) (الفلك ليس بمستدير)، فإنه لا يمتنع اجتماعهما في الصدق إن أريد بلفظ الفلك كله في جانب الإيجاب وفي السلب بعضه، وكذا لا يمتنع اجتماعهما في الكذب أن لو عكس الحال، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان التوارد بالإيجاب والسلب على الكل أو البعض.
الثالث اختلاف الموضوع بسبب إهمال شرطه في إحدى القضيتين واعتباره في الأخرى كقولنا: (المتحرك متغير) (المتحرك ليس بمتغير، فإنهما يصدقان إن نظر إلى في الموجبة إلى حالة التحرك، وفي السالبة إلى حالة عدمه، ويكذبان إن كان الأمر بالعكس، وعلى نحوه قولنا: (المجنوب يسعل) (المجنوب ليس يسعل، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان السلب وارداً على ما ورد عليه الإيجاب بشرطه بأن يقال المتحرك ما دام متحركاً متغير) المتحرك ما دام متحركاً ليس بمتغير).
Second: Unity of the name and difference of its denotation by agreement and implication, as in our statement: 'The sphere is round' and 'The sphere is not round.' For it is not impossible for both to be true if by the word 'sphere' is meant the whole in the affirmative and a part of it in the negative. Similarly, it is not impossible for both to be false if the situation is reversed. They only truly contradict if the affirmation and negation apply to the whole or to the part. Third: Difference of the subject due to neglecting its condition in one proposition and considering it in the other, as in our statement: 'The moving thing is changing' and 'The moving thing is not changing.' For they can both be true if in the affirmative one looks at the state of motion, and in the negative at the state of its absence. They can both be false if the situation is reversed. Similarly, our statement: 'The person with pleurisy coughs' and 'The person with pleurisy does not cough.' They only truly contradict if the negation applies to what the affirmation applied, with its condition, as in saying: 'The moving thing, as long as it is moving, is changing' and 'The moving thing, as long as it is moving, is not changing.'
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage defines two sources of fallacy in contradictory propositions that arise from the subject term. The first (listed as the second overall cause in the text) is when the same word is used in two different senses in the affirmative and negative propositions, based on the logical distinction between meaning by agreement (mutabaqa) and meaning by implication (tadammun). For example, 'the sphere' can refer to the whole sphere in one proposition and to a part of it in the other. The second source (the third overall cause) is when a condition attached to the subject is omitted in one proposition but considered in the other. For instance, 'the moving thing is changing' can be true when considered during motion, and 'the moving thing is not changing' can also be true when considered at rest. The author emphasizes that true contradiction only occurs when the affirmation and negation are applied to exactly the same subject with the same condition. The example of the person with pleurisy illustrates that coughing is not constant; thus both statements can be true in different conditions. This aligns with the Islamic juristic principle that rulings often depend on conditions—e.g., 'The traveler shortens prayer' and 'The traveler does not shorten prayer' can both be true depending on whether the condition of travel is present.

Fallacy from Homonymy of the Predicate
First cause of predicate fallacy: homonymy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a pair of contradictory propositions are both true due to the predicate being a homonym with different meanings.

Core Idea
Same predicate word, different meanings: both propositions can be true.

Sometimes two statements that appear contradictory can both be true because the predicate word has more than one meaning. This is a common source of sophistry in debates. The author gives three examples: homonymy, potentiality vs. actuality, and relativity. In this lesson, we focus on homonymy and its subtypes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate term in each statement
Look for the word that is affirmed or denied of the subject. In the example, the predicate is 'buyer'.
2
Check if the predicate word has multiple meanings
Determine if the same word can refer to different things. For 'buyer', it can mean a person in a sale or the planet Jupiter.
3
Determine which meaning is used in each statement
See if the affirmative and negative statements each use a different meaning. If so, both can be true simultaneously.
4
Verify the fallacy condition
They only truly contradict if the same meaning is affirmed in one and denied in the other. Otherwise, no contradiction exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يرجع إلى المحمول فبأن يكون المحمول في إحداهما غير المحمول في الأخرى ونظن اتحاده، وذلك أيضاً بأسباب:
الأول: أن يكون الاسم مشتركاً كما في قولنا: (المتولي لطرف القبول في البيع هو المشتري) (المتولي لطرف القبول في البيع ليس هو المشتري) فإنهما يصدقان إن أريد بالمشتري في الموجبة ما هو المقابل للبائع، وفي السالبة المشتري الذي هو الكوكب، ويكذبان إن عكس الأمر، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان ما أثبت هو نفس ما نفي، وما نفي هو نفس ما أثبت وفي معنى هذا ما اتحد اسمه واختلف معناه بالقوة والفعل كقولنا: الخمر في الدنّ مشكر) (الخمر في الدن ليست مسكرة) فإنهما يصدقان إن أريد أنها مسكرة بالقوة ولا مسكرة بالفعل، ويكذبان بالعكس، وعلى هذا النحو قولنا: (الصبي عارف بالأشكال الهندسية) (الصبي ليس عارفاً بالأشكال الهندسية، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان التوارد بالنفي والإثبات على أحد الطرفين - أعني القوة والفعل - وفي معنى هذا أن يكون الخبر مضافاً لم يتوارد السلب والإيجاب عليه من جهة ما هو مضاف إليه كقولنا : زيد) (أب) (زيد ليس باب فإنهما يصدقان إذا كان سلب الأبوة بالنسبة إلى من ليس بمضاف إليه، وإيجابها بالنسبة إلى المضايف، ويكذبان بالعكس، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو توارد السلب والإيجاب على أحد الاعتبارين.
الثاني: اختلاف شرط المحمول كقولنا: (الحيوان يمشي) (الحيوان ليس يمشي) فإنه يصدق في الإيجاب ما دام ماشياً، وفي السلب لا في حالة كونه ماشياً، ويكذبان بالعكس، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو قيل الحيوان) ماش ما دام ماشياً) (الحيوان ليس بماش ما دام ماشياً، وفي معنى هذا أن يكون المحمول في نسبته إلى الموضوع مشروطاً بوقت إما معين أو غير معين، ولم يقع التوارد بالإيجاب والسلب عليه، أما المعيّن منه كما في قولنا : الشمس منضجة للثمار وذلك لها ليس إلا في وقت مخصوص، فإن نظر إليه كانت القضية صادقة الإيجاب كاذبة السلب، وإن قطع النظر عنه جاز صدقهما معاً بأن يكون الإيجاب في ذلك الوقت والسلب في غيره، وجاز كذبهما معاً بأن يعكس الحال في نفسه، وأما الوقت غير المعين فكما في قولنا الحيوان بتنفس الحيوان ليس يتنفس فإن التنفس للحيوان إنما هو في وقت غير معين كما عرف، ولا تتكاذب القضيتان إلا أن يكون حرف السلب نافياً في إحداهما ما أثبت في الأخرى، وإلا جاز اجتماعهما في الصدق أو الكذب، وفي معنى هذا أن تكون نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع مشروطة بمكان خاص، فمنهما قطع النظر عنه أمكن صدق القضيتين معاً وكذبهما معاً وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان حرف السلب نافياً في إحداهما ما أثبت في الأخرى، وذلك كما في قولنا: (شجرة البلسان يخرج منها صمغ هو دهنها) (شجرة البلسان لا يخرج منها صمغ هو دهنها)
As for what relates to the predicate, it is that the predicate in one of them is not the predicate in the other, but we think they are the same. That also has causes: First: That the name is homonymous, as in our statement: 'The one who undertakes the side of acceptance in a sale is the buyer' and 'The one who undertakes the side of acceptance in a sale is not the buyer.' For both can be true if by 'buyer' in the affirmative is meant the one corresponding to the seller, and in the negative is meant the planet Jupiter. Both can be false if the situation is reversed. They only truly contradict if what is affirmed is exactly what is denied and vice versa. Related to this is when the name is the same but its meaning differs in potentiality and actuality, as in our statement: 'The wine in the skin is intoxicating' and 'The wine in the skin is not intoxicating.' For both can be true if it is meant that it is intoxicating potentially but not actually, and both false if reversed. Similarly, our statement: 'The boy knows geometric shapes' and 'The boy does not know geometric shapes.' They only truly contradict if the affirmation and negation apply to one of the two modes—namely potentiality and actuality. Related to this is when the predicate is relative, and the negation and affirmation do not apply to it from the aspect of its relation, as in our statement: 'Zayd is a father' and 'Zayd is not a father.' Both can be true if the negation of fatherhood is with respect to someone not related to him, and the affirmation is with respect to someone related to him. They can both be false if reversed. They only truly contradict if the negation and affirmation apply to one of the two considerations. Second: Difference in the condition of the predicate, as in our statement: 'The animal walks' and 'The animal does not walk.' For the affirmative is true as long as it is walking, and the negative is true not in the state of walking. Both can be false if reversed. They only truly contradict if it is said: 'The animal, as long as it is walking, is walking' and 'The animal, as long as it is walking, is not walking.' Related to this is when the predicate in its relation to the subject is conditioned by a time, either determinate or indeterminate, and the affirmation and negation do not apply to it. As for the determinate time, like our statement: 'The sun ripens fruits'—that is only at a specific time. If that time is considered, the affirmative proposition is true and the negative false. If the time is disregarded, it is possible for both to be true together, that the affirmation is at that time and the negation at another time, and possible for both to be false together if the situation is reversed. As for the indeterminate time, like our statement: 'The animal breathes' and 'The animal does not breathe.' For breathing for the animal is only at an indeterminate time, as known. The two propositions do not truly contradict unless the negative particle negates in one what was affirmed in the other. Otherwise, it is possible for them to agree in truth or falsehood. Related to this is when the relation of the predicate to the subject is conditioned by a specific place. If that place is disregarded, it is possible for both propositions to be true together or false together. They only truly contradict if the negative particle negates in one what was affirmed in the other. That is like our statement: 'The balsam tree produces a gum that is its oil' and 'The balsam tree does not produce a gum that is its oil.'
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage discusses two main causes of fallacy related to the predicate term, with the first cause having two sub-types. The first cause is homonymy of the predicate (ishtirak al-ism). The author gives an example where 'buyer' means both the person in a sale and the planet Jupiter. Similarly, the predicates 'intoxicating' and 'knowing' can differ in potentiality vs. actuality, so a boy may potentially know geometric shapes but not actually. Another sub-type is when the predicate is relative, like 'father'—Zayd is a father relative to his son but not a father relative to someone else. The second cause is a difference in the condition of the predicate (ikhtilaf shart al-mahmul). This can be a temporal condition (determinate like sun ripening fruits only in summer, or indeterminate like breathing) or a spatial condition (like the balsam tree producing gum only in a specific place). In each case, the author shows that the affirmative and negative can both be true if the condition is ignored, and they only truly contradict when the condition is included in both statements. The commentary follows the author's order, explaining each sub-cause with its example. In Islamic sciences, similar considerations apply: e.g., 'The prayer is valid' and 'The prayer is not valid' can both be true if 'valid' means legally valid in one and spiritually accepted in another, or if conditions like time or place differ.

Key Terms
اشتراك الاسم
المحمول
القوة والفعل
المضاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which cause of fallacy is illustrated by: 'The wine in the skin is intoxicating' and 'The wine in the skin is not intoxicating' if the first refers to potential intoxication and the second to actual intoxication?
A) Homonymy
B) Difference in potentiality and actuality
C) Relative predicate
D) Condition of the predicate

Key Takeaway
When two statements seem contradictory, check if the predicate term has multiple meanings; if so, both may be true without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the following pair: 'The fasting person who eats forgetfully breaks the fast' and 'The fasting person who eats forgetfully does not break the fast.' Determine if both can be true due to homonymy of 'break the fast' (invalidates vs. requires no expiation).
?
Find an example from the Qur'an where a word has multiple meanings and could cause a logical fallacy if taken out of context.

Fallacy from Condition of the Predicate
Second cause: condition of the predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when two contrary statements can both be true because the predicate is conditioned by time (determinate or indeterminate) or place.

Core Idea
The predicate's truth depends on time or place; ignoring the condition yields false contradictions.

Sometimes a predicate is true only under certain conditions, such as a specific time or place. Two statements that appear contradictory can both be true if each refers to a different condition. The author explains this second major cause of fallacy, giving examples of determinate time (sun ripening fruits), indeterminate time (breathing), and specific place (balsam tree gum).

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate and any implied condition
Look for whether the truth of the predicate depends on a time or place. In 'The animal walks', 'walks' is true only when the animal is actually walking.
2
Determine if the condition is explicitly stated or omitted
If the condition is not included in the statement, the statement can be true or false depending on when/where it is evaluated.
3
Check if both statements can be true under different conditions
For 'The sun ripens fruits', if we consider summer, it's true; if winter, it's false. So both 'ripens' and 'does not ripen' can be true if the time reference differs.
4
Verify the condition for genuine contradiction
True contradiction occurs only if the same condition is affirmed and denied. Otherwise, both propositions can be true (or both false) without logical conflict.

Source Text

الأول: أن يكون الاسم مشتركاً كما في قولنا: (المتولي لطرف القبول في البيع هو المشتري) (المتولي لطرف القبول في البيع ليس هو المشتري) فإنهما يصدقان إن أريد بالمشتري في الموجبة ما هو المقابل للبائع، وفي السالبة المشتري الذي هو الكوكب، ويكذبان إن عكس الأمر، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان ما أثبت هو نفس ما نفي، وما نفي هو نفس ما أثبت وفي معنى هذا ما اتحد اسمه واختلف معناه بالقوة والفعل كقولنا: الخمر في الدنّ مشكر) (الخمر في الدن ليست مسكرة) فإنهما يصدقان إن أريد أنها مسكرة بالقوة ولا مسكرة بالفعل، ويكذبان بالعكس، وعلى هذا النحو قولنا: (الصبي عارف بالأشكال الهندسية) (الصبي ليس عارفاً بالأشكال الهندسية، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان التوارد بالنفي والإثبات على أحد الطرفين - أعني القوة والفعل - وفي معنى هذا أن يكون الخبر مضافاً لم يتوارد السلب والإيجاب عليه من جهة ما هو مضاف إليه كقولنا : زيد) (أب) (زيد ليس باب فإنهما يصدقان إذا كان سلب الأبوة بالنسبة إلى من ليس بمضاف إليه، وإيجابها بالنسبة إلى المضايف، ويكذبان بالعكس، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو توارد السلب والإيجاب على أحد الاعتبارين.

Fallacy from Misplaced Negative Particle
Author explains fallacy when negative particle is placed after a modal qualifier instead of before it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of placing the negative particle after a modal qualifier and correct it to achieve proper contradiction in modal propositions.

Core Idea
The negative particle must negate the modality, not just the predicate.

In logic, contradiction requires that two propositions cannot be simultaneously true or false. When a modal qualifier like 'necessarily' or 'obligatory' is added, placing the negative particle after that qualifier creates a fallacy: it only negates the predicate, leaving the modality affirmed. This lesson examines this fallacy and shows how to avoid it in Islamic reasoning, such as in usul al-fiqh or kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal qualifier
Locate the modal term (jiha) such as 'necessarily', 'possibly', 'obligatory' that qualifies the predicate.
The modality sets the force of the proposition.
2
Locate the negative particle
Find the negative particle (harf al-salb) like 'not' (laysa). Note if it comes before or after the modality.
3
Assess the scope of negation
Determine whether the negative particle negates only the predicate or the entire proposition including the modality.
If after the modality, it only negates the predicate.
4
Test for contradiction
Check if the affirmative and negative propositions can both be false. If they can, they are not contradictories.
Example: 'Zayd is necessarily a physician' and 'Zayd is necessarily not a physician' can both be false if Zayd is not necessarily either.
5
Correct the placement
For proper contradiction, move the negative particle before the modality, e.g., 'Not (Zayd is necessarily a physician)' meaning 'It is not necessary that Zayd is a physician'.
This negates the necessity itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما يرجع إلى حرف السلب فبأن يكون قد زيد على نفس الخبر جهة له، وحرف السلب لم يدخل عليها، بل ذكر بعدها وقبل المحمول، أو أنه زيد عليه بذكر صفة له أو صفات وحرف السلب لم يدخل إلا عليها أو على بعضها، والأول كما في قولنا في الموجبة زيد) هو بالضرورة طبيب وفي السالبة (زيد بالضرورة ليس طبيباً بتأخير حرف السلب عن الجهة وتقديمه على المحمول، فإنهما لا يتكاذبان
As for what relates to the negative particle, it is that a modal qualifier has been added to the predicate itself, and the negative particle does not enter upon it, but rather comes after it and before the predicate, or that the predicate has been increased by mentioning an attribute or attributes, and the negative particle only enters upon them or some of them. The first is like our statement in the affirmative: 'Zayd is necessarily a physician,' and in the negative: 'Zayd is necessarily not a physician'—by postponing the negative particle after the modality and placing it before the predicate. For these two are not contradictory.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage identifies a fallacy related to the grammatical scope of the negative particle (harf al-salb). When a modal qualifier (jiha) such as 'necessarily' is added to the predicate, and the negative particle is placed after that qualifier but before the predicate, the resulting propositions are not true contradictories. For example, 'Zayd is necessarily a physician' and 'Zayd is necessarily not a physician' do not contradict each other because the negative particle only negates the predicate while the modality ('necessarily') remains affirmed. Thus, it is possible that Zayd is necessarily both a physician and not a physician? Actually, they can both be false if Zayd is not necessarily either, but the point is they are not contradictory. The author also mentions a second scenario (which is cut off) where the predicate has additional attributes and the negative only negates some of them. The lesson shows that for true contradiction, the negative particle must negate the entire proposition including the modality. In Islamic legal theory, similar issues arise when negating conditional or modal statements, e.g., 'The traveler must shorten prayer' and 'The traveler must not shorten prayer'—if 'must' is a modal of obligation, the negation must apply to the obligation itself, not just the action.

Key Terms
حرف السلب
جهة
الخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why are 'Zayd is necessarily a physician' and 'Zayd is necessarily not a physician' not true contradictories?
A) The subject is different
B) The negative particle only negates the predicate while the modality remains affirmed
C) The predicate is homonymous
D) They are actually contradictory

Key Takeaway
For proper contradiction in modal propositions, the negative particle must precede the modal qualifier to negate the entire modal statement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Conditions for Truth of Affirmative and Negative Propositions
Author discusses two types of variation affecting contradictory pairs: relation and predicate condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether two propositions are truly contradictory by checking subject, predicate, time, place, and relation conditions.

Core Idea
True contradiction requires exact match—no variation in time, place, or relation.

This passage from Daqāʾiq al-Ḥaqāʾiq explains a crucial principle: an affirmative and negative may both be true or both false if they differ in condition. We will explore two such variations and learn how to ensure we are dealing with genuine contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two propositions
Take a pair of affirmative and negative statements, e.g., 'the animal walks' and 'the animal does not walk'. Recognize that they are candidates for contradiction.
2
Check for variation in relation
If the negation is about a different relation than the affirmation (e.g., paternity relative to non-relative vs. relative), they may both be true. Verify whether the relation is the same.
The example about paternity illustrates this.
3
Check for variation in predicate condition
Determine if the predicate is conditioned by time or place. If the time or place is different for the affirmative and negative, they can both be true or both false. Only when the conditions match exactly do they contradict.
The passage gives examples: sun ripening fruit (definite time), animal breathing (indefinite time), balsam tree (place).
4
Apply the rule
For a pair to be true contradictions, they must share the same subject, same predicate, same relation, same time, and same place. If any of these differs, the logical ruling changes.
This is essential for precise argumentation in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الأول: في القضايا الحملية يصدقان إذا كان سلب الأبوة بالنسبة إلى من ليس بمضاف إليه، وإيجابها بالنسبة إلى المضايف، ويكذبان بالعكس، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو توارد السلب والإيجاب على أحد الاعتبارين. الثاني: اختلاف شرط المحمول كقولنا: (الحيوان يمشي) (الحيوان ليس يمشي) فإنه يصدق في الإيجاب ما دام ماشياً، وفي السلب لا في حالة كونه ماشياً، ويكذبان بالعكس، وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو قيل الحيوان) ماش ما دام ماشياً) (الحيوان ليس بماش ما دام ماشياً، وفي معنى هذا أن يكون المحمول في نسبته إلى الموضوع مشروطاً بوقت إما معين أو غير معين، ولم يقع التوارد بالإيجاب والسلب عليه، أما المعيّن منه كما في قولنا : الشمس منضجة للثمار وذلك لها ليس إلا في وقت مخصوص، فإن نظر إليه كانت القضية صادقة الإيجاب كاذبة السلب، وإن قطع النظر عنه جاز صدقهما معاً بأن يكون الإيجاب في ذلك الوقت والسلب في غيره، وجاز كذبهما معاً بأن يعكس الحال في نفسه، وأما الوقت غير المعين فكما في قولنا الحيوان بتنفس الحيوان ليس يتنفس فإن التنفس للحيوان إنما هو في وقت غير معين كما عرف، ولا تتكاذب القضيتان إلا أن يكون حرف السلب نافياً في إحداهما ما أثبت في الأخرى، وإلا جاز اجتماعهما في الصدق أو الكذب، وفي معنى هذا أن تكون نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع مشروطة بمكان خاص، فمنهما قطع النظر عنه أمكن صدق القضيتين معاً وكذبهما معاً وإنما يتكاذبان أن لو كان حرف السلب نافياً في إحداهما ما أثبت في الأخرى، وذلك كما في قولنا: (شجرة البلسان يخرج منها صمغ هو دهنها) (شجرة البلسان لا يخرج منها صمغ هو دهنها
The first art: On categorical propositions. They are true if the negation of paternity is in relation to one who is not a relative, and its affirmation is in relation to the relative, and they are false by the opposite. They are only contradictory if the negation and affirmation apply to one of the two considerations. The second: difference in condition of the predicate, like our saying: 'the animal walks' and 'the animal does not walk'. For the affirmative is true as long as it is walking, and the negative when it is not walking, and they are false by the opposite. They are only contradictory if it is said: 'the animal is walking as long as it is walking' and 'the animal is not walking as long as it is walking'. The meaning of this is that the predicate in its relation to the subject is conditioned by a time, either definite or indefinite, and the affirmation and negation do not apply to it together. As for the definite time, like our saying: 'the sun ripens fruits', which it does only at a specific time. If that time is considered, the affirmative proposition is true and the negative false; if it is disregarded, it is possible for both to be true together—the affirmative at that time and the negative at another—and possible for both to be false together by reversing the situation. As for the indefinite time, like our saying: 'the animal breathes' and 'the animal does not breathe', for respiration in the animal occurs at an indefinite time as known. The two propositions are not contradictory unless the negation particle negates in one what is affirmed in the other; otherwise it is possible for them to agree in truth or falsehood. Similarly, if the relation of the predicate to the subject is conditioned by a specific place, then if it is disregarded, both propositions can be true together or false together. They are only contradictory if the negation particle negates in one what is affirmed in the other, as in our saying: 'the balsam tree produces a gum that is its oil' and 'the balsam tree does not produce a gum that is its oil'.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines two types of variation that prevent an affirmative and negative pair from being true contradictions. The author first introduces the art of categorical propositions and states that an affirmative and negative can both be true if the negation is about a different aspect (e.g., paternity relative to non-relative vs. relative). The second type involves the predicate being conditioned by time or place. For example, 'the animal walks' is true only when it is walking; at other times it is false. If the condition is made explicit, the pair become contradictory, but if the condition is left unspecified, both can be true at different times or false at the same time. The example of the sun ripening fruit at a specific time illustrates definite time, while animal breathing illustrates indefinite time. Place is analogous: the balsam tree produces gum only at a specific place; if that place is considered, one proposition is true and the other false, but if disregarded, both can be true or false. The key principle is that true contradiction only occurs when the negation directly opposes the exact same affirmation—the same subject, predicate, time, place, and relation.

Key Terms
القضايا الحملية
اختلاف شرط المحمول
حرف السلب

Checkpoint
Consider the propositions: 'The sun ripens fruit' and 'The sun does not ripen fruit'. Under what condition can both be false?
A) When the sun is not shining.
B) When considering a different time for each.
C) When the time is disregarded and the sun is at a stage where it neither ripens nor fails to ripen.
D) When the negation enters on the predicate only.

Key Takeaway
An affirmative and negative are truly contradictory only when they match exactly in subject, predicate, relation, time, and place; otherwise they may both be true or both false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the statements: 'Zakat is obligatory' and 'Zakat is not obligatory'. Under what condition could both be true? (Hint: think about time or wealth threshold).
?
Analyze the pair: 'The sun rises in the east' and 'The sun does not rise in the east'. Are these truly contradictory? Why or why not according to the passage?
